# The Wrong Mouth

# Chapter 1

The farmer had been dead two days by the time they sent for Maren, and the house smelled like it.

The body wasn't the problem. Orla Garsen had packed her husband in salt and dried lavender, following her mother's method, and Haff Garsen lay on the kitchen table looking more like cured meat than the farmer who'd collapsed in his own barley field. The smell was the other thing. The sweet-ozone reek that crept in through the walls after someone died near dungeon territory, the contamination smell that meant residue was pooling somewhere close, settling into the dirt and the well water and the grain stores. Maren had smelled it in a dozen farmhouses across the Fenhold flats. It was his cue to arrive, look grave, and collect his fee.

He set the ceramic bowl on the table beside Haff's left hand, close to the body but not touching, near enough that the family could see it mattered. The bowl was eleven years old, purchased from a market stall in Fenhold for four coppers when Maren was seventeen and had just decided that pretending to eat sins was easier than actually working. It had no System properties. It had no properties at all, besides being watertight and small enough to carry in a satchel without clinking against the candle holder. He'd chipped the rim twice and glued it back both times with pine resin, not because the bowl mattered but because the family needed to see a sin-eater who cared for his tools. The glue had darkened with age and looked, if you squinted, almost like a deliberate marking.

"Orla," he said. "I'm going to need quiet for a bit."

She nodded. She was standing in the doorway with her arms crossed over her chest, not from coldness but because her elbows were clamped so tight they pinned her hands in place. Her son Tam Garsen, maybe fourteen, with his father's heavy jaw and his mother's set mouth, stood behind her trying to look like he was not watching. He was watching.

Maren lit the candle. It was beeswax, not tallow. He'd learned early that the wax mattered to people. Tallow smelled like the rendering they did every autumn, and nobody wanted rendering-smell mixed with their father's last rites. The beeswax cost him two coppers out of his twelve-copper fee, but the investment paid for itself in trust. The flame held steady in the still air, throwing the room into gold and harder shadow, making Haff's salt-packed face look almost dignified.

The hum was there, as it always was. It was low and constant, like something large and electrical sitting just past the edge of hearing. Maren had heard it his whole life. He'd asked Derry once what the hum sounded like to someone who could see the System, and Derry had said, "What hum?" That told Maren everything he needed to know about being the only person in the world who noticed the background noise.

He placed his hands on the rim of the bowl. He pressed down until his knuckles whitened. This was the first posture, supplication, the sin-eater opening himself to receive. He'd copied it from a woodcut in a book he'd stolen from a traveling merchant when he was sixteen, and he still was not sure whether the woodcut was accurate or artistic license. It did not matter. Orla and Tam had never seen a real sin-eater work. Neither had anyone else in Fenhold.

Maren closed his eyes and began the murmuring. The sound was low and rhythmic, a not-quite-language that sounded devotional if you did not listen too closely. He'd invented it. The first few years he'd been terrified someone would recognize that the words were nonsense, fragments of a lullaby his mother had sung, mixed with numbers and the names of rivers he'd crossed as a boy. But nobody had ever stopped him. Nobody had ever said, *that's not how the ritual goes.* Because nobody in Fenhold knew how the ritual went. Sin-eating was like plumbing: everyone agreed it needed doing, nobody wanted to watch it happen, and the only qualification the job really required was showing up.

He murmured. He pressed his hands against the bowl. He let his face do the work, the slight downward pull of the mouth, the furrowed brow, the quality of concentration that told Orla and her son something difficult and holy was happening in the space between the bowl and the body.

He watched them through his lashes. This was the part that mattered.

Orla's shoulders dropped an inch. Her breathing slowed. Tam shifted his weight from his left foot to his right, relaxing into the performance instead of bracing against it. Maren catalogued every small surrender on instinct, with professional interest, noting where the room loosened and where it still held. In another year, Tam would be the man of this house. He'd be the one to send for the sin-eater when a neighbor dropped. If Tam believed today, he'd believe then, and that was another twelve coppers every time death came to the Garsen family's corner of the flats.

The contamination smell was stronger near the body. It was sweet and sharp, like overripe fruit left in the sun with a metallic undertone that clung to the tongue. Maren had been tasting it for eleven years, in every farmhouse and field shed and dirt-floor chapel where he'd knelt beside the dead and pretended to eat what killed them. The taste never changed. Nothing ever happened when he performed the ritual. He'd stopped expecting it to. He did the motions, got nothing, and came back the next time someone died because the work was still there waiting for him. Today the smell had an extra edge. It wasn't stronger, exactly. It had gathered itself tight. The room had spent two days holding its breath and now, the instant he raised his hands, it let everything out through him.

He shifted to the second posture, reception. He lifted his hands from the bowl, palms up, fingers spread. The stolen book had shown the sin-eater's hands in this position with lines of light flowing into them, which Maren had always assumed was embellishment. Quest-light pooled outside dungeon mouths like pale standing water, and sometimes a faint shimmer hung in the air after someone died, a residue like heat-haze with color, but it had always stayed where it was. This time the shimmer over Haff's body tightened. Even with his useless eyes he could mark it, pale grease in the air, drawing itself thin toward the space between his hands. The hum rose out of the floorboards and into his teeth. The bowl buzzed under his fingertips.

He held the posture. He counted to forty in his head. Forty was the number he'd settled on years ago, long enough to look effortful, short enough that the family did not start fidgeting. At thirty-two, he usually began the closing murmur, the rise in pitch that signaled the ritual was ending, that the sins had been eaten and the contamination cleared and the dead could rest. By twelve the room had narrowed to the bowl, the corpse, and the pressure building between them. He should have broken posture and stepped back. Instead he made the one mistake he'd never made in eleven years: for half a second he stopped selling the ritual and actually reached into it, not out of faith but out of the stupid reflex to steady himself against whatever had just leaned toward him.

He got to fourteen.

Something reached back.

It did not come from the body or from outside the farmhouse. It came from a place Maren did not have a word for, behind his sternum, or underneath his thoughts, or in the space where the hum had always lived. A pressure, sudden and enormous, like a fist closing around something he had not known was there. His hands locked. His fingers went rigid, still spread in the reception posture but no longer under his control. The air in his lungs stopped moving.

Then the paladin hit him.

Nothing hit him from outside. There was no impact, no blow. It was an implosion. Everything Aldric had been, fourteen years of combat drills, the specific way to shift weight before a sword stroke, the prayers he'd said at dawn for a decade, the face of a woman he'd loved and never told, the smell of stone corridors in the Order's chapter house, the grief and the grief and the grief of a man who had died serving something he believed in, all of it crashed into Maren's skull like someone had kicked open a door to a room that had always been sealed and now everything was falling on him at once.

His spine arched. His hands snapped closed into fists he had not made. Something in his shoulders knew how to throw a punch and was trying to, right there at the kitchen table, Haff Garsen's body three inches from his elbow. His right arm cocked back. His weight dropped to the balls of his feet in a fighting stance that belonged to a man he'd never met.

Orla said something. The words did not reach him.

His legs moved wrong, not his legs, someone else's muscle memory trying to inhabit the same limbs, a body that knew how to pivot and advance and drop its center of gravity for a shield-brace Maren had never learned and could not stop performing. His knee hit the table leg. The candle toppled. Beeswax spattered across Haff's salt-crusted shirt, and the thought that surfaced was absurd and entirely his: that cost me two coppers.

Then the voice came.

It came from inside him, not through his ears, not arriving from any direction. It occupied the same space as his own thoughts but was clearly, violently not his. The voice was formal and clipped, each word set down with the weight of recitation and command.

*Kneel.*

Maren's knees tried to buckle from obedience, not weakness. The impulse landed in his legs with the same ugly certainty his own mouth used when it found the right smile for a mark before he'd decided to wear it.

*Kneel and pray. Now. We are in the presence of the dead and you will show respect.*

He braced against the table. His fingernails scraped salt from the wood. Orla was backing toward the doorway, pulling Tam with her, and even through the chaos of his own muscles betraying him, Maren caught her face, not fear exactly, but the alarm of something that was supposed to be safe becoming unpredictable. She'd trusted him. Twelve coppers of trust, beeswax-scented and professional, and the sin-eater she'd hired was thrashing against her kitchen table with his eyes wet and his arms doing things arms should not do.

*Kneel. I will not ask again.*

"Get — off —" The words came out choked and wrong because his throat was doing something new, trying to shape words in a register too low for his vocal cords, the paladin's baritone fighting his tenor for the same mouth.

The memories kept coming. A sword pommel in cold rain. The weight of plate armor on shoulders that had carried it so long the body did not know how to stand without it. A young man, maybe twenty, taking his first oath in a chapel where quest-light pooled in stone fonts like colored water, and the oaths meant something, every word, and the man saying them believed with the specific ferocity of someone who had never yet been disappointed by the thing he served.

Maren's eyes were wet and he did not know whose grief it was.

He stumbled backward. His hip caught the chair. He went down, one hand on the floor, the bowl still on the table. He'd placed it with a con man's instinct for keeping props out of the violence, pushed back against the wall, away from the thrashing. His stomach finally revolted.

He made it outside. Barely. The farmhouse door was four steps from the table and he covered them in three, hand on the frame, the late-summer sun hitting his face like an accusation. The ditch beside the path was dry, weedy, humming with something small and persistent in the grass. He fell to his knees, not prayer, just gravity, and threw up.

It was not a dignified thing. It was loud and convulsive and it tasted like the contamination, ozone and rot, as though his body was trying to expel whatever had entered through the only method it understood. His hands pressed into the dirt. Small stones bit into his palms. His stomach clenched again and he spat bile into the dead grass, strings of it hanging from his lip while his back heaved and the muscles along his ribs spasmed in patterns that were not his, the aftershock of combat training forcing itself into a body that had never been drilled.

The hum was louder now, much louder, and beneath it the paladin's voice, still furious, still certain.

*This is a desecration. You opened the rite and then — the entire sequence was — I cannot determine what you did.*

"I'm throwing up," Maren said into the weeds. "In a ditch. Give me a minute."

*The incantation was gibberish. I could hear it through the residue — numbers, fragments, nothing I recognized. You held a genuine supplication over a concentrated field and sealed nothing into the posture. And the opening seated through all of that.*

"My job." He spat. The taste did not go away. "What are you?"

*I am Aldric of the Order of the Veil. Knight-paladin of the Fourth Seal. I appear to be — I am dead. And I appear to be inside — what is your classification?*

"My what?"

*Your System classification. Your class. Your — you are not a sin-eater. The rite was wrong from the first word but the opening was real. How?*

Maren pressed his forehead into the dirt. It was warm from the sun and gritty against his skin. Behind him, through the farmhouse door, Orla was talking fast and low to Tam. He could hear the quick planning in it, whether to run from him or drop the bar.

"I'm a fraud," he said, because it was easier than lying right now and because the truth had a kind of weight to it when you were on your knees in a ditch with bile on your chin. "I've been faking this for eleven years. Nothing ever — you shouldn't be here."

Aldric did not answer at once. The hum chewed at the back of Maren's teeth. Orla's voice kept threading through the farmhouse door, too fast to make out. When the paladin spoke again, he started from a different place.

*Fraudulent rites do not admit contact,* Aldric said at last. *That corpse — the residue had coherence, but that alone — you made an opening. Somehow.*

"I lifted my hands because that's what the picture in the book did."

*At some point you stopped pretending and actually reached.*

Maren remembered the hum in his teeth. The pressure in the room. That stupid half-second of leaning because something had leaned first.

"I leaned," he said.

*Yes.*

"That counts?"

*Improperly. Briefly. Apparently enough. I took the contact and then — I should have dissolved. I did not.*

Maren lifted his head an inch. "And every other village?"

*If you were faking the opening, the contamination just stayed where it was. This time you gave it a path.*

He shut his eyes again.

*You have been staging this over villages for eleven years,* Aldric said.

"Yes."

*Then every place you touched is still carrying what you left there.*

"I know."

The next words came rougher, some of the parade-ground snap gone out of them.

*What kind of person does this?*

Maren spat again. His stomach was done but his hands were shaking, a tremor that started in his wrists and ran up to his elbows, and he still could not tell whether it was the vomiting or the paladin's outrage or his own muscles trying to sort out which movement patterns actually belonged to him.

"The kind who's in a ditch," he said.

The farmhouse door opened a handspan.

Orla Garsen stood there with the bowl in both hands, arms locked straight as if she were returning a sprung trap to the woods. Tam hovered behind her shoulder, pale around the mouth. Maren pushed himself upright on one knee and wiped his mouth with the back of his wrist.

"Looks bad," he said. "It's fine. Took more out of me than usual, that's all."

Neither of them moved.

"Spiritual exertion," he added, because the lie arrived on old rails. "Ugly, but normal. Nothing for you to worry over."

Orla came close enough to set the bowl on the packed path beside him and did nothing more.

"You forgot this," she said.

"Right."

He picked it up. The ceramic was warm from the house.

"Keep the kitchen shut till sunset," he said. "Then do what you need to do."

Orla gave one short nod that did not mean belief. She drew Tam back toward the door. They watched him from the farmhouse doorway while he got his feet under him and tucked the bowl beneath his arm. Then the door shut. A second later the bar slid into place.

Fair enough.

***

Fenhold was a long enough walk that a man could almost empty himself on it. Maren tried. By the time the Garsen farmhouse had dropped behind the barley and the path widened into a rutted road, his stomach had stopped turning over, but his hands still shook around the bowl.

Late-summer light had gone copper at the edges. Stubble scratched at his boots where the path narrowed. Every few steps some scrap of Aldric's posture tried to reassemble him from the spine outward, shoulders back, chin level, stride longer than his own. Maren kept having to ruin it on purpose.

*I am not reading the public registration Fenhold sees,* Aldric said. *I am reading the structure underneath it.*

"I was wondering when you'd start that."

*Underlying architecture: unregistered designation. Classification field present, unfilled. Level field present, unfilled. Skill allocations: none. Active quest involvement: none.*

"I told you. There's nothing there."

*Incorrect. There is a structure there. It is empty. That is different. It is also not the same thing as the listing villagers use when they send for you.*

Maren hitched the bowl higher against his ribs.

"So either I'm empty or I'm employed. Pick one."

*Both are presently true,* Aldric said. *The outer layer is a public service registry. It says there is a registered sin-eater here, active on the Fenhold flats. I can see the deeper architecture because I am inside the contact rather than looking at you from the road. The registry says what you are supposed to be. The interior says something else.*

"I'm System-blind," he said. "Been blind my whole life. I can't see the interface. Never have."

Silence.

*You have lived twenty-eight years without access to the interface.*

"That'd be the one."

*How have you been navigating anything?*

"I watch people. When something changes for them, they — eyes move. Shoulders jump. They stare at empty air and act like it told them what day it is. You learn to read that."

*And your partner?*

"Derry reads me the parts I need. Numbers. Names. Anything that'll keep me from sounding stupid."

*So you have been guessing. From their faces.*

"It paid for candles."

"Can anyone else see the empty part?"

*No one could without reading deeper than the public layer. Most people do not. Why would they, when the registry has already told them what job you are?*

The road dipped. The contamination smell thickened in the low ground and thinned again when he climbed out of it. The hum had not gone down. It sat under his skull now, steady and intimate.

*Passive contamination exposure: significant,* Aldric said. *There are three contamination-clear requests within two miles. Those sit on the public listing, not the hollow underneath it. The nearest is attached to the Garsen holding and assigned to a registered level-seven sin-eater operating the Fenhold flats.*

"Level seven."

*That is what the listing claims.*

Maren barked a laugh that scraped his throat. "That's awkward."

*Do not mistake me for amused.*

The road bent. The farmhouse dropped behind a stand of willow and came back again in broken pieces between the branches.

*Accumulation record across this district extends eleven years,* Aldric said. *Fourteen occupied farmsteads inside the current spread.*

Maren kept walking.

*Nearby deceased entity. Haff Garsen. Farmer. Level six. Cause of death: environmental exposure. Subcategory: contamination-related organ failure. Long-term.*

The road kept moving under him. That was rude of it. He had the feeling the whole world should have stopped for a second and let that sentence sit.

"No."

*Yes.*

"He was turning his own field last week."

*The interface attributes the failure to prolonged contamination exposure.*

Maren stopped hard enough that the bowl knocked against his ribs. The pines along the roadside went soft for a second and then came back into shape.

He pictured Haff on the table with salt in the seams of his shirt and lavender stuffed at his throat. Then Grella, eleven years earlier, and the bread her family had given him after he'd done the same lie over her cooling body. He started walking again because standing still with that in him was worse.

"There are more?" he asked.

*Fourteen farmsteads in the nearest spread. Estimated population sixty-eight. Approximately twenty children.*

He kept his eyes on the track. "Stop giving me the numbers."

*You need to hear them.*

"I don't need twenty children as a number right now."

Silence. Then, more quietly:

*No. Perhaps not.*

That changed something, small but real. It wasn't warmth or pardon. It was just the first clean fact about the man in his skull that was not accusation.

They walked in it for a while.

By the time the road lost the last of the willow shade, the bile taste had dried to a metal paste at the back of Maren's tongue. A narrow runoff channel crossed under the road through a cracked stone culvert, clear enough on the surface that he stepped toward it before he had finished thinking thirst.

*Stop.*

He was already halfway down the bank. "I'm not asking permission. I'm rinsing my mouth."

*Then do it upstream. Do not use the pooled section.*

"It looks clean."

*That is not the relevant measure.*

He crouched anyway. Up close the water smelled of mud and crushed green things, but under that sat the same sweet-metal taint that had soaked the Garsen kitchen, thin enough to miss until his face was near the surface. It was old pennies and fruit turning in heat. He jerked back before his fingers touched it.

*Three steps left,* Aldric said. *There is cleaner inflow where the bank splits. This section holds runoff from the Garsen drainage.*

"You're reading drainage now?"

*I am reading residue. Move.*

Nettles dragged at his trouser legs as he shoved farther up the bank. The trickle there was thinner, slipping over broken stone in a thread no wider than twine. Aldric kept correcting him with clipped irritation: not that hollow, the next one, higher. Maren used the bowl because it was there and because holding his hands under the water would have shown too clearly how badly they shook. The ceramic filled with water the color of weak tea.

He held it under his nose. It smelled like cold stone, wet leaves, and dirt. There was no ozone bite. He drank. The first swallow cramped his empty stomach hard enough that he had to brace one fist against his thigh, but it stayed down. The second one washed the bile off his tongue. He stood there with ditch water on his mouth and the bowl cold against his palm, trying to account for how many times he had drunk from whatever was nearest and called it fine because he had never known what fine was.

When Aldric spoke again, the cadence had tightened in a different direction, less outraged now, more like a field report filed by someone who had accepted that the damage was already done and would need counting anyway.

*One practical detail. From inside this arrangement I can perceive the residue itself, not merely the interface notation. I can tell where it pools and where it thins. Looking through you changes the read.*

"What does that do for me?"

*It keeps you from poisoning yourself by habit,* Aldric said. *If you insist on walking into contaminated ground again, I can tell you where it is worst. I can also tell you when a well is spoiled before you drink from it.*

Maren looked back at the broader pooled water below the culvert, sunlight laid over it like clean glass, and thought about all the times he had knelt in mud and ponded runoff because it was there and he was thirsty.

"Useful," he said.

*Useful, if you plan on surviving.*

He had not made a plan that far.

The road rose. Fenhold's outer fields showed themselves in strips through the trees, dark earth, late barley, a scarecrow listing sideways in the evening wind.

"What am I to the System, then?" he asked after a while. "Practically."

*At present your underlying profile is a structure with the entries missing,* Aldric said. *The public registry hanging over it is something else. I have seen damaged interfaces, cursed interfaces, corrupted records. I have not seen this.*

"That sounds bad."

*It sounds unfinished,* Aldric said. *I am not accustomed to profiles I cannot evaluate.*

Maren almost laughed at that, but the laugh would have come out too close to a choke.

"I'm Maren," he said instead. "Since this seems to be sticking."

A beat passed.

*Aldric.*

"I got that part."

*I am in your head. Stop being polite.*

"I'm not going to pray," Maren said.

*I had gathered.*

"Good."

They walked another stretch in the smell of cut barley and old contamination. Somewhere out in the darkening field a nightbird started up.

*Tomorrow,* Aldric said, *you will give me the villages. Every one. I need to know the spread.*

Maren shifted the bowl under his arm.

"Tomorrow I'm getting water and finding Derry and trying not to throw up again."

*Then the villages,* Aldric said.

By the time the first Fenhold roofs showed through the dusk, Aldric had gone quiet again. He wasn't gone. He was just holding himself back, as if speech cost more now.

Maren crossed the village with his head down. A woman carrying wash took one look at his shirtfront and the bowl under his arm and stepped aside without speaking. He let her.

His room over the shed smelled like dust, old straw, and the damp wool of the blanket he'd never once managed to air out properly. He set the bowl on the floor by the bed, found the water crock by touch, drank until his stomach threatened mutiny, and dropped onto the mattress without taking off his boots.

In the dark behind his eyes, Aldric spoke once more.

*Tomorrow.*

Maren put an arm over his face. "Yeah."

The hum followed him down. It always had.


# Chapter 2

Maren woke to a voice cataloguing the structural deficiencies of his ceiling.

*The primary beam has a lateral crack approximately two hand-spans in length. I would estimate six months before failure under current load. The thatch needs replacement along the southern face. There is daylight visible through the weave, which suggests water ingress during rain. There is also a bird's nest above the door frame. Swallow, I believe, though my ornithological training was limited.*

"Shut up," Maren said to his ceiling.

*Additionally, you sleep with your mouth open. I did not need to know that about you.*

The morning light came through the gaps in the thatch in thin bars, full of dust. Late summer light, the kind that promised heat by midday and kept its promise. Maren lay on his straw mattress and tried to piece together the walk home. The ditch. His vomit in the ditch. The Garsen family watching from the farmhouse door while he staggered upright and wiped his mouth and told them everything was fine, just the spiritual exertion, very normal, nothing to worry about. The long walk back to Fenhold with his hands shaking and his ceramic bowl tucked under his arm and a dead man explaining, in the patient tone of a quartermaster discovering weevils in the flour, exactly how many years of System data Maren had been living without.

Twenty-eight years. His entire life. Every notification, every quest prompt, every threat marker and skill update and ambient data stream that the rest of the world swam through from birth like fish in water. All of it, gone. Not dimmed or degraded but absent, as if someone had built a house and forgotten to install the windows.

He sat up. His body ached in places that made no sense: shoulders, the base of his skull, the tendons along both forearms. It was the deep, dragged soreness of hours spent hauling stone, though the heaviest thing he'd carried yesterday was a bowl that weighed less than a loaf of bread.

*The muscular soreness is consistent with fragment integration. My combat training is embedding into your motor pathways. You will find you have reflexes you did not possess yesterday. I would recommend a period of controlled physical activity before you attempt anything more demanding.*

"I'm going to wash my face," Maren said. "You don't need to narrate that."

*I am not narrating. I am providing operational context. The distinction matters.*

"It's the same thing."

*It is not the same thing.*

Maren swung his legs off the mattress. The packed-dirt floor was cool under his bare feet. The clay walls held the night's coolness the way a cellar held preserves. His dwelling was one room. A sleeping pallet, a table he'd built himself seven years ago that listed slightly to the left and had never stopped listing, a shelf where the bowl sat next to the candles and the dried herb bundles he used as props. Everything in this room was a stage set. The ritual ash in its jar was ordinary fireplace ash, scooped from Derry's hearth. The binding cord on its hook came from a rope vendor in the market and he'd soaked it in juniper water because it made the house smell like a practitioner's home. Eleven years of this. Eleven years of building a room that told a specific lie to anyone who stepped inside it.

He found the water bucket by the door. Still half full. He splashed his face, rubbed his eyes, pushed his wet fingers through his hair. The water smelled faintly of iron from the well.

*There is a System notification in your ambient field. Shall I read it?*

Maren stood there with water dripping from his jaw. Twenty-eight years of silence where the System should have been, and now there was a voice offering to fill it like a clerk reading appointments to a man who'd been asleep his whole life.

"No."

*It is a regional contamination advisory. Level two. The Garsen farmstead is flagged for residual quest activity. Given that you performed a sham ritual over the body yesterday, and the actual clearing was accomplished entirely by my arrival, that should concern you more than my reading it aloud.*

"I said no."

Water ran off Maren's jaw and tapped the bucket rim four times.

*Your skill tree is remarkable.*

Maren pulled on his shirt, the same one from yesterday, stiff with dried sweat along the collar. He owned two. The other was drying on the line outside and probably stiff with yesterday's dried sweat too. "If this is another lecture, don't."

*I am reading your System profile. Or rather, the part of it the System is willing to show cleanly. You are registered as a sin-eater. Class designation: active. Level seven. Specialization: consumption. These are the public entries visible to any System user who looks at you. The deeper record beneath them does not resolve properly. No progression data. No consumption log. No fragment integration history. No capacity scores. The public entries are tidy enough. Beneath them, whole sections stop. Headers remain. The supporting structure is gone.*

Maren stopped with one arm through a sleeve. The light caught the dust floating between his body and the far wall, motes turning slow in the morning warmth.

"How do you know what my profile looks like to other people?"

*I can see both layers. The System presents a public face and maintains an architecture behind it. Most people see only the face. My order trained us to read deeper. Veil-sigil holders were trained in architectural reading. Interface diagnostics. Your public profile says sin-eater. Under it, the record misaligns. Sections refuse to render. It does not resemble any healthy record I was taught to read.*

An image pushed into Maren's mind, not his own but from a place behind his own thoughts. A silver sigil on dark armor, the shape of an eye half-veiled. The weight of it against a chest that wasn't his. The cool metal through a linen undertunic, the way it shifted when you moved your sword arm. The pride and the responsibility and the specific, quiet satisfaction of wearing a symbol that meant you were trusted with things most people didn't know existed.

Maren shook it loose. The memory faded like heat off a stone, slow and residual.

*There is something else. Your consciousness does not interact with the System in the manner I would expect from documented System-blindness. The Order kept records of the condition. Rare, perhaps one in a hundred thousand births. In those cases, the profiles present as degraded. Partial rendering. Reduced functionality. Entries blur or fail when queried. Yours does not present as damaged. The public layer resolves cleanly enough, then the deeper pattern thins and shifts when examined. That combination does not match the records I studied.*

Then he stopped, as if he'd reached the edge of what he meant to say.

"That's awfully specific," Maren said.

*It is an observation.*

"From less than a day in my skull."

*The integration process is disorienting. My thoughts are not always well-ordered. I will rephrase: your profile presents an anomaly that my order would have considered significant. That is all.*

Maren dragged the shirt down over his ribs. Aldric left him with dust in the light and the creak of the shelf.

He pulled the shirt the rest of the way on and tucked it into his trousers. The bowl was on the shelf where he'd left it last night. Ordinary ceramic, brown-glazed, the rim rough in two places where he'd chipped it and glued it back with pine resin. He'd carried it for eleven years and it had never done anything except hold water and ash and the occasional handful of dried rosemary. He left it where it was.

He ate cold porridge from last night's pot, standing at his listing table. The porridge had set into something you could probably use to fill masonry cracks. He ate it anyway, scraping the pot with a wooden spoon, because food was food and he'd spent eleven years in a village that paid him just enough to eat poorly.

*Do you always eat standing up?*

"When the chair's got a broken leg, yeah."

*The chair has been broken for how long?*

"Couple of years."

*And yet you have not repaired it.*

"Because I don't know how to fix chairs. I fake sin-eating. Different skill set."

*Clearly.*

The morning outside was warm and bright and full of small village sounds: a rooster that didn't know when to stop, someone hammering something wooden, the distant complaint of livestock. Maren stood in his doorway and let the sun hit his face. It was the kind of morning where everything looked like it always had: the packed-dirt path to the square, the leaning fence around Tam's goat yard, the scorch marks on the stone wall of the grain store where a hero patrol had tested some ability months ago and nobody had cleaned it up because nobody ever cleaned those things up. They just accumulated, a record of careless power that the village stepped around the way you stepped around horse shit.

He started walking.

*Threat assessment for this area: negligible. No active hostiles within standard scan range. Ambient contamination levels: low overall, concentrated near the eastern woodline and the Garsen property. Regional hero activity: one patrol, ranger-class, moving northeast along the main road at approximately four miles' distance. They are not heading toward this village.*

"I didn't ask."

*You did not need to. Morning briefing was standard procedure in my order. Situational awareness for the operating environment. This was something I did every day.*

Maren let the rooster and the hammering fill the gap. He didn't push it.

Fenhold's square was just a widening of the main path where three tracks met: a well, a bench, old Poul's horse trough, and enough open dirt for a dozen people to stand around in without bumping elbows. It was already occupied by the low commerce of late summer. Grenna was shaking a rug over her fence, beating it with a wooden paddle in steady, punishing strokes. Poul was leading his carthorse toward the trough, the animal's hooves raising clouds of fine dust. Two children ran between the houses, changing direction at nothing, stopping at nothing, reaching for things Maren couldn't see.

He'd never been able to see it. Twenty-eight years. And he'd never stopped watching the people who could.

Grenna paused mid-swing to glance at something above the roofline. Her eyes tracked left, held, then returned to the rug. A notification. Something in the System's constant ambient chatter had caught her attention for half a second before she dismissed it. Poul's horse balked at the trough and Poul's gaze flicked to a spot about three feet to the left of the animal's head, a threat marker maybe, or a quest flag, though threat markers near a village water trough weren't usually worth the flinch. The children's path made no sense unless you understood they were following invisible waypoints, their trajectories determined by prompts only they could see.

Maren had learned this language the way a deaf man learns to read lips. Not the System itself, but the shadows it cast on other people's faces. The flinches and glances and micro-pauses. Where people looked when a notification appeared. How long they looked. Whether they smiled or frowned or dismissed it. He'd built a career on reading those shadows, matching his responses to what a blind man should do, should notice, should miss.

"Morning, Maren." Grenna's voice carried the particular half-pitch-up that people used with him. Warmer than normal, slightly louder. The voice you'd use with a child or someone recovering from an illness. "Watch the path by the bench, love. Poul's been spilling feed again."

There was no spilled feed. Maren knew this because Poul hadn't come from the feed store and because Grenna's eyes, when she said it, were focused on a point near the ground about two feet left of the bench, a System marker of some kind that her brain had translated automatically into a physical hazard she could warn a blind man about. She'd been doing this for years. The whole village had. A quiet, uncoordinated conspiracy of small translations. Every puddle that was really a quest residue. Every "broken step" that was really a scorch mark. Every "careful, the sun's in your eyes" that really meant a notification was flashing in the space between Maren and whoever he was talking to.

*She is lying to protect you,* Aldric said. *The marker is a minor resource node. Copper-grade. Barely worth collecting. She has translated it into a physical obstacle because she assumes you cannot process System information and she does not want you to appear confused in public.*

"I know," Maren muttered, quiet enough that Grenna wouldn't catch it. He smiled at her, stepped carefully around the nothing by the bench. "Thanks, Grenna."

*You are remarkably skilled at this.*

He kept walking. He wasn't sure whether that was meant as admiration or diagnosis.

*Both,* Aldric said.

"Stay out of my thoughts."

*I cannot. Your thoughts and my awareness share the same architecture. It is not partitioned. I hear what surfaces in your mind the way you hear what I say. It is not a choice I would make either, believe me.*

The walk to the well was a gauntlet of translations. Tam shouted from his goat yard about a "soft patch" in the road that was actually a low-grade contamination residue Maren could smell but couldn't see. It had that dry, faintly sour tang with a metal edge, the one he'd always assumed was normal, the background stink of living near a dungeon mouth. Hella waved him away from her doorstep with something about spilled wash-water, which was actually a scorch mark that had warped the packed earth into a shallow depression. Each interaction required Maren to play two roles simultaneously: the blind man who relied on their kindness, and the fraud who knew the kindness was based on a misunderstanding he'd cultivated for over a decade.

*Skill assessment,* Aldric said, unprompted. *I have been reviewing the details of your registered capabilities. Your listed skills include: Consumption, rank three. Contamination sensing, rank two. Ritual binding, rank one. Fragment dissolution, rank one. These are, I should note, the minimum ratings consistent with an active sin-eater designation. They appear to be automatically assigned defaults rather than earned progressions. You have gained no experience in any sin-eating skill in eleven years of ostensible practice.*

"Are you going to read my entire file to me?"

*I already have. That was the entire file. Four skills, minimum rank, no progression. Behind the entries, the architectural data remains as I described. Thin. Incomplete. Your profile has the depth of a door painted on plaster. It suggests access without offering it.*

Maren reached the well. He lowered the bucket, the hemp rope rough and familiar in his hands. The splash echoed up the shaft, cool and distant.

"Useful comparison," he said. "Thanks."

*The comparison is accurate, not useful. There is a difference, though I concede the distinction may not matter to you.*

He hauled the bucket up and drank from it, cold water against his dry mouth, the iron tang of the well on his tongue. Around him the village was doing what it always did: Grenna beating the rug, the children chasing invisible objectives, Poul's horse finally drinking from the trough now that whatever marker had spooked it had either been dismissed or moved. The hum was there too, as it always was, low and constant near the eastern woodline where the contamination ran densest. A sound Maren had lived with his whole life without understanding what it was. The System's background noise, the operating frequency of the infrastructure that organized everyone's existence except his.

He'd always heard it. He just hadn't known what he was hearing.

---

The Wollen house was on the west side of the village, set back from the path behind a vegetable garden that was doing better than the garden deserved. Maren had visited three months ago to clear the contamination after the old woman died in her bed. He'd performed his usual ritual with the bowl, the ash, and the murmured approximation of words that sounded appropriately solemn, and collected twelve coppers and left the contamination exactly where it was. This morning one of Britt's children had come to fetch him because the cat was back to crouching by the rear alcove and screaming at nothing, and because Maren had told Britt to send for him if the clearing ever felt unsettled before harvest. Follow-up visits made him sound thorough. Sometimes they earned him a few extra coppers.

The daughter, Britt, met him at the door. She was forty and had the raw-knuckled hands of someone who washed things for a living: laundry, dishes, floors, the body of her dead mother. She'd hired Maren because the contamination had started making the cat behave oddly and she'd heard, correctly, that residual quest energy could affect animals before it affected people.

"Maren." She smiled the smile of someone who wanted to believe good news. "Come in, come in. I just need to know if it's settled this time."

He stepped inside. The house was clean, sparse, smelled like soap and boiled cabbage and, underneath, the dry-sour taint of old contamination, settled into the place so long Britt probably registered it as weather.

*Residual contamination, grade three,* Aldric said. *Concentrated at the shrine in the rear alcove but with lateral spread into the sleeping area and the food storage. The occupants, I count four individuals registered at this address, have been sleeping in low-grade contamination for approximately ninety days. Prolonged exposure at this level correlates with fatigue, joint pain, mood disturbance, and worse effects in young children.*

"Everything looks clean," Maren told Britt. "The clearing took well. She's at rest."

Britt's face did something that Maren had seen a hundred times and had never learned to watch without a specific, localized shame in his chest. Relief. Not the small relief of a resolved inconvenience but the deep, structural relief of someone who had been frightened for her mother's soul and had just been told by a professional that the fear was over. Her eyes went wet. She blinked it back.

"Thank you," she said. "Really. Maren, thank you."

She pressed three coppers into his hand. He took them.

*The contamination in this house has been building for three months,* Aldric said. *It will continue to build. The cat she mentioned is likely already symptomatic. In six to eight months the youngest occupant, age four, will begin showing early signs of exposure. Fatigue. Night terrors. You are taking money from this woman for work you did not do, and her family will suffer the consequences of your failure in specific, measurable ways.*

Maren closed his fingers around the coppers. They were warm from Britt's hand.

"Thank you, Britt. You take care."

He walked home. The afternoon heat was building. The air was thick and still, the dust on the path warm through his boot soles. The drier, sourer stink of old contamination thickened near the Garsen edge of the village, with a sharper ozone edge drifting off the eastern woodline, and the hum was louder there too, a low vibration that sat in his teeth. He'd walked this path a thousand times and he'd always noticed the smell and the hum and he'd always let them slide past because they were just the way things were, the background texture of a world he couldn't fully perceive.

He sat on the bench outside his door and put the three coppers on the wood beside him. They sat there, small and warm and damning.

*I will refrain from further comment on that transaction,* Aldric said. *My opinion is, I believe, adequately established.*

"Yeah."

Maren worried a splinter loose from the bench. The wood had gone hot under his palm. A fly landed on his arm and scrubbed its legs together while the three coppers sat between them like a second set of eyes. Morning had been bickering. This was worse.

"How long were you with the Order?" he asked. Not because he wanted to know. Because if he kept staring at Britt's coppers he'd hear her thanking him again.

*Fourteen years. I was commissioned at twenty-one. Standard path: field postings, dungeon surveys, System-anomaly investigation. I reached senior investigator at twenty-eight.* The fly lifted from Maren's arm and settled on the bench slat. *I was thirty-five when I died.*

"How?"

*A dungeon collapsed. Survey mission. Routine. A minor anomaly in the sub-architecture, the kind of entry we catalogued by the dozen. The collapse was instantaneous. No structural warning. No System alert.*

"Aren't there supposed to be warnings?"

*The System monitors structural integrity in active dungeon environments. A collapse without advance alert is either a catastrophic sensor failure or—* The wall again. He hit it mid-thought and the thought stopped. *The investigation classified it as structural failure. The inquiry lasted two days.*

"Two days."

*Two days. For a senior investigator. My order submitted six requests for extended review. All denied. The case was filed and the file was sealed and my name was added to the memorial wall in the eastern chapel. That was the end of Aldric Venn.*

The full name landed in the space between them. Maren hadn't known it until now. Aldric Venn. A real name, the kind of name that had been spoken by real people in real rooms, shouted across training yards, murmured in evening prayers, written in reports and sealed in files. A name with weight.

"I'm sorry," Maren said. Then, because the sincerity startled him and he didn't know what to do with it: "That's shit. I mean — two days? That's really shit."

*Your capacity for eulogy is noted.*

"Did you have — was there anyone? Family, or—"

Maren bent the splinter until it snapped off in his thumbnail. The dog by the eastern woodline barked twice. Aldric didn't answer until the second bark died.

*I had my order,* Aldric said. The formality was still there, but it dragged in places now, threadbare at the elbows. *I had duties. I had a barracks room with a window that faced east, and every morning the light came through at an angle that struck the far wall at the hour of first prayers. I maintained my own sword because I did not trust anyone else with the edge. There was a woman in the commissary who remembered how I took my tea. Every morning. Without being asked. I never learned her name.*

Maren's hands stopped on the bench.

*I have thought about that more in the last day than seems reasonable. Her name. I did not think to ask it in fourteen years, and now I cannot stop. She had short hair. Brown. She poured the tea left-handed. That is what I have. That is everything I have of her. I had morning prayers. I had evening reports. I had the weight of my own body and the feeling of cold water on my face after training and the way bread tasted when you had earned it through physical work, and I did not appreciate any of it. I did not understand that these things could be taken, that everything could be—*

The last word snagged and vanished. Maren's own throat tightened around nothing. When Aldric tried again, the voice came back scraped raw, too stripped to hide behind sarcasm or protocol.

Maren sat on his bench in the late summer heat with three coins beside him and no idea what to say. The dead paladin wasn't a client. There was no angle here, no version of events that would make this better, no deflection smooth enough to redirect grief this specific.

"I don't know what to — I'm not good at this part," Maren said. His voice sounded wrong to him. Too plain. Not enough.

*That is the first honest thing you have said in my presence.*

"Yeah, well."

The fly had come back. It landed on one of the coppers and Maren watched it navigate the surface of the coin, its legs picking across the stamped face of whoever was important enough to be on a copper. The dog near the eastern woodline was barking again. It had been barking all day, probably at the contamination that sat in the trees like a smell the animal couldn't get away from.

*I am not asking for your sympathy,* Aldric said. The words came back squared off again, each one set down carefully. *I am stating facts. I had a life. It was taken. I am here now. And I cannot leave. I have tried. The architecture does not permit separation. I am embedded in your consciousness. The metaphor escapes me. I am here. That is the operational reality.*

"Okay."

*Okay is not a response to what I have just told you.*

"It's the only one I've got."

Maren turned one of Britt's coppers under his thumb. The stamped face had worn nearly smooth.

*There is a quest notification for the eastern farmsteads,* Aldric said eventually. His voice had resettled into its usual register, formal and clipped, the voice of a man filing a report to an order that no longer existed. *Harvest season. The contamination will need clearing before the grain comes in.*

"Yeah."

*You cannot do it. You do not know how.*

"I know."

*The people in this village have trusted you for eleven years. They are sleeping in contamination you were paid to remove. The Wollen family is already paying for it.*

"I know."

He didn't say it angrily. The words came out flat, worn thin from use before he'd even said them.

The fly stepped off the copper and lifted into the heat. Aldric didn't follow it with another word.

The sun had dropped low enough to warm Maren's boots through the leather. The three coppers sat on the bench between his hand and his thigh.

"Tomorrow," Maren said. "You tell me how to clear the eastern farms."

*You are agreeing to act?*

"I'm agreeing to listen. Don't make it bigger than it is."

Heat soaked through his boots before Aldric answered.

*Very well.*


# Chapter 3

Derry had brought eggs, two of them, brown-shelled and still warm from Marta's henhouse, and he'd been talking about leeks for what might have been five minutes. The whole west bed had gone to leeks this year. He didn't know why. Soil, maybe. Or Pell had been watering everything the same amount because she wasn't three yet and believed all plants were equally thirsty, and Lenna was too tired in the evenings to correct her, and anyway who needed that many leeks? You could only make so much soup.

Maren cracked one of the eggs into a cup and drank it raw. The yolk was warm and thick and sat heavy in his empty stomach.

*He has been talking for four minutes,* Aldric said. *I have been timing it. He has not paused for breath. The man is terrified.*

Maren knew. He'd known Derry for most of his adult life, and the leek monologue was a tell. The worse things got, the more Derry talked about his garden, his daughter, what the cow did yesterday. The content didn't matter. What mattered was the noise, the filling of space, the refusal to let silence in where fear could pool.

"So the farmer's family," Derry said, his tone shifting abruptly, the way you set down something light and pick up something heavy. "They're telling people you had a seizure during the ritual."

"I heard."

"They're not angry, if that helps. More concerned. Breda's been saying you might've been touched — she means it the religious way, not the —" He waggled his hand. "You know."

*Touched,* Aldric said. There was something in his tone that sat between contempt and recognition, like a word he knew being used in a way he hadn't expected.

Maren drank the other egg. It was cooler than the first, the yolk thinner. He wiped his mouth with the back of his hand.

"I'm handling it," he said.

"You're handling it."

"Yeah."

"Maren, you were looking at the table just now."

"I look at the table all the time. It's where the table is."

"You were looking at it the way people look at someone who's talking. You tilted your head. Like you were listening to something near the table, except nothing was near the table, so."

The dwelling was quiet except for a chicken outside performing its daily complaint about the existence of morning. Light came through the single window in a bar that landed on the bowl, the ceramic ritual prop sitting on the table where it always sat, ordinary and specifically worthless.

"Derry —"

"And yesterday. In the square." Derry's voice had lowered, not for secrecy but for the weight the volume implied. "The threat notification that came through around midmorning — standard dungeon-mouth alert, nothing urgent. You flinched. You looked up and to the right, which is where the notification renders on the interface, and then you looked away like you'd caught yourself."

Maren said nothing.

"I've been standing next to you for eleven years while those come and go. I'm the one who tells you what they say. I've described hundreds of those notifications to you and you've never once — not once — reacted to one on your own. You've never looked where they actually are, because you can't see where they are. That's the whole point. That's why we work."

*He is more observant than you give him credit for,* Aldric said.

The silence had weight to it. Morning heat and dust and the faintly sweet smell of contamination that drifted from the north when the wind came off the fields wrong.

"Something answered," Maren said.

Derry went still. His hands, which had been pulling at a loose thread on his sleeve, stopped.

"At the ritual. Over the farmer. I wasn't — I mean, it was fake, same as always, but something — someone —" Maren stopped and started again, a worse sentence but a more honest one. "I've got a dead man in my head. A paladin. He died and ended up in the contamination and when I did the ritual he came through. He's been in my skull for two days and he reads me the interface."

He waited for the reaction. Derry's face went through a sequence: disbelief, then calculation, then a kind of stalled horror that didn't resolve into any single expression but sat there like a sum that wouldn't balance.

"A paladin," Derry said.

"Yeah."

"Talking to you."

"All the time. He's got opinions about what I eat, how I walk, whether I'm —" Maren cut himself off because the sentence was heading toward the fact that Aldric had critiqued his posture during urination yesterday and that was not a conversation for this morning. "The point is he can see the System. He reads it to me. And I'm reacting to what he says without meaning to — he tells me something and I flinch, or I look, and I can't stop doing it."

*You could, in fact, stop it,* Aldric said. *With discipline. Which you do not have.*

"He says it's my fault," Maren added.

Derry rubbed the back of his neck. His hand was trembling, a fine vibration visible in the sweat that caught the morning light on his knuckles.

"So the fraud's done."

"The fraud was done the second I threw up in that ditch."

"And he's not — this paladin isn't going away."

*I would prefer to go away. I am in this room against my preference and my will, same as every room I have been in for the past two days.*

"He's not going away."

Derry looked at the eggs, the bowl, the stained table. His gaze snagged on each thing in turn and stayed there a beat too long, as if he were already trying to remember the room from someplace else.

"All right," he said. "All right. So what do we do?"

***

They walked to the well because water still had to be fetched, even now, even with a dead paladin commentating on the structural integrity of the well rope and the ambient contamination levels of the drainage ditch and the specific threat classifications of three birds sitting on the chapel roof.

*Residual contamination at the northwest field boundary reads point-three,* Aldric said. *Climbing. Yesterday it was point-two-six. Consistent with uncleared accumulation from six to eight months of inadequate sin-eating maintenance.*

Maren pulled the bucket up. The rope was rough and familiar in his hands and the bucket came heavy with cold water that sloshed against its sides.

"Six to eight months of inadequate maintenance," he muttered. "That'd be me."

*I was not going to say that.*

"You were absolutely going to say that."

Breda was on her doorstep with her morning broth, chin tipped toward the square, following everything in it without seeming to do more than move her eyes.

"Morning, Maren. You're looking better."

"Thanks, Breda. Bit of a rough patch." The performance rose automatically: easy warmth, a slightly sheepish smile, his shoulders loose and open. He'd been performing since seventeen and the muscles of it were deeper than thought.

"My sister's boy had something similar," Breda said. "Fits. He grew out of it."

"I'm twenty-eight, Breda."

"So was he, at the time. A man can grow out of things at any age." She smiled and sipped her broth.

Derry had stopped a few paces back, hands in his pockets, eyes moving between Breda, the well, and the lane out of the square.

"She doesn't know anything," Maren said quietly once they were past.

"I know she doesn't." Derry's voice was tight. "That's not — I wasn't thinking she —"

"Then what?"

"I'm thinking about who does know. You flinched in the square yesterday, Maren. I'm the one who noticed because I know what to look for, but I'm not the only person in this village with eyes."

Maren splashed water on his face. The cold sat on his skin, a sensation with no data attached, no System overlay, no numbers. Just cold water on warm skin on a summer morning.

*Maren.*

Aldric's tone had changed. The running commentary, all the contamination indices and threat assessments and moral objections to Maren's hygiene, had been constant for two days, a stream that Maren had started learning to push to the back of his attention the way a person stops hearing a clock. But this was different. This was the voice Aldric used when the formal architecture of his speech went rigid, when the sentences got shorter and the words got more precise, and Maren had known him for only two days but he already understood that Aldric getting more formal was the same as a normal person screaming.

*The System has been auditing a contradictory record since last night,* Aldric said. *Yours. The queries were broad and regional, but not public. I did not tell you because until it resolved there was no classification, no bounty, only the fact that the System had noticed something it could not sort.*

Maren dried his face on his sleeve. Derry was watching him, waiting, reading the pause the way he'd learned to read Maren's pauses over eleven years of partnership.

"What?" Derry said.

"Hold on." Maren put the bucket down. His hands wanted to grip something and the bucket handle was the available option.

*It is resolving now. The classification has just gone live. Designation: unclassified entity. Threat level: active. I have never —* A pause. *This designation is not standard. It is reserved for systemic anomalies. Infrastructure failures. The classification itself is a contradiction — a designation for the unclassifiable. And the System has attached a hero bounty.*

"A bounty," Maren said, and the word came out quieter than he'd meant.

*Four thousand gold crowns. Broadcast initiated now. Hero-class interfaces receive the contract first. Civilians receive only the hazard banner, and it writes in layers. People in villages this close to dungeon territory learn to ignore the first flash unless the second one tells them why.*

The bucket handle bit into his fingers. He was gripping it hard enough to feel the grain of the wood, the places where years of hands had worn the surface smooth and the places where splinters still caught, and these details mattered because the number didn't make sense and the handle at least was real.

"That can't be right. Say it again."

*Four thousand gold crowns. Capture or elimination. Unclassified entity, Fenhold region. The bounty is live and broadcasting at maximum range, which at this threat designation I estimate to be — considerable. Far beyond this village.*

"Maren." Derry's voice had changed. Not the chatter, not the nervous fill, not even the low-and-serious register from this morning. Something else. Something flat. "Maren, I've got a regional warning on my interface."

Maren looked at him. Derry's eyes had the unfocused quality of a person reading System text, the slight divergence and small involuntary tracking movements that Maren had spent eleven years learning to mimic and never actually experienced. He'd spent those same eleven years reading every prompt that crossed his vision fast enough to keep Maren's fraud upright. If anyone in Fenhold was going to catch the first ugly blink instead of waiting for the chores to finish, it was him.

*That is the civilian banner,* Aldric said. *It does not include the bounty.*

"Active threat in Fenhold region," Derry said. "Stay clear. Report hero contact if sighted. Maren, what did he say?"

"I'm the threat," Maren said. "Four thousand gold crowns."

"That's —" Derry's voice went high and cracked. "That's not real. That can't — bounties like that don't — people put together whole companies for numbers like that. Real companies, with gear and party compositions and — four thousand crowns buys you a hero-class squad and enough left over to retire on, and if the hero feeds have that already then they've got our region marked."

"I know."

"Every hero between here and wherever the hero feeds reach can see it, and if that warning banner finishes writing before we're gone every farmer in the square is going to know Fenhold's got something bad sitting in it."

"I know, Derry."

They were standing at the well in the village square on a late-summer morning and the blacksmith was hammering and a dog was barking at the chapel door and three children were chasing something down the lane with the directionless joy of children running, and all of it was the same as yesterday and the day before and every day for eleven years. Breda was still drinking broth. The blacksmith still had his hammer up. Whatever red had started writing itself across the village's vision had not reached the part that made people drop what they were holding and start shouting. But the hero feeds had the number already, and the direction was here.

*At this bounty value, organized parties will mobilize within hours,* Aldric said. *I am uncertain of the nearest hero staging area, but this is not a sum that inspires patience.*

Derry's face had gone the color of wet clay. His hands hung at his sides and his wedding ring, a plain copper band gone green at the edges from years of soil and sweat, caught the sun the way metal catches the sun, without caring what kind of morning it is.

"We have to go," he said. "Right now. We have to — Maren, this village, these people — if heroes come here tracking the bounty and start asking about the sin-eater—"

"They'll find out I've been faking."

"They'll find out we've both been faking. And they'll find out there's an unclassified whatever-it-is living in the village where their neighbors live, where the children are, and that's not — people don't handle that well, Maren, they don't — and if anyone in Fenhold figures out what that bounty means before we're gone—"

*He is correct. Your departure should be immediate.*

"How fast can you be ready?" Maren asked.

Derry looked at him. The answer had been sitting in his face since he walked in with the eggs, in the late pauses and the way his eyes kept slipping toward the lane home. Maren had read it hours ago and kept skidding past it anyway.

"Lenna," Derry said. "And Pell."

The bucket handle dug into Maren's palm. He'd done the arithmetic already, but hearing their names made it uglier. Heroes tracked fast, and a woman carrying a child not yet three would leave a trail all the way to whatever caught up.

"They can't come." Maren didn't phrase it as a question.

"I know they can't come." Derry's voice was still flat, but there was strain under it now, breath forced through clenched teeth. "I know. I'm just — give me a minute to—"

He didn't finish. His mouth pressed thin and his hands found each other and the fingers laced and squeezed, the green-edged ring pressing between his knuckles.

"Go talk to her," Maren said. "I'll pack."

"Yeah." Derry took a step toward the lane that led to his house and then stopped. "Maren."

"What?"

"This is because of you. All of this — the bounty, the ritual, whatever happened at the farmer's house, this paladin — and my wife and my daughter are in that village while that number's hanging over it with our location on it, and I'm not — I'm not saying it to be cruel, I just need you to know that I know."

The blacksmith hit iron. A dog kept barking at the chapel door. Derry's jaw worked once. The hand with the ring opened and closed at his side, and his eyes went past Maren toward the lane to his house before coming back.

"I know," Maren said.

Derry went.

***

Maren packed. The bag took two minutes.

He rolled the spare shirt around the ceramic bowl so it wouldn't crack against anything. The bowl was light and plain and the cheapest he'd been able to find at market eleven years ago, when he'd first decided to fake sin-eating rituals and needed a prop. Four coppers. He remembered that part because it had left him one heel of bread and nothing else, and because he'd stood there at seventeen with the bowl in his hands thinking holiness looked cheap if you bought it from a woman selling turnips two stalls over. It had no System properties. It was not special. He'd held it over a hundred and thirty bodies by now, enough houses and barns and sickrooms that the lie had started to feel like weather, something people lived inside because he was there making the motions. The words and hand positions had come from a woodcut in a stolen book. What the woodcut hadn't taught him, he'd stolen later from a real sin-eater working outside Fenhold: the long pauses, the lowered head, the careful throat-clearing before the last line, all the little bits that made people think the work had been done. He'd been living off that theft for eleven years. Now the bowl felt heavier than fired clay had any right to feel.

He tucked the candle stubs into the shirt's sleeves and counted his money: forty-three coppers in a leather fold. He counted them twice because the first count felt wrong, not the number, which was accurate, but the scale of what the number needed to cover.

*Forty-three coppers,* Aldric said, having apparently done the same arithmetic. *The bounty on your head is four thousand gold crowns and your total wealth is forty-three coppers.*

"I didn't ask for commentary."

*That is not commentary. It is arithmetic.*

Outside, the village kept making its usual noises: the blacksmith's hammer, the dog finally giving up on the chapel door, children farther down the lane still busy with whatever mattered to children on a hot morning. Maren shut the door before he could start turning Fenhold into inventory.

Somewhere across the square somebody cursed at the first red wash and then kept talking. That was the whole margin. One more layer and the warning would stop being background noise and start being about Fenhold, specifically, right now.

Derry came back.

He'd been gone twenty minutes. His bag was larger than Maren's, stuffed with bread, a blanket roll, a second pair of boots strapped to the outside with twine he'd tied badly because his hands were shaking. His eyes were red. One knot had already slipped half loose. His wedding ring was gone, and the skin beneath it made a pale band through the dirt on his finger.

He smelled like his house: woodsmoke, the herb soap that Lenna made from whatever grew in the garden that wasn't leeks, and underneath both, something smaller and sweeter, the particular warm scent of a child's hair pressed close, breathed in deep, held as long as the breathing would allow.

He set a cloth-wrapped heel of bread on the table. "Lenna said take this part first. It keeps longer."

"South?" he asked.

"South. North puts us closer to the dungeon mouth and the hero patrols."

*The southern route adds approximately two days before intersecting the main road, but avoids the highest concentrations of quest-light and active hero presence. Given the bounty's range, this is the correct decision.*

"South works," Maren said.

Derry nodded, picked up his bag, and went out first. He kept two paces between them before they'd passed the forge.

They walked past the blacksmith, who nodded at them without pausing his hammer, past Breda's doorstep, where her broth was finished and the cup sat empty on the stone beside her chair and she raised a hand without even sitting forward, already looking back into the square, past the chapel with its contamination haze in the ditch, and past the well where half an hour ago the number had fallen on them and the morning had emptied out.

At the village edge, where the lane turned to packed dirt and the first fields opened out toward the southern road, Derry stopped.

He turned and looked back at Fenhold, at the roofs and the forge smoke and the lane kinked left. Somewhere behind the first row of stone sat the house he'd built over six years of weekends and borrowed tools, with Lenna in it and Pell likely asking why the door kept opening and shutting.

His hand went to the pale band where the ring had been and stayed there.

Maren kept his eyes on the ruts ahead. The bowl in his bag tapped once against his spine when he breathed.

"Come on," Maren said, not unkindly.

Derry turned from Fenhold. He hitched his bag higher and fell back into that two-pace distance. Maren matched him without looking back. Dust climbed his boots at every step, and the bowl tapped his spine hard enough to count the pace for him.

*The southern road,* Aldric said. *Approximately six days of travel to the market town of Silt, assuming no encounters. I will provide updates as conditions change.*

"Thanks," Maren said.

Under Aldric's voice, the hum stayed low and steady and close.


# Chapter 4

The road south from Fenhold was packed earth going to powder in the late-summer heat, and Maren's boots left prints that filled in behind him like the ground couldn't be bothered to remember he'd passed. Two days of walking. Two days of Aldric inventorying quest markers, threat assessments, ambient contamination readings, hero patrol routes, every item delivered like the world had been handed to him misfiled. Two days of Derry filling the road with oven stories, bad directions, well-water opinions, and whatever else he could get into the air before silence settled properly.

*Quest notification. Range: approximately three hundred meters, northeast. Level eight, expired. Residual contamination minimal.*

"Thanks," Maren said.

Derry glanced back from two steps ahead. He'd been keeping that distance since Fenhold, not leading so much as placing space between them without admitting to it. "What?"

"Nothing."

Derry's shirt was dark between the shoulder blades. He'd been adjusting and readjusting the straps of his pack every half mile, a habit that had nothing to do with the fit and everything to do with needing his hands occupied. He was carrying bread from the last village they'd skirted. He'd traded a copper for a loaf and another for directions south, and both transactions had taken him three times as long as necessary because he'd gotten the baker talking about her oven and the directions man talking about road conditions.

"You keep talking to yourself," Derry said. He wiped his face with his sleeve. "People notice that."

"There's no people."

"There will be. Settlement somewhere past this ridge. I saw chimney smoke this morning."

*Correct. Settlement bearing west-southwest, approximately two miles. Population estimate based on ambient System activity: forty to fifty individuals. Agricultural. No hero presence detected.*

The information scrolled through Maren's skull in Aldric's voice. Three days of this and Maren still couldn't decide if the constant narration was a gift or a punishment. Both, probably. The paladin translated the visible architecture of a world Maren had lived in for twenty-eight years without seeing, and he did it with the slight persistent distaste of a man reading ingredients off a package he would not eat from.

They walked. The sun sat heavy on the road and the dust had a specific quality out here, finer than Fenhold's, more like chalk than dirt. It got into Maren's throat and stayed. Each stone came through the thin soles of his boots. He'd been meaning to get them resoled for two seasons, and now that was the kind of problem that belonged to a life he'd walked away from.

Derry kept up a running assessment of the settlement ahead. Maybe they cut around it. Maybe they went through and kept their heads down. Maybe a place that size would have a decent well and maybe it wouldn't, and if the water tasted of iron you could never get the taste out of bread. He wondered if the smoke meant cookfires or a kiln, whether southern farms ran more goats than chickens, whether Pell would hate this dust because it got into your sandals and rubbed blisters where the straps sat. His hands kept going to the pack straps. Pull. Adjust. Release. Pull.

Maren let him talk. It was familiar.

Then the hum changed.

He'd been hearing it his whole life, that low-frequency drone at the edge of perception, louder near dungeon mouths and contamination zones, the sound of the System doing whatever the System did. Most people filtered it out unconsciously. Maren couldn't. His brain had nothing to filter it against. It was always there under everything else, like the sound of his own blood.

But now it thickened. Got denser, one note fattening into a chord. Something ahead of them, past the next rise where the road dipped between two low hills.

"Stop," Maren said.

Derry stopped. "What?"

*Hold on.* Aldric's voice shifted, less narration, more attention. The difference between reading aloud and actually looking at something. *Quest marker. Abandoned. Level twelve, deactivated approximately three weeks ago. The contamination was not cleared after deactivation. It has been pooling.*

The smell hit next. Ozone-sweet and rotten underneath, the specific reek of quest residue left to ferment in summer heat. Fresh contamination smelled almost clean, chemical. This had been sitting for weeks, baking in the warmth, and it had turned, that sour undertone like fruit going to vinegar, the smell of something biological beginning to break down.

"Yeah, I can smell it," Derry said. He'd gone still. His hands were locked on the pack straps and not moving. "That's contamination. From a quest?"

"Abandoned one." Maren walked to the top of the rise and looked down.

The hollow was maybe thirty paces across, a natural depression where two low ridges met. Quest-light pooled in it like rainwater. Maren could see the shimmer, that pale glow that was all the System's brilliance amounted to from his side of the blindness. Without the data Aldric could read overlaid on it, it was just light caught in a dip in the ground. Pretty, almost, if you didn't know what you were looking at. The contamination sat above the pool like humidity, thick enough that the grass at the edges had yellowed and gone brittle. No insects. Even the flies had pulled back.

*Contamination density is increasing at approximately four percent per day. The topography is funneling the drift westward, toward the settlement. At current rate, it reaches the settlement boundary in six to eight days. If it contacts their water source first, which is likely given the drainage pattern, the timeline may be as little as four.*

*In children especially,* Aldric added, and his voice had changed register, moved from System narration into something that sounded almost like the prayer cadence Maren had heard in those first terrible hours, *the effects of sustained exposure at this density are significant.*

"We should go around," Derry said. He'd come up behind Maren and was staring at the hollow with the expression of someone who understood exactly what he was looking at and wanted no part of it. "There's got to be a way around. We add an hour, maybe two, and we just keep walking."

"Four days until it hits their water."

"And someone will clear it. A real sin-eater, someone who actually knows what he's doing. Maren. Come on."

Derry was right. They had a bounty on their heads, four thousand gold crowns, enough money to pull every hungry hero in three regions onto their trail. Every hour they stood still was an hour for someone to close the distance. The smart play was to walk wide, add the detour, keep moving. Let whoever was supposed to clear the contamination clear it.

But nobody was going to clear it. The quest had been abandoned three weeks ago and no sin-eater had come, and Maren knew as surely as he knew the weight of his own ceramic bowl in his pack, the specific heft of eleven years of fraud, exactly why. There weren't enough sin-eaters. There never were. The work piled up faster than the workers could manage, and the System kept generating quests and the quests kept generating contamination and the contamination kept pooling in hollows like this one, drifting toward settlements where farmers drew water from streams and children played in the fields and no one thought about what was seeping through the soil because someone was supposed to have cleaned it up.

Someone like him. Who hadn't.

"Maren." Derry's voice was careful now. "We've got a bounty. Every hero in range can see it. We don't have time for this."

"I know."
