*You cannot be serious.* Aldric's voice had gone clipped, which meant he had already assessed the situation and was marshaling objections in formal order. *You have never performed a genuine consumption. You do not know the ritual sequence. You do not understand the containment protocols. The failure modes of a Level twelve consumption attempted without training or supervision are severe.*

"What happens if nobody clears it?"

Silence from Aldric. Then, quietly: *You know what happens.*

"Tell me how to do it."

*I am a paladin. I studied sin-eating theory within the Order. I did not practice the discipline. The gap between theory and execution in this case is not small.*

"So it's theoretical." Maren was already walking down the slope. His boots slid in the dry grass and he caught himself with one hand against the warm earth. "Walk me through the theory."

---

He set up at the edge of the pool as he'd set up a thousand times before. Bowl on flat ground, candle to the left, himself kneeling with his palms on his thighs. The postures of his old life. He could do these in his sleep, had done them in his sleep once, Derry shaking him awake at a farmer's bedside and Maren's arms already moving through the opening gestures before his eyes were open.

But the intention was different, and it sat in his chest like a fist. His chest didn't want to open.

*The containment pass must come first. You establish the boundary of the consumption zone before attempting any clearing action. Think of it as creating a vessel around the existing pool. Not with the bowl. With your awareness. Your attention. The real ritual requires genuine openness to the contamination. You allow it to contact your consciousness without your consciousness rejecting the contact. This is the component that cannot be faked.*

"Great. How do I do that?"

*That is precisely the gap between theory and practice. The texts describe it as a form of acceptance. You are not consuming the contamination yet. You are acknowledging it. Allowing it proximity.*

Maren looked at the pool. The shimmer was right there, an arm's length away. Up close, the contamination had physical weight. The glow itself was faint and directionless. The real force was pressure on the skin, like standing too close to an oven. His forearms broke into gooseflesh. The sweet-rotten smell was thick enough to coat his tongue.

His body knew what contamination meant the same way it knew what cliff edges and snapping dogs meant. By staying away.

"Maren." Derry stood at the top of the ridge, both hands wrapped around his pack straps, his knuckles pale. "I don't like this. I know you know that. But do you actually know what you're doing? Because if you don't, maybe we find somebody, or maybe we leave a message at the settlement, tell them there's contamination, let them send for help."

"Send for who, Derry?"

Derry's mouth worked for a moment. Then he looked away.

Maren knelt. The ground at the pool's edge was damp and warm, heat radiating up through his knees, and the dampness soaked through his trousers immediately. He placed his hands on his thighs and tried to do the thing he'd been pretending to do for eleven years. He thought of sickrooms, kitchens, barns, the places where worried families had stood back and let him move his hands and murmur over a bowl while Derry fed him cues. Those rooms had always felt safer once he began, because once he began he knew his part. Here the part was the thing that killed people.

Opening. That was Aldric's word. Opening yourself to the contamination. Letting it in.

For eleven years, every ritual Maren had performed was about closing. Closing the performance. Sealing the lie. Keeping the boundary between what he appeared to be and what he was. Every gesture, every murmured word, every careful angle of the bowl was designed to keep the contamination out while looking like it was being let in. His whole career was a locked door dressed up as an open one.

Now he had to actually unlock it, and every instinct he had pulled the other way.

He breathed. The contamination smell flooded his sinuses, ozone and rot and that chemical sweetness, like something organic being dissolved. His eyes watered. His throat clenched and he swallowed bile and kept breathing because Aldric had said openness and openness meant not pulling back when every nerve said pull back.

The first contact was heat. It came from inside, as if the contamination had found its way through his skin and was warming him from the blood outward. His fingers tingled against his thighs. The hum rose in pitch, or his hearing sharpened, or both.

*Good. That is initial contact. Now the containment pass. You must establish the boundary first.*

But Maren's body was already moving.

The old choreography, the broad sweeping arm motion he'd used in a thousand fake rituals, the one he always did first because it was dramatic and the paying families liked drama, pulled at his muscles like a reflex. Before Aldric finished speaking, Maren was halfway through the clearing pass, his right arm tracing the arc that had never cleared anything, that had always been pure theater.

Except now the contamination was inside him. And the clearing pass, done before containment, opened a channel that was not bounded by anything.

*No. Maren, stop. That is the clearing motion. You have not established containment.*

The contamination surged.

The pool of quest-light flared bright enough that Maren could see it without any help, raw light now, the pale yellow-white of a wound going septic. The hum spiked to a whine that drilled through his back teeth and settled behind his eyes. The contamination that had been sitting in its hollow pushed outward through the uncontained channel his botched ritual had opened, hit him like swallowing a river, and kept coming.

It went through him. Straight through. And it brought everything that was embedded in it, memory and habit and unfinished intent, all of it looking for purchase.

She hit like a blade between his eyes.

Aldric had arrived as weight and grief, a presence settling into Maren's skull with the heft of a man sitting down in a room he didn't want to be in. This came in edges. Fast, angular, precise even in violence. Something landed in his consciousness like a knife in a doorframe, point first, quivering, still carrying the force of the throw.

Maren's hands spasmed. The bowl tipped sideways and rolled into the warm mud. The candle went out. He was on all fours with his fingers digging into the wet earth while something new rearranged itself behind his eyes.

*What?* Aldric said.

*Where am I.*

A demand, flat and immediate. In the half-second before Maren's body seized, back arching, fingers clawing furrows in the mud, the new voice was already cataloguing. Location unknown. Position compromised. No weapons. Body not hers. Someone else's hands in the dirt. Someone else's lungs pulling someone else's air. Another presence already here. Holy order. Formal classification. A paladin.

*You've got to be fucking kidding me.*

The collision happened.

The fragment's arrival had already happened. The collision was what came next, when two opposed System classifications occupied the same unclassifiable space. Maren felt it like a bone breaking, the wrongness first, the deep structural protest of something being forced past its design tolerance. His vision whited out. His jaw locked. Something inside his skull crackled like ice splitting in a glass, and his body moved without his direction, his hands lifting from the mud and tracing patterns he'd never learned, his head turning to track something only the fragments could see, his spine straightening and then curving as if pulled by two different forces at once. His teeth snapped together hard enough to spark pain through his jaw, and the taste of blood came a beat later.

The quest-light around them flared to a brightness that hurt. The hum climbed past hearing into something felt, a vibration in his teeth and his sternum and the bones of his wrists. Even the ground seemed to shift, the warm damp earth going cold under his knees and then hot again, as if the land itself couldn't decide what it was near.

Then it stopped.

He was face-down at the edge of the hollow, covered in mud and his own sweat. The candle was out. The bowl was somewhere behind him. Inside his skull, where there had been two voices, his own thoughts and Aldric's careful narration, there were now three, and the three didn't fit.

*Who the hell are you,* the new voice said, directed at Aldric. No warmth. No curiosity. The tone of someone clearing a room.

*I am, I was, Aldric Venn, Knight-paladin of the Order of the Veil's Fourth Seal.*

*A paladin. In this host.* The word "host" landed with a contempt so specific it had texture. *And the host is what? What's wrong with this body? Why can't I see the interface? Why can't I get a read on anything?*

She stopped. The silence lasted two full breaths.

*I'm dead.*

The statement had no wobble in it. No plea for contradiction. She laid it down like an inventory item, and Maren hated her a little for how cleanly she could do that.

Maren pushed himself up to his knees. His arms shook. He could taste copper and ozone and the sweet rot of contamination that had passed through him and left residue on everything, his tongue, his teeth, the lining of his throat.

*I was on a contract.* The voice had gone flat, not calm but forcibly stabilized, like a table propped level on an uneven floor. *Torren Blackhall. Third bell. East gallery. Shift change with a blind corner at the stair. I was three days from finishing it.*

She stopped again. Recalibrated. Maren could feel her doing it, a mind faster than his reorganizing itself around information that changed everything.

*How long.*

"What?" Maren's voice came out wrecked, scraped raw from the bile and the ozone.

*How long have I been dead.*

"I don't know. The quest's been abandoned three weeks, but you could've been in the contamination longer than that."

*I did not ask about the quest. How long since I died.*

*The contamination layers suggest the fragment has been embedded for a substantial period,* Aldric began, falling back into his report cadence.

*I wasn't asking you.*

"I don't know," Maren said. He sat back on his heels. His hands had gone still, which surprised him, because nothing else had. "I'm sorry. I really don't know."

Below the ridge, Derry shouted his name again. Gravel slid. He had started down and thought better of it halfway, stuck between wanting to help and not wanting to get near the roiling light.

*The host is System-blind,* Aldric said, addressing the new presence with the careful diction of a man explaining a fire to someone standing in it. *He cannot see the interface. I have been serving as translator.*

*System-blind.* A pause, not hesitation but calculation. *And you're reading the interface for him. A paladin, playing guide dog for a System-blind fraud. What is he? What's his class?*

*That is complicated.*

*Uncomplicate it.*

*He is a sin-eater. Fraudulently. For eleven years. He has no genuine training. What you just experienced was his second real consumption. The first was me. He does not know what he is doing. The System has placed a bounty on him because his classification changed.*

*A fraud.* The voice, Vess now, the name surfacing sharp and exact from the fragment, went flat in a way that made Aldric's formality sound like warmth. *I was consumed by a fraud. In a botched ritual. In a ditch.*

"It's a hollow," Maren said, because his mouth still worked faster than his judgment.

*The distinction does not help your case.*

Aldric made a sound that might have been agreement. The first thing the paladin and the assassin had agreed on.

*So I'm dead.* Each word deliberate, placed like a stone on a scale. *I'm a fragment. In a System-blind fraud. With a paladin. My contract is open. Blackhall is still alive. My guild doesn't know what happened to me.*

She hit a gap. Maren felt it, a hitch in the current of her thoughts, like a foot stepping where a stair should be and finding air. She had reached for something and it wasn't there.

*My handler. There should be a name there.*

The gap widened. Maren could feel the edges of it, not blurred, not uncertain, but cleanly cut, as if something had been removed with precision rather than lost through decay.

*There's a piece missing.* There was no panic in it. Just the clinical notation of a professional discovering one of her tools was broken. *I can feel the relationship. I know the protocols, the drop locations, the code sequences. But the name is gone. That shouldn't happen.*

*Fragment degradation.* Aldric's voice had gone quiet, genuinely quiet, not his usual loaded silence. *Embedded consciousnesses lose coherence over time without proper consumption. Your memory structure has gaps. This is consistent with prolonged embedding in uncleared contamination.*

*I know what degradation means.* She said it once, precise and already finished with the subject. *What's the tactical situation.*

Derry called again, closer now, and Maren finally managed to lift one hand. It came up shaking and mud-slick. That was enough. Derry slid the rest of the way down, stopped when the reek hit him, and stared from Maren to the half-spent glow in the hollow with the expression of a man who knew the world had broken again and was furious that it kept picking him to witness it.

---

By the time Derry got him up the slope and propped him against a scrub pine, Maren's skull felt too crowded to belong to one man. Aldric held the back of it, rigid and upright, all rank and restraint. Vess had wedged herself behind his left eye and was already using the borrowed angle, checking the hollow, the road, the weak shine at the west lip. Maren's own thoughts had to shoulder between them.

Bark pressed through his shirt. Warm mud dried on his knees. The contamination stink had lodged so high in his nose he could taste it when he swallowed. Derry crouched three feet away, opening and closing his hands on the pack straps, his face the color of old whey.

"So there's two of them now," Derry said. Both hands on the pack straps, pulling. "Two. In your head. The paladin was already — and now there's another one, because you went down there and did the — and she's — wait. She's an assassin? She's an actual assassin?"

"Was."

"Was. Because she's dead too. Right." His hands worked the straps, released, grabbed again. "So you've got a paladin and an assassin both in there, and — I mean, can that even — a paladin and an assassin, that can't work, can it?"

Derry barely finished before the other two started.

*You let him open a level-twelve pool without containment,* Vess said.

*I did not "let" him,* Aldric said, his voice clipped down to wire. *The contamination is still active. The west boundary is failing.*

*So I'm dead, the bank's going, and I'm in a fraud,* Vess said. *Understood.*

"They're talking about me," Maren said to Derry.

"Bad?"

"Depends on your definition."

Derry looked at the hollow, at the quest-light still pooling in it. "Did the ritual work?"

Maren looked at the pool. The shimmer was different now, dimmer in patches, brighter in others, the contamination disturbed but not dissolved, like mud stirred in water. The smell had shifted too, more acrid, the stink contamination took on when it had been disrupted without being properly processed.

"No," Maren said.

*It did not,* Aldric said. His voice had gone thin and formal. *You disrupted the pool without establishing proper containment. It is moving now. Not cleared.*

*And I embedded instead of dissolving,* Vess said. *That is the other result.*

"Can it still hit the settlement?" Maren asked.

*Yes,* Aldric said. *Possibly faster. The west lip is failing.*

He kept going because he always kept going. *During the collision the System initiated a reclassification. It could not assign a designation to what resulted.*

He stopped.

"What does that mean?" Derry asked.

Maren waited.

Aldric tried again. *When I look at you now, from inside, I see*

The rest vanished. Maren waited for the correction, the report cadence, the clean second attempt. It did not come.

*Do not ask me to describe it. I cannot continue commentary.*

"Aldric."

Nothing.

Maren got one foot under him. The hollow blurred at the edges but he could still see the west lip shining where the runoff wanted out. "Then tell me how to finish it."

"No," Derry said at once.

*Also no,* Vess said. *Your left hand is numb past the knuckles. Your pulse is stuttering. You go back into that pool now and whatever pulled me in finishes the job.*

Maren looked at his left hand. Mud clung to fingers he could not quite feel. When he tried to flex them, they answered late.

*That bank gives way,* Vess said. *The runoff takes the low field and then the well. Move dirt or leave.*

Maren slid down the slope before he had finished deciding. The bowl lay half-buried where he'd knocked it over. He dug it out of the mud, wiped it once on his sleeve, and drove the rim into the soft earth at the west edge of the hollow. Ceramic scraped stone. He levered up a slab of wet dirt and dumped it against the sagging lip where pale seepage had already started to creep.

Derry stared at him. "What are you doing?"

"Buying them time."

"With a bowl."

"It's what I've got."

For one second Derry stayed where he was. Then he swore, slid down after him, and dropped to his knees on the cleaner side of the bank.

"Not like that," he said. "You'll have it slough off. Here. Pull from the roots. Pack it hard."

Maren did. So did Derry. The work turned stupid and physical fast. Bowl in, pry up mud, dump, tamp with both hands, again. Grass tore. Stones rolled under Maren's knees. The contamination warmed the muck nearest the glow until it felt almost body-hot, then sent needles of cold up his wrists when he shoved too deep. Ozone sat on the back of his tongue. Every breath tasted spoiled.

The first few loads vanished as soon as they hit the seepage, soaking through and sagging. They had to learn by failing. Bigger chunks. More roots. Stone pressed into the bottom to keep the mud from washing through. Derry cursed every time the bank slumped and immediately dug another armful.

*Two feet left,* Vess said. *The ground drops there.*

"Two feet left," Maren said.

Derry shot him a look and shifted anyway. He started cutting squares of grass loose with the edge of his hand and sliding them into the weak point roots-first, as if he were patching a garden trench at home instead of trying to hold contaminated runoff out of a stranger's well. They built the bank ugly and low: mud, torn grass, loose rock, the kind of emergency wall that only made sense if the other option was nothing. By the time Maren's shoulders started shaking, the west lip stood a handspan higher than it had.

Thin threads of luminous seepage still wormed through.

Vess took a beat to judge it. *If it holds, maybe until morning. If the ground slumps in the dark, less.*

Maren braced his hands on his thighs and looked toward the chimney smoke beyond the ridge. "We warn them."

Derry laughed once, sharp and tired. "No."

"We can't leave it like this."

"I know exactly what it looks like." Mud streaked Derry's forearms to the elbow. He wiped sweat off his mouth with the back of one wrist and only made a bigger smear. "I heard the part about the well. I heard the part about children. I've got one too, remember? I'm not walking into that settlement with four thousand gold crowns hanging over your head because you picked one bad hole you couldn't pass."

Maren opened his mouth.

Derry kept going, words tripping over one another now that he'd started. "No, listen. I left Pell once already. I left Lenna. I did that. I'm here. Fine. But I'm not doing this part too. We go in there looking like this, smelling like this, and either they panic or they hold us long enough for somebody with a sword and an interface to notice. Then what? Then I die in a place that doesn't even know my name."

Maren looked back at the hollow. The bank they'd built was already sweating light through the seams.

*He's right,* Vess said. *You go in covered in opened contamination and they stop hearing after the first sentence. This buys them something. Not enough. Something.*

Barely enough. A wall of mud and roots. Until morning if the ground was merciful.

He wanted to say there had to be something else. A better ritual. Better hands. Better life, maybe. The words didn't come. Aldric stayed silent.

Half a mile south, a creek cut under the road through reeds and flat grey stone. Maren knelt in the shallows and scrubbed contamination mud off his fingers, his wrists, the outside of the bowl. The water took the dirt and left the smell. He rinsed his mouth, spat, rinsed again, and still ended up bent over on one hand while bile and creek water came up stringy between his teeth. Derry washed downstream without looking at him. When they climbed the far bank and found the road again, Derry let Maren go first and kept a half-step of distance that was no longer accidental.

The new arrangement inside Maren kept showing itself in small ugly ways on the climb. His weight shifted off loose stones before he knew they were there. His left hand curled twice for a knife he did not own. At the top of the bank his shoulders snapped square and upright on some borrowed paladin instinct while the rest of him was still bent from retching, and the mismatch nearly pitched him sideways back into the reeds. *Duck,* Vess said, and he did, a beat before a willow branch whipped across the path where his eyes had been. Aldric stayed silent through all of it. The silence beside her flat efficiency made the inside of Maren's skull feel less crowded and more dangerous.

Nobody spoke after the creek crossing.


# Chapter 5

Aldric hadn't spoken since the night before.

Maren had walked for hours with that silence inside him. It had weight, a mouth-shut pressure that told him somebody was choosing it. He knew that shape. Priests had gone quiet like that when they caught him mid-fraud. Derry's wife had worn it the night they left Fenhold. Villagers had carried it while deciding whether to pay for a ritual they suspected was fake. It was the loudest kind of quiet he knew, the kind that meant someone had seen something and still hadn't decided how to carry it.

The road ran between two fields that had gone fallow long enough for the weeds to turn woody, knee-high tangles of dry yellow scrub that rattled when the wind shifted. Late-summer sun pressed on the back of his neck. The pack kept rubbing the same raw spot on his right shoulder it had been working on since Fenhold. Half a step behind him, Derry kept the same cramped gait he'd had ever since they left the hollow where the botched consumption had scrambled everything sideways. Neither of them had spoken since the creek crossing.

Vess was not quiet.

She'd been inside his skull for less than a day and was already doing something Maren could only call *watching*, a constant low alertness sitting behind his eyes like the front edge of a headache. She tracked the road ahead, the treeline to the east, the ditch running alongside the packed dirt, and the angle of shadows on the ground. Aldric would have narrated the interface. Vess simply worked, and Maren got the result: a prickling presence behind his eyes, uninvited, constant, thorough.

*Two hundred yards. South. Two, armed, approaching from the sheep fold.*

She delivered it with the household flatness of a warning that soup was getting cold.

"What?" Maren said aloud, and his voice came out hoarse, scraped thin from disuse.

Derry turned his head. "Hm?"

*They've been paralleling the road from the fold. They're closing now.* After a pause that sounded almost clinical, she added, *They look like they've been waiting.*

"Someone's coming," Maren said.

Derry didn't carry weapons. His hand went to the camp knife riding in the pack on Maren's shoulder, the short utility blade he used for rope and kindling and stubborn jar lids. His fingers brushed the handle and stayed there. That was as far as his courage went.

"How do you know that?" Derry started, but Vess cut across him.

*Aldric.*

Her voice cut sharp and impatient, like she'd knocked on a door she expected to find open.

The paladin came back like a latch lifting. His voice was flat and controlled, every sentence shaved down to bare facts and coming a shade too fast.

*Two hero-class combatants. System profiles active. They have been tracking the bounty marker. The younger one is reading your classification field and seeing nothing. He is confused. The woman is not confused.*

"How many?" Maren asked.

The question was aimed at both of them, the dead paladin and the dead assassin. The absurdity of asking two ghosts for a tactical count on a road in the afternoon sun might have been funny thirty seconds later, if thirty seconds later were not about to become a different kind of experience entirely.

*Two,* Vess said.

*Two,* Aldric confirmed. Then, with something that might have been reluctance, he added, *The bounty is flagged for hostile engagement. They are authorized to kill.*

They stepped out from behind the sheep pen's collapsed wall at maybe sixty yards. The late-afternoon sun was behind Maren, so they were walking into it and squinting, and for one stupid second he thought he could wave and keep going and they'd take him for some farmer heading home.

The woman was the closer threat. The knowledge reached his muscles before his mind managed to name it. She was around forty, maybe older, with weather-darkened skin and bracers that had been repaired more than once. The leather had been patched over with mismatched hide that said years of field work and no budget for new gear. A short sword rode on her hip, its pommel wrapped in faded blue cloth, wind-frayed and tied with the tight automatic knot of something she'd retied often enough to do by touch.

Her partner was younger, mid-twenties. A mace hung in one hand while he kept shifting his grip with the jerky uncertainty of a man who'd practiced in a yard but not against a body that bled. A port-wine birthmark ran along the left side of his jaw from ear to chin, and the pack on his shoulders sat too stiff and too new, every buckle still bright.

"That's the mark," the woman said. Her voice carried easily. It was calm and professional, the tone of someone who had done a version of this before.

The young one squinted at Maren. "His profile's empty. There's nothing, I mean literally nothing. I've never seen that."

"I know."

*The younger one's threat level is moderate. The woman is high. She has a class-enhanced blade, System augmentation on the edge. Her stance indicates...*

Aldric's assessment dissolved into irrelevance. The camp knife rode in Derry's pack on Maren's right shoulder, short-bladed and ash-handled and meant for rope and kindling. His fingers snapped around it, reflexive as a hand catching a railing when the stair gives out. The grab beat thought. By the time his mind got to the word weapon, the blade was free.

The pack shifted on his shoulder. Behind him, Derry made a sound, a small choked syllable that might have been "Maren" if it had gotten far enough to form.

The woman drew her sword and the hum thickened, packing itself into a drone close as insects at the inner ear. Something in the System's local field woke around the blade before Maren could place it. The air around the edge rippled with a faint shimmer, heatless and thin. Aldric could read it. Maren could only hear it.

She came forward at a walk. Each step covered exactly the distance she wanted. The blade sat in a guard position her System probably overlaid with optimal angles and threat trajectories and all the other combat mathematics Maren would never see. All he got was the woman herself: weight on her toes, shoulders set, eyes fixed on his center mass.

His legs ran toward her before any part of him approved it.

That choice came from Aldric. The paladin's doctrine was to meet an engagement rather than absorb one. Maren's body was six inches shorter and sixty pounds lighter than the body the training had been built for, so the stride came out wrong. His feet hit the packed dirt at intervals his muscles did not want, and the mismatch made him stagger on the second step.

*Lower,* Vess said. *You're too tall for this stance.*

The instruction made no sense. He was short. He had always been short. Even so, his knees bent and his center of gravity dropped, and suddenly the running worked. He went lower, faster, ugly and wrong-looking. Aldric's long stride fought Vess's crouch in his legs, and the result was a lurching sprint that ate the distance because neither set of instincts would give.

The woman swung.

She was good. The blade came in flat, a lateral cut at his ribs, and the System enhancement on the edge made the air whine like a finger drawn across wet glass. Up came Maren's left arm, forearm turned out, palm flat, the guard position for a shield he didn't have and had never held. Aldric's body memory put it there. Fourteen years of training with a steel disc landed on bare flesh and thin cotton.

Steel bit him below the elbow. The cut ran long and shallow; the enhancement wanted a thrust, and the flat angle turned it into a scraping drag across the outer forearm. Pain went up his arm in one wave, through shoulder and jaw. Everything below the cut turned cold. Blood ran into his palm and between his fingers, hot against skin gone numb, and the warmth pooling in the creases of his hand was the most physically real thing he had felt in six days.

The knife hand came up.

Vess supplied the intent. His hand found the gap between the woman's sword arm and her neck, the geometry of a body opening in the follow-through of a swing. She was committed to the arc. Her sword had gone wide and pulled her shoulder with it. The space between jawline and jerkin collar stood open. The camp knife was in his fist. The fist was already moving. He was inside her guard, closer than a sword could reach.

The blade went up into the soft triangle below her jaw.

The whole thing traveled through the handle: skin catching, then soft give, then cartilage yielding with a small wet pop. The handle twisted in his grip as the knife seated itself. Vess put the quarter-turn into it to widen the channel, a blank professional motion learned somewhere blood had to stay quiet.

The woman grabbed his right wrist.

Each ridge of her grip stood out against his forearm. Each finger was a separate pressure point digging into the tendons above his hand. She was strong. She was dying and stronger than he was, and her grip had nothing to do with combat now. It was only a body's refusal to accept what was happening to it.

*Pull it. Side-draw. Don't lift.*

The knife jerked sideways.

Dark blood ran down her collar and into the neck of her jerkin, dripping from the leather to the road. Her sword lay in the dust beside them. One of her patched bracers scraped over his skin as her hand slid off his wrist.

Then her grip went slack all at once, like a rope cut through under tension. She sat down in the road instead of falling, legs folding under her with a strange neatness. Her mouth moved. No sound came out. Her eyes stayed on his face, fully present and still processing, and then they were looking at nothing. The change was so thin he could not have pointed to it if his life had depended on naming the instant.

She died sitting up in the dust with one hand still raised toward the wrist of the man who'd killed her.

The younger hunter was already running. His mace clanged somewhere behind him on the packed dirt. The too-new pack bounced against his spine, bright buckles flashing in the sun. He ran like a man whose practice had all happened in yards and drills, all elbows and knees, no economy, breath ragged by twenty yards.

Maren's legs tensed with Vess's reflex, the professional urge not to leave a witness.

*No.* Aldric gave the word with more force than he'd used since the fragment collision.

That was enough. The pursuit stopped before it started.

Dust and copper sat on his tongue. The cut arm hung at his side. Blood ran from fingertips in slow drops that hit the road with small sounds he could not stop hearing or counting. The camp knife remained locked in his right fist, the ash handle glued to his palm by a cramp his conscious mind could not reach. He tried to open his hand. Nothing moved. A second try only tightened the forearm muscles until they stood out against the skin.

Behind the sheep pen wall, Derry breathed fast and shallow, pressed so flat against the stone he might have believed less space meant less danger.

In front of him, the woman sat with dust hazing around her in the low sun. A fly had already found the blood on her collar. She'd folded into the road so neatly that from here it looked like she'd chosen to sit.

Then he looked at his own right hand, at the knife he still could not drop, at the dark slick on the blade and between his fingers and in the lines of his palm. The thought came with the cold clarity of something borrowed from a more competent mind: Those are not my hands.

---

Behind the sheep pen wall, twenty feet from the body, he threw up.

The vomiting was mechanical, his stomach clenching around nothing, thin bile rising in two hitches from a body that had not eaten enough to give up much else. After that came only the clench and dry heaves, the diaphragm spasming against emptiness. By then the knife had dropped. He braced a palm against the stone and fixed on its warmth, the grit, the grain biting into his fingertips.

Derry sat with his back against the far end of the wall, hands shoved between his knees. Through the whole fight he'd been there behind the stones. He had not run and he had not helped, and neither of those facts looked likely to come up between them.

"We should move, I think. The other one ran, right? He'll go back wherever they came from, and there'll be more, won't there? So we should probably, I mean not right now. You need a minute. But soon. We should move soon. I've got the cloth strips. Lenna cut them from that old shirt of mine, the one with the..."

The sentence dried up when it reached the domestic detail. That shirt belonged to a life that was far away now, and both of them knew it.

Maren straightened and spat. Blood had started seeping through the left sleeve again, a slow dark spread that looked worse than it was because the edges were already drying. The cut ran about six inches along the outer forearm, shallow enough that the skin was trying to close. Cleaning it properly meant cutting away the sleeve and picking up the knife.

But the knife stayed where it was.

"Let me see it," Derry said, already on his feet and already digging into the pack for cloth strips. His hands shook, not as badly as Maren's but enough to show. He talked while he rummaged, about a creek that might be nearby, about whether this was the right roll of cloth, about how Lenna always said water should be boiled before it touched a wound and maybe they would just have to ignore that part.

Work steadied him. He wrapped the cloth around Maren's forearm with the same care he used on fence posts, tight and even and methodical. Once the knot was tied, he patted it lightly.

"There. It's not deep. I mean, the arm isn't. Are you okay there? That's a stupid question."

*A clean kill,* Aldric said.

The voice was quiet, almost gentle. Maren knew the register from the memories he carried: the tone a paladin used on young soldiers after first blood, calm and affirming, a trained kindness built over years of watching people die and teaching survivors how to name it.

*She felt no extended pain. The strike was precise and the angle correct. Your body performed with discipline under pressure.*

"Shut up."

Derry's hands stopped on the bandage.

"Not you," Maren said. His voice came out rough and scraped raw. "Him."

Derry nodded and went back to smoothing the edges of the cloth with careful fingers.

*I understand that the shock of it can...*

"You don't understand shit." The words came out harder than he intended, stripped of the charm and deflection he usually wrapped around anything that mattered. What was left was anger and fear and the raw misery of a man whose body had done something unspeakable while he watched from inside it. "Don't put your words on it. Don't call it a clean kill. That's the framing you're going with? A clean kill?"

Aldric went silent.

"Because it wasn't clean. Nothing about it was. I had a camp knife, Aldric. The thing Derry cuts rope with. I stuck it in a woman's throat and she held onto my arm while she died. She grabbed me. She held on. And you want to tell me that was clean?"

*I was attempting to provide context.*

"I know what you were attempting." Warm stone pressed against his back while he half sat against the wall with his left arm cradled to his chest. Blood was already seeping through the bandage. "You were attempting to make it okay. To put it in whatever box you've got. 'Clean kill, no suffering, proper engagement.' You've done this before. You've got a whole system for it, a whole way of talking about killing people that makes it fit inside your doctrine, or your framework, or whatever word lets you sleep after you've..."

The next word never came. His throat had closed around it and left it trapped somewhere behind the sternum.

From here he could still see the three different scraps in her bracers, ugly field repairs darkening where blood had run over the cuff.

*I have no framework for this.* Aldric's voice had changed. The trained gentleness was gone. What remained was rawer, stripped of protocol. *I had a framework. I am attempting to use it because it is the only thing I have. The alternative is to describe what it felt like to watch your body kill that woman with a precision that was partly mine and partly Vess's, and I do not have...*

The sentence stopped there. For Aldric, whose sentences usually closed like doors, that break carried its own alarm.

Vess spoke into the silence he left.

*They were a scouting pair. Standard bounty protocol for unknown targets. Send two to confirm, then dispatch a full party. Their approach puts the staging town less than a day east. When the younger one reports, someone will come looking.*

She could have been reading tomorrow's weather.

"How long?" Maren asked.

*Tomorrow. Maybe tonight, if he ran the whole way.*

Derry folded the remaining cloth strips into a square too neat to matter, stuffed them back into the pack, then lifted the shoulder strap off Maren with two careful fingers and settled it on his own back. He still wasn't looking at Maren's face. When Maren reached for the waterskin, Derry set it on the dirt between them and nudged it over with his boot.

"We should move," Derry said. He kept his eyes on the road beyond the wall. "Before that boy gets somewhere with people in it. Before he gets horses, maybe. I don't know. We should move."

So they moved.

The knife stayed on the ground by the sheep pen wall until Derry went back for it himself. He wiped the blade on a fistful of dry grass, then again on the inside hem of his shirt, jaw tight the whole time, before sliding it into the pack under the spare shirt and the bread. Maren never touched the handle again.

Road and sun and dust stretched on. Shadows lengthened east. Dry weeds rattled in the ditch. Once, blood seeped through the bandage again, and Derry retied it without speaking. When the road narrowed between two deep cart ruts, Derry fell farther back rather than brush shoulders. Later Maren slowed out of habit, waiting for him to come even. Derry slowed too and kept the same strip of dust between them. The hum from the nearest dungeon mouth came and went with the terrain. Quest-light pooled to the right of the road, faint in late sun, just heat-haze shimmer to Maren and data to everyone else alive.

His hands shook against his thighs. Pressing his palms flat into the fabric helped for a few seconds, and then it stopped helping. Fear had burned off somewhere in the first mile. What remained was the body's record of what had happened, written into the small muscles of his hands and the tendons in his forearms, testimony his mind could outrun for a moment but not erase. Every few minutes the right hand closed around nothing. The first two times he thought he was reaching for the old pack strap. By the third he knew it was looking for the knife.

Aldric said nothing. The silence had changed shape. Last night's had held its breath over something he could not describe. This one belonged to someone who had tried to help and been told, accurately, that his help was the wrong shape for the wound.

Vess said nothing because she had already said everything she considered relevant.

Derry talked instead. He talked about the road, about the deeper ruts and what they meant for carts, about the chance of a market town ahead and the fact that his waterskin was nearly empty. Then he found the well in Fenhold, the one by the south wall with the rope that always jammed. Pell used to hang on the crank handle and try to pull it up herself. She was too small to move the rope, but she'd hang there with both hands and her feet off the ground and laugh.

He stopped when he reached his daughter. The talking had carried him straight to the place it was supposed to carry him away from.

"Sorry," Derry said.

"Don't be sorry."

"I just, she keeps, I keep thinking about her. Is that stupid? We're out here and you just... and I'm thinking about whether she can count to twenty yet. She was stuck on fourteen. She'd go thirteen, fourteen, fourteen, fourteen."

"Not stupid," Maren said.

Dark took its time. Off the road, Derry found a fallen oak that made a windbreak, its trunk mossy and soft enough on top that leaning against it did not hurt. They sat with Derry across from him and the pack between his knees. No one built a fire. The evening stayed warm, late-summer warmth that would not break until deep night, and the sky drained itself by stages until the quest-light in the ditches shone brighter than the last blue overhead. It looked pale and cold against the dark, System residue pooled in every low place as patient as groundwater.

Back against the trunk, hands flat on his thighs, Maren listened to the hum. It was louder here than on the road. Maybe they were closer to a dungeon mouth. Maybe the residue in the ditches ran denser than usual. The sound sat below ordinary hearing, bone-deep and steady. Aldric and Vess and everyone else System-sighted in the world filtered it out because the interface told them what it meant, and meaning was enough to make it disappear. Maren had heard it for twenty-eight years without knowing what it was. Aldric had arrived and told him the hum was the world running.

After a while Maren said, "Need the water."

Derry took the skin from the pack, uncapped it, and set it on the ground beside the shoulder strap. His hand rested on the leather a second longer than it needed to. Then he took it away.

Maren leaned forward and rinsed his mouth. The water tasted like warm leather and old metal. Pink threads ran off his fingers and disappeared into the oak roots. He scrubbed at the webbing between thumb and forefinger until the wound in his left arm pulled and the bandage went tacky again. The smell stayed. Iron and sweat and something animal under both. He wiped his hand on his trouser leg and only spread it thinner.

When he reached toward the open pack, Derry's head came up.

"The bowl," Maren said.

Derry looked at him, then past him into the dark, then back at the pack. He dug under the folded shirt and the bread, found the ceramic bowl by feel, and set it down between them instead of handing it over. The glaze caught a strip of quest-light and held it.

The bowl sat cool in Maren's palm, ordinary weight, ordinary curve. His fingers knew it before his mind did. He'd carried it through enough kitchens and deathrooms that half the old routine lived in his hands instead of his head. His thumb found the rim. Then came the turn, the breath, the stretch of stillness long enough for the family to believe stillness meant something had happened.

He got as far as the turn.

His right wrist snapped into the other motion, the one Vess had put through him on the road. It was a quarter-turn, hard and efficient. The bowl jerked in his grip. Water slopped over the lip and onto his thigh. For one blind second the slick glaze under his fingers turned to an ash handle, and the tendons in his forearm locked so fast the pain reached his elbow before he understood why.

He bent over the bowl and dry-heaved into it.

Nothing came up this time. His stomach had emptied itself by the sheep pen wall. What came instead was spit and bile strings hanging from his mouth to the inner glaze, his shoulders pulling hard enough to reopen the ache in his cut arm. The bowl rang once against a root when his hand shook. Derry made a small movement across from him and then stopped. He did not come closer.

*Put it down,* Vess said.

Maren tried. His fingers would not open. The hand had the same cramp it had taken from the knife, thumb clamped over first knuckle, wrist rigid. He had to use the other hand to peel them back one at a time. The last finger left a crescent of diluted blood on the white glaze.

*A conditioned response,* Aldric said, too quietly to sound like comfort. *Your body is pairing the movement with the kill.*

"Don't," Maren said.

That was all. Aldric gave him silence and kept it.

Maren set the bowl upside down in the dirt. A thin line of pink water leaked from under the rim and sank into the moss. Across from him, Derry pulled the pack back between his legs and folded his arms over it as if the posture were only for warmth. The knife was somewhere under his wrists now. When Maren shifted to ease the pull in his forearm, Derry shifted too, not away exactly, not toward either, just enough to keep the pack in the same place between them.

No one said they should sleep. No one asked what the bowl had been for. The night insects started up in the weeds. Far off, something with hooves moved through brush and kept moving. Quest-light filled the roadside ditch in a pale strip he could not quite see straight on and could not stop tracking at the edge of vision, there when he blinked, there when he looked away.

He looked at his right hand in the dark until the shape of it blurred. The woman had died holding that wrist. The quarter-turn stayed in the tendons like a wire pulled too tight; when his thumb brushed the rim of the overturned bowl by accident, the whole hand twitched.

He put both hands flat on his thighs again. Sometime after moonrise the cramped right one tried to curl, empty and hunting. He pressed it down harder until the nails of his left hand bit through the cloth and into his skin, and sat like that watching the overturned bowl gather dew and a skin of pale quest-light until the sky began to thin and he still had not slept.


# Chapter 6

The bread sat in Maren's hand like a stone. Two days in the bottom of the pack had turned the crust the color of dry mud, cracked one side, and left the inside dense and cold, the kind of cold that came from riding against a body all night and still not warming. Derry had broken the loaf in half and put the bigger piece in Maren's hand without looking him in the face.

They were sitting by a stream maybe half a mile off the road, in a spot where the bank had been cut back by spring flooding and then left alone. Birch roots clawed out of the exposed dirt, pale as tendons. The water was shallow and moved without much purpose, collecting in a flat pool before slipping through a gap in the rocks. Late summer light came through the canopy in uneven pieces. It was a good place to stop. It would have been a good place to eat.

Maren brought the bread to his mouth and his throat closed.

Not dramatically. No gagging, no heave. Just a quiet, total refusal. The muscles in his neck went hard and his jaw locked. The bread touched his lips and his body said no. The smell of it, yeast and flour and the faint sourness of age, rolled through him and his stomach turned with a slow greasy twist. He put the bread back on his knee.

There was still a rust-dark crescent under the nail of his right thumb. He had scrubbed at it before dawn with spit and ditch water until the skin around it went raw, but when he flexed the hand the tendons gave him the same small remembered pull they had yesterday, the quarter-turn that had put the knife where Vess wanted it and kept it there while a woman died holding his wrist. Bread and old iron tangled together in his nose until he could not tell which one made him sick.

His right hand shook once against the crust, small enough that only the crumbs gave it away.

*You should eat,* Aldric said.

The voice was flat. No lecture under it. No comparison to men who had served the Order with discipline and purpose. Just the statement.

Maren didn't answer.

*Your body requires fuel. Weakness will worsen if you do not take something in.*

*He knows,* Vess said. *Leave it.*

Silence inside his skull. The two of them pulling back to their separate corners, or whatever the equivalent was when three people occupied the same head. Maren could feel them like pressure changes, Aldric's disapproval a dull weight behind his right eye, Vess's watchfulness a cooler thing lower down, settling where his neck met his shoulders.

Derry was eating. Methodically, without any sign of enjoyment, tearing pieces off his half and putting them in his mouth and chewing and swallowing. His jaw worked with the steady patience of a man who had spent his life doing necessary things without much ceremony. He sat with his legs stretched toward the water, boots unlaced, sweat dried on his shirt in pale salt maps. He had gone red in the face from the walking and the heat and something else Maren did not look at directly.

"Pell counts everything now," Derry said.

Maren looked at him.

"Steps to the door. Chickens. Buttons. She did my sleeve this morning, every one twice because she lost track at the cuff and got mad about it. She gets stuck on seven. Says it once, knows it came out wrong, says it again like maybe the second one will land better." He tore off another piece of bread. "Then she has to start over. Whole world stops because seven won't behave."

"That's good," Maren said. His voice came out thin, as if he were speaking through cloth.

"Yeah." Derry chewed. Swallowed. "Lenna decided she was going to grow fennel by the south wall because the apothecary in Silt pays three coppers a bundle and somebody told her fennel will grow in anything. It won't grow in shade, which I knew and didn't say because I like living. She got an inch of green out of it and then the whole bed just gave up. She's already planning what to try next."

Maren watched Derry's hands on the bread. He held it the way he held everything else, firm and careful, the grip of a man who had spent his life picking things up and putting them down without damage. Eggs. Fence posts. Tin cups with cracked handles.

"And the cow got out again," Derry said. The words came with the exhausted affection of a man who had been bested by livestock. "Day before we left. I walked her back from Garron's field for an hour and a half. She doesn't run, that's the thing. If she ran I'd respect it. She just heads the wrong direction slow as weather and keeps going till you follow."

That almost got a laugh out of Maren. It came out as a breath through his nose, brief and broken, but Derry heard it and something in his face eased.

He stopped then, looking at Maren's untouched bread.

"You should eat," he said.

"I know."

"You really should."

"I know. I'm going to. Just not this second."

Derry nodded like that answered anything.

Maren broke off a piece of bread no bigger than his thumbnail and put it in his mouth because not trying had started to feel childish. The crust went damp at once and turned to paste against his tongue. He chewed twice. His throat sealed. By the time Derry bent to retie a pack strap, Maren was already spitting the mess into the grass beside his boot and grinding it under his heel like evidence.

The quiet of it shamed him more than throwing up would have. It felt sneaky, like refusing a kindness by bookkeeping trick.

For a while they sat there with the stream moving beside them and the light shifting in the canopy. A bird bounced a branch somewhere in the birches and then settled again. The air smelled like warm dirt, water, and underneath both of those the faint sweet ozone that meant quest-light was somewhere nearby. He had smelled it his whole life and never known what it was until Aldric told him four days ago in a voice that made the information sound like a personal failing.

The bread sweated a little in his hand. His thumb found the split in the crust and worried it wider. Fine crumbs stuck to the damp skin near his knuckles. Under that, or inside it, or in the part of him that had started inventing smells yesterday and refusing to stop, the iron note stayed put. He rubbed his thumb against the heel of his palm hard enough to raise heat and got nothing for it.

*We need to choose a route,* Aldric said.

Maren closed his eyes.

*The main road is untenable. Selwick Pass removes us from it and limits approach angles. There is stone cover for most of the ascent.*

*And a bridge,* Vess said. *One approach. No cover. Any tracker with half a class sits there and waits.*

*I am aware of the bridge. The pass itself remains defensible if we move before dusk.*

*Doesn't matter if he can't fight.*

The word sat in Maren's skull. Fight. What had happened yesterday, his body moving without him, the knife going in while he watched from somewhere behind his own eyes, was not a thing he knew how to call by that name.

*East gives us brush, bad sightlines, and room to disappear,* Vess said. *Off the roads. Move at night when the interface reads shorter.*

*East also gives us residual pools,* Aldric said. *If necessity drives him to consume again before we understand what the previous ingestion produced, the result may be catastrophic.*

Silence.

*I know,* Vess said.

Maren opened his eyes. The stream was still there. The birch roots were still there. Derry was lacing his boots, thick fingers working leather through the eyelets with the patience of a man who laced boots every morning of his life and expected to keep doing it for another thirty years.

Maren looked down at his right hand. When he let it rest open on his thigh, the fingers were almost still. When he loosened them any further, something ran through the tendons and the index finger tried to curl, empty and private, as if the hand were checking whether the knife was still there. He shut it again around the bread before Derry could see.

"Hey," Maren said.

Derry looked up.

"Tell me about the shoes again."

Something complicated moved through Derry's face and settled into relief. He put his heel down and leaned forward on his knees.

"She lines them up by the door," he said. "All of them. Mine, hers, the little pair with the loose strap, everything. Smallest to biggest. If one turns sideways she starts over and gives you this look like you've personally insulted the idea of order." He rubbed a thumb over the lace. "She's going to be three next month and already acts like the whole house is badly arranged."

Inside Maren's skull, the silence kept its shape. The Crook meant real sin-eaters, a whole camp of them looking at him long enough to understand what he was and then deciding whether the cleanest way to deal with the thing in him was to clear it or clear him with it. East meant low ground, hidden places, wet ditches where quest-light liked to gather, and the possibility that sooner or later desperation would put him on his knees in front of it again. Vess had said *I know* in the same tone she used for distances and arteries. That frightened him more than if she had kept arguing.

Selwick would put them on stone with clean sightlines and nowhere to hide the way his hand kept trying to remember. East meant wet ground, bad footing, and pools of residual light waiting in the ditches. The pass sounded like one clear way to die. East sounded like a hundred smaller mistakes wearing a man down until he made the worst one on purpose because hunger had made it look practical.

He pinched the bread until the crust cracked. Derry kept talking, and after a minute the words flattened into cadence more than meaning, the rise and fall of a tired man describing a world where boots could still be misplaced and that counted as trouble. Maren kept his eyes on him and let him go on. He could listen to that for another minute. He could do that instead of choosing.

---

The quest-light was in the water.

He had gone down to the stream to wash his hands because the smell would not leave them. Bread had made it worse. The heel of his right palm still held a trace of old leather from the knife hilt and something coppery underneath, not blood exactly, or not only that. He crouched at the bank, pushed both hands into the current, and the cold closed around his wrists so fast it almost felt clean.

To Maren it was what it had always been: a faint shimmer like heat haze, except the air was not hot enough and the shimmer moved against the current instead of with it. It pooled in the flat stretch where the stream slowed, collecting behind rocks and in the lazy circles where leaves turned without making up their minds. To Maren it looked like moonlight would look if moonlight had weight and preferences about where to gather. He had seen it a hundred times. A thousand. In ditches outside dungeon mouths, in the corners of rooms where someone had died, in the air above the ceramic bowl when he performed fake rituals and nothing answered.

He had stepped into the stream without thinking. Water moved over his boots, then around his ankles, the cold specific enough to strip every other sensation down to size. Light twisted in the pool. It caught on itself. It slid through his fingers and did not break.

"There's quest-light in the water," he said. Quietly, because Derry had gone upstream with the water skins and Maren was not really talking to Derry.

*I see it,* Aldric said.

A pause. Usually the reading came fast, the paladin's account of the interface laid over the world, data Maren could never see, delivered with the clipped rhythm of a man cataloguing someone else's mess. Threat assessment. Residual quest class. Contamination grade. Probability of hostile emergence. The information had been useful and humiliating in roughly equal measure.

But Aldric did not start reading.

*It is a residual pool,* he said. *Low-grade. Passive. No active quest structure. No threat emergence that I can detect.* Another pause, longer this time. *The interface markers are ordinary. The light is not. It is refracting through the water in a way I have not seen before.*

Maren watched the shimmer. It bent and stretched as if the whole pool were warped glass, pulling itself into shapes that had no name and did not need one. Where it caught on the skin of the water it made something he could almost call beautiful, except beautiful was not a word he trusted much. It asked things of a person. It suggested a kind of certainty he had never once been qualified to offer.

For eleven years he had borrowed other people's awe and sold it back to them. Candle smoke, solemn pauses, the careful angle of a bowl held toward a window. He knew what wonder looked like on a face because he had spent a decade studying it for work. This was the first time the strangeness belonged to him before it belonged to anybody else.

*How it moves,* Aldric said, and his voice had gone quieter. Stripped of the report cadence. Stripped of the careful precision of his disappointment. *It looks as though the light is trying to become something. Not a quest. Not a threat. Only a shape the world has not decided on yet.*

Maren stood in the stream and the cold ran over his boots and the quest-light turned in the pool and for a moment neither of them said anything. Not the loaded silence of disagreement or the pointed silence of withholding. Just quiet. The con man and the dead paladin looking at the same thing from opposite sides of the same skull, and the thing being, for once, not a problem to solve or a danger to assess or a failure to name.

The light caught on a wet seam of stone and flared. Warmth brushed his face, brief as candle heat when somebody opened a door.

*It is beautiful,* Aldric said.

He did not qualify it. He did not take it back.

Vess said nothing. Whether she was looking through Maren's eyes at all, he did not know. Her silence was neither agreement nor contempt. It was the silence of someone letting another person have a private moment and not stepping into it.

Maren stayed there until his feet went numb. Upstream, Derry filled the skins, leather gulping water, then the slap of the last drops shaken off before the ties were pulled shut. Ordinary sounds. The sounds of a man doing a small job well because it was the job in front of him.

He looked down at his hands. The stream had gone pink around the raw skin by his thumbnail where he had scrubbed too hard before dawn, no more than a faint cloud thinning at once into clear water. Nothing dramatic. Nothing enough to notice if he had not been staring. The current worried at every small jump in his fingers until the tremor blurred into the stream's movement and became something outside him.

He bent, scooped water to his mouth, and drank. It tasted like stone, cold, and the faint sweet chemical edge of whatever quest-light left behind when it sat in water long enough. He drank again. His throat opened for the water. It had shut against the bread. It was not food, but it was something going in instead of everything coming up, and his body took it with the desperate gratitude of a system that had been locked for a full day.

The first swallow hit empty hard. His stomach cramped around it, considered revolt, then let it stay. The second went easier. By the third he could feel the water settling into him in layers, cold in his chest, colder lower down, something plain and necessary reentering a body that had spent the last day treating every offer like a threat. He stood there breathing through the ache and waited to see if it would all come back up. It did not.

When he walked back to where they had been sitting, Derry was repacking the bag. The bread was still on the ground where Maren had left it. Derry looked at it, looked at Maren, and put it away without comment.

"East, I think," Maren said. "Off the road. Vess says there's better cover."

Derry nodded. He did not ask who Vess was. He had stopped asking about the voices after the second day. It worked like a neighbor's dog that barked all night, not acceptance, just the flat knowledge that asking did not change anything.

*Acceptable,* Vess said.

*I did not agree to east,* Aldric said.

*You do not have legs,* Vess said. *You agreed when he did.*

They walked east. The stream fell behind them. Derry talked beside him about nothing urgent, about the way Garron sharpened a scythe wrong every harvest and argued with anyone who told him so, about a gate hinge that would freeze solid come winter if nobody saw to it first, about whether the weather had enough bite in it yet to turn the leaves early. Maren listened to Derry's voice and the leather creak of the water skins and the dry hiss of weeds against their legs. He tucked his right hand against his thigh and kept walking.


# Chapter 7

Silt smelled like tallow and horse piss, which made it the largest settlement Maren had entered in six days.

The market town sat half a mile off the main road, down a packed-earth track rutted by cart wheels and scored with scorch lines from some hero who'd apparently discharged a fire-class ability while doing their shopping. The buildings were wood and daub, taller than Fenhold's cottages, two stories in places, leaning toward each other across narrow lanes like old men sharing a fence post. Late-summer sun pressed the smells flat against the ground: a tanner's vat somewhere behind the square, meat turning on a spit outside an inn whose sign had been charred off by quest-light residue, chickens making problems for a boy who clearly had opinions about better uses for his afternoon.

*Three hero-class signatures within four hundred yards,* Aldric said. *None currently targeting. The bounty overlay remains standard visibility. They would need to search for it specifically.*

*Two at the inn, one at the far stall,* Vess added. *The one at the stall is drunk. Afternoon drunk. Not a threat unless you fall on her.*

"Fine," Maren said, to no one visible.

Derry winced. "You've got to stop doing that."

"Doing what?"

"Answering them out loud. In public." He wiped sweat from his forehead with the heel of his hand. His shirt was already dark under the arms, and there was a mended tear at the shoulder, Lenna's work, tight small stitches, that he kept brushing at with his fingers, a gesture Maren had seen a hundred times on the road and never once in Fenhold. "People notice, Maren."

"Right. Yeah." Maren kept his voice low, moving between market stalls where a woman sold bruised apples and another sold linen that had clearly been curtains in a previous life.

The sin-eater. That was why they were here. If anyone could explain what the fragments were doing, what the thing Aldric wouldn't name was becoming, it was someone who'd spent a lifetime doing the job he'd faked.

He asked the apple seller. "The sin-eater. The woman who works the contamination routes. Where do I find her?"

The woman gave him a look he recognized, mild distaste, mild discomfort, the expression people wore when reminded that the toilet needed cleaning. "Thessa? She's out at the Vonn farmstead. Clearing contamination, if she hasn't finished already. Back past the stream, left at the dead oak." She picked up a bruised apple and turned it in her hands, looking for a side worth showing. "She works fast. You wanting a clearing done?"

"Something like that."

*The Vonn farmstead registered moderate contamination density this morning,* Aldric said. *If she has been working since dawn, the field should be safer than it was.*

*Handled,* Vess said.

*I was providing context.*

*You were providing the same thing with more words around it.*

They found the dead oak, a massive trunk split by old lightning, blackened on one side, the other still pushing out leaves as if half a tree was enough. Past it, a track led down to flatlands where a farmstead sat behind a low stone wall, barley fields half-harvested beyond, the stubble golden in afternoon light. Quest-light residue pooled in the low weeds along the track, pale and faintly warm, sitting in the ditches the way it always did near settlements. Maren had walked past pools like these his whole life. After eight days of Aldric reading the data overlaid on every surface, they did not look the same. They had depth. They had information he couldn't read. They had consequences he'd spent half his life ignoring.

"Maybe we should talk about what to tell her," Derry said. He'd been reaching for the back of his neck with his farmer's hand, a gesture that left the skin there red and rubbed. "How much does she need to know? Because if she finds out about the whole thing, all of it..."

"She's a sin-eater, Derry. A real one. She's going to see."

"Right, but can't we just maybe start with the fragments? Without the rest of it? Just say there was an accident, and..."

"Yeah. I'll handle it."

He wouldn't handle it. He already knew that. The same instinct that could read Derry's discomfort, the apple seller's distaste, every family who'd ever hired him, that instinct told him that a woman who'd spent thirty years doing the real work would strip any shaped truth he offered the way she stripped contamination from soil. She'd strip it because stripping was what she did.

Derry was quiet after that, which was worse than his talking. When Derry went quiet, it meant he was thinking about Pell, and when he thought about Pell, the fact that he'd left her sat in his face like something visible, the jaw set, the eyes down, the fingers working at the mended shoulder of his shirt.

Someone was working in the nearest field.

Maren saw her from fifty yards. Tall, angular, grey hair cut short enough that it didn't move in the breeze. She was kneeling in the dirt between stubble rows, her hands pressed flat against the ground, and even from this distance the staining showed, dark and mottled, the color of old tea or dried blood, worked so deep into the skin it had become permanent. She'd been doing this long enough that the work had marked her.

He looked at his own hands. Clean.

*She is performing a second-stage residual extraction,* Aldric said, and his voice had shifted, lower and more careful, the register he used for things that earned his respect. *The contamination density around this farmstead has dropped significantly since we arrived. She is efficient.*

*She's good,* Vess said.

Thessa stood without brushing the dirt from her knees. She looked at Maren and Derry at the edge of her field. Then her eyes narrowed. She tipped her head a fraction, listening past the wind in the barley.

"Stay where you are," she said.

She walked toward them without haste, each foot landing flat and deliberate, testing the ground by habit. Up close, the staining went past her wrists in mottled patterns that looked burned in rather than absorbed. Her face was weathered deep, lines cut around her eyes and mouth, and the tiredness in it didn't look temporary. It had gone into the bones, permanent as the staining on her hands.

She stopped six feet from Maren. Her nostrils flared once.

"What are you carrying?"

Maren had rehearsed something on the walk over, a version of the truth carefully shaped to hide the worst parts. Something about an unusual consumption, fragments he couldn't explain. A story with the bones of honesty and the flesh of misdirection, the kind he'd been constructing since he was seventeen.

Thessa held up one stained hand. "Don't bother."

She stepped closer. Her eyes moved over him, not his face but his chest, his hands, the air around him, reading something he couldn't perceive. The hum was louder here than it had been on the road. Or maybe a real sin-eater's proximity made things vibrate differently.

"Two fragments," she said. "Paladin-class and shadow-class. Opposed. Lodged. Not dissolving." She looked at his hands again. Clean hands. "And something underneath them. Something that doesn't fit."

Her gaze cut past him to the drainage ditch that bordered the west strip of barley. "Walk."

She turned before he answered. Maren followed because the alternative was standing in a field knowing that a woman who'd spent three seconds assessing him had already learned more about his condition than he'd managed in eight days of living with it. Derry followed two steps behind, fingers finding the mended tear at his shoulder again.

Thessa stopped where the field dipped toward the ditch. The smell changed there. Wet earth. Cut barley. The faint sweet chemical edge of contamination sitting in water and going still. A clay bowl rested in the weeds beside a row of cut stalks. Its rim was dark with use. A line of disturbed soil ran from the ditch into the field, not straight but wavering, the way spilled oil wavers when it soaks into cloth.

"Tell me where it ends," Thessa said.

Maren looked at the ditch, then at the barley. The worst of it seemed obvious enough. Two rows had gone pale at the heads, not dead but wrong, thinner than the rest. Quest-light residue clung low over the water and licked at the roots where the bank had slumped.

"There," he said, pointing at the pale strip. "Maybe two rows in."

"Maybe." Thessa did not look where he pointed. "Why."

"Because that's where it's showing."

She squatted and drove two stained fingers into the dirt a full yard past where he'd marked. When she pulled them up, the soil came with them in a dark wet clot. She broke it open with her thumb. Inside, the roots were threaded with a slick grey shine too dull to be quest-light and too clean to be mud.

"Because that's where it's showing," she repeated. "Surface lies. Structural work starts under what still looks fine." She held the root mass up at his chest. "If I cut only what you pointed at, autumn rain pulls this farther downslope, into the ditch, into the trough, into the well lip. Then I get to come back next month and tell Vonn why his cow won't keep feed and his daughter's gums won't stop bleeding."

Maren stared at the roots. They smelled almost like nothing. Damp dirt, old metal, a sweetness underneath that only arrived if he inhaled hard enough to make the back of his nose sting.

"I can't smell it," he said.

"Exactly."

She dropped the roots into the bowl and wiped her fingers together over it. Grey moisture streaked the clay.

"What bowl do you start with?" she asked.

He looked at the bowl in the weeds. Looked back at her. "The active one."

Her mouth flattened. "Assessment first. Then residual. This is not active anymore. Active would have burned the ditch grass white and put both of us on the ground if we'd walked in blind." She pointed at the field. "What are you looking at."

He swallowed. "Barley."

"I'm asking what matters."

He looked again because there was apparently a correct answer in front of him and he had been wrong twice in less than a minute. Wind moved over the heads in one direction. Near the ditch, two patches moved late, a drag in the stalks as if something under them held on a beat longer than it should. The soil there stayed darker. One stretch of bank had crusted white where the water line should have dried brown.

"The ground by the ditch holds wet longer," he said.

"Yes."

"The left patch is slower in the wind."

"Because the roots are fouled. What else."

He had nothing. A man who'd been paid to know this for half his life, and what he had was barley, ditch, damp.

Thessa bent, took a stalk from the pale strip, and split it with her thumbnail. The pith inside had gone grey-green instead of cream.

"That's what the grain comes up on when you miss it," she said. "If you catch it at this stage, you lose a strip. If you miss it until seed set, you lose the sack, then the yard where you store the sack, then whatever rat gets into the torn corner, then the cat that eats the rat, then the child who milks the goat that drank ditch water because the trough looked clear on top."

A voice carried across the field. "Mistress Thessa?"

A man at the far end of the barley had straightened from binding sheaves. Shirt off, hat in one hand, ribs showing. He looked toward the ditch instead of toward Maren, the way people looked toward whoever actually did the work.

"Can we cut the north strip tomorrow," he called, "or am I losing that too?"

"Not until I finish the well lip," Thessa said.

He scrubbed a hand over his mouth. "Damn."

"Better a strip than the whole field."

He nodded once and bent back to his work. No argument. No bargaining. Just the tired acceptance of someone who knew the crop and knew she knew it better.

Thessa looked at Maren. "What would you have told him."

Maren pictured himself in this field a month ago, bowl in hand, grave face fixed in place, Derry at his shoulder feeding him cues he didn't understand. He knew exactly what he would have said because he'd said versions of it in villages all his adult life. Clear enough. Watch the ditch for a day or two. Boil the water. Keep the children out of the strip. Say it slow, say it solemn, make the bowl look important, take the coins, leave before anyone noticed the barley still wrong.

"That it was handled," he said.

"Of course." Thessa straightened. "Come on."

She picked up the bowl and led them toward the house at the edge of the track. Derry did not speak. His boots kept catching in the ruts where the cart had cut down after the last rain. Once he nearly went to a knee and caught himself on nothing.

Halfway to the house Thessa stopped again, crouched by a patch of nettles, and scraped two fingers along the stone edging of the well run. The stain on the stone was so faint Maren would have called it old weathering and kept walking.

"There's the real trouble," she said. "Field you can cut back. Water you have to chase." She rose. "You would never have seen that."

"No."

"I know."

They went the rest of the way in silence.

---

Thessa's house sat where Silt thinned into scrub and the track met open ground. One room below, a loft above, and it smelled like the work, that ozone-sweet contamination smell, but older, layered into the walls and the wooden floor and the single table where ritual tools sat in a careful row. Three clay bowls, each stained darker than the last. A grinding stone with a worn channel down its center. Glass bottles holding liquids Maren couldn't identify, amber, grey-green, one the color of a deep bruise. The floor in front of the cold hearth was worn smooth, the wood lighter than the rest, a rectangle of use where someone had knelt thousands of times.

"Sit." She pointed at a bench against the wall. Maren sat. Derry stayed by the door, gripping the frame with both hands, his breathing shallow and quick.

Thessa set the field bowl apart from the others and wiped her hands on a rag already stained beyond saving. Then she pulled a stool to the center of the room and sat facing Maren. Afternoon light through the single window caught the grey in her hair.

"Show me your hands."

Maren held them out, palms up. Clean. Unmarked. The skin was rough from travel and from years of carrying equipment, but none of the mottled staining that covered Thessa's to the wrists. Nothing that showed the work had ever touched him.

She held one of her own hands beside his, palm to palm without contact. The contrast didn't need explanation, hers dark with thirty years of absorbed contamination, his showing nothing but the ordinary calluses of a man who'd carried a prop and walked a lot. She dropped his hand.

"Where's your equipment?"

"I've got a bowl. Ceramic. In my pack."

"One bowl."

"Yeah."

She glanced at her table, three bowls, darkest to lightest, each in its place. "Active contamination, residual work, structural assessment. Different grades need different vessels."

Maren said nothing. There was nothing to say that wouldn't make it worse.

"How long have you been working?"

"Eleven years."

"Where."

"Fenhold, and the villages around it. Dunmore, Cress, the settlements along the river road."

"I know the area." Her voice flattened. "I've worked the eastern routes for thirty years. Including the overflow from certified sin-eaters who supposedly handled their zones." She studied him. "Walk me through a field death in summer. Start where you put your knees."

This was the question he could almost answer. He'd performed the fake version so many times the order was muscle memory, the bowl placement, the kneeling posture, the words spoken in the right cadence. "You set the bowl at the body's head.
