Kneel at the left side. The opening invocation, standard form, three repetitions. Then the hand positions, flat against the contamination line. The consumption pass..."

"Which hand?"

"What?"

"Which hand opens the containment seal before the consumption pass?"

He didn't know. He'd never known. The hand positions in his fake ritual were theatrical. They looked right because he'd studied what sin-eaters looked like from the outside, not what they did. "The right."

"Left." Thessa's voice closed like a door. "The left hand opens. The right holds the seal. Every sin-eater learns this in the first week because getting it backward channels contamination through the dominant hand and into the body instead of the bowl."

"I haven't been channeling at all."

The words were out before he could bite them back. His mouth went dry on the last one. Derry made a sound by the door, small and strangled.

Thessa went very still.

"Show me a containment pass."

Nothing. His hands stayed on his knees.

"A residual clear."

Nothing.

She waited one beat longer than she had in the field, maybe because she wanted to see if shame could make knowledge appear where there'd never been any. It didn't.

She looked at the table. Three bowls. Three things even the names of which he had learned in the wrong order, standing in a ditch while she did real work five minutes earlier.

She looked at his clean hands again, as if they were evidence she kept having to re-examine and each time found the same absence.

Maren looked at the floor. At the rectangle worn into the wood by genuine practice. His own knees had worn nothing anywhere, left no mark on anything. All those years of kneeling beside bodies and the only evidence was clean hands and a ceramic bowl that had never held anything real.

"You're not a sin-eater," Thessa said.

"No."

"You never were."

"No."

*She is correct,* Aldric said.

"How many rituals."

Maren wet his mouth. "More than a hundred."

"And zero consumptions. Every family that paid you, every farmer who died in his field and hired you to clear the ground before the contamination spoiled the crop, you took the money, performed the gestures, and left the contamination where it was."

"Yes."

She stood. Walked to the table and picked up the darkest bowl, the one that looked like it had absorbed thirty years of practice. Turned it once in her stained hands.

"One season of uncleared residue," she said. "Yield drops by a third." She set the bowl down. "Two seasons, the barley comes up thin. Grey rot in the wheat. You saw that out there and still called the strip by eye instead of the roots."

"Thessa, I didn't know."

"Yes." She lined the bowl back with the other two. "That's the problem."

Her voice did not rise. It did not need to. She leaned one hand on the table and looked at him the way she'd looked at the field, as a working problem that would get worse if she guessed wrong.

"Five years and the soil holds it like wood holds smoke," she said. "Can't plant. Can't graze. Water takes it farther. Flour carries it into a whole house. A woman kneads bread on a table she thought was clean and then wonders why the baby won't settle."

Maren's fingers had found the fabric of his trousers and were digging in, knuckles white against the rough bench. A splinter caught at the seam above his knee. He counted the bottles on the table. Six. Amber, grey-green, bruise-purple, three others whose colors he couldn't name.

Thessa sat back on her stool.

"You worked Dunmore."

"Yes."

"When."

"Three, maybe four years ago."

"I cleared Dunmore two years ago. Routine maintenance, supposedly." She was still for a moment. "The contamination had gone structural. Into the water table, into the foundations. People sleeping in it."

She waited. Maren said nothing.

"There's a girl in Dunmore. Maret. Nine years old. Her hands shake so badly her mother has to feed her."

Six bottles. Splinter in the bench. The worn rectangle on the floor.

"Her exposure started before she was born," Thessa said. "Her mother carried her in contaminated soil."

Derry had slid down the doorframe to the floor. Not dramatically. His legs just stopped holding, and he sat on the threshold with his boots on the dirt outside and his back against the wood and his face the color of old paste. He'd been in Dunmore. He'd watched those rituals. He'd fed Maren the System details that made the performance look real, and afterward they'd split the coins and gone to the inn.

"You worked Cress," Thessa said.

"Twice. Maybe three times."

"Holloway farm. Six acres lost, twenty years of work. The boy has tremors." She paused. "Fenhold."

Maren's jaw set.

"A farmer died in Fenhold this season. Contamination exposure. I hadn't reached the eastern route yet." She looked at him. "How long has the contamination been building in that village?"

"Since I was seventeen."

"Eleven harvests." She was quiet for a beat. "That farmer. Did you do his ritual?"

"Yes."

"What happened."

"Something answered." The words scraped his throat. "A fragment. A paladin. That's why I'm here. That's why I came to find you."

Thessa kept looking at him, from his mouth to his hands to the place under his ribs where the fragments sat.

"You came to me," she said, "because in eleven years of fraud, you finally ate something real, and now you're scared."

"Yes."

She held his gaze another moment, then shifted it to Derry on the floor.

"And you," she said. "You knew."

Derry's mouth opened and shut. "I knew he wasn't real."

"Did you know what the money was for."

His eyes squeezed closed. "We told ourselves it was burial work. Comfort, I guess. Saying the words so people had something, so the family wasn't just standing there with a body and nothing."

"Comfort doesn't pull contamination out of a well."

"No."

She did not press. She turned back to Maren.

"The worst part isn't that you lied," she said. "People lie. Half the certified idiots on the western routes lie about what they can clear. The worst part is that you were cheap enough that villages used you instead of waiting for somebody real, and cheap enough becomes local custom faster than rot. One fraud in the right place teaches three towns that solemn words are good enough if the bowl looks old."

Maren stared at the bruise-colored bottle until the glass doubled.

"I charged twelve coppers," he said, because that was what his mouth chose to do when the rest of him had gone bad. "Sometimes less if they fed us."

"That's the price that breaks a route," Thessa said. "Cheap enough that villages stop waiting."

Derry made a sound that might have been laughter if laughter had a corpse in its throat.

Maren remembered a widow in Cress paying with a chipped cup of coins and two eggs because she didn't have the full fee. Derry had said take them, we'll eat the difference, and Maren had laughed because there was a joke somewhere in the shape of it and he was still the kind of man who looked for jokes near bodies. He'd carried the eggs in his pocket till one cracked warm against his thigh. He'd been annoyed by the mess. The house behind him had probably still been dirty with the thing he'd promised to take.

---

The silence sat in the room with the contamination smell. Maren worried the splinter over his knee with one thumb and kept his eyes on the bruise-colored bottle. Usually he'd have filled a gap like this. He had nothing.

"Help me," Maren said. "Teach me. If I can learn to actually consume, do the real work, maybe I can stabilize the fragments, or contain the thing underneath them."

"No."

"The thing underneath them, the unclassified. You said you've heard of it. If you know what it is, how to..."

"I said no." Thessa folded her arms. "The thing underneath your fragments is growing. I can feel it from here."

"Then help me stop it from widening."

She unfolded her arms. "If I open you with genuine technique and that thing widens, it takes everything within a mile of this house. My territory. Thirty years of work."

"I'm not asking you to consume me. Just the basics. The real sequence. The hand positions."

"If I had four trained hands in this room and three boundary bowls already working, I'd consider putting you on the floor and cutting for the thing under the fragments before it roots any deeper." She looked at the table. The bowls. The bottles. The arithmetic of risk. "I don't. So no."

"Then what am I supposed to do?"

"Keep moving."

"Where."

Thessa's mouth tightened. "I've read two accounts of what you're carrying. One from my teacher's teacher. One from a text so old it fell apart when I opened it. Neither one described how to live with it. They described what was left after." She stood and walked to the door. "You don't have time for hand positions."

"What does that mean?"

She opened the door. Late-afternoon light fell across the threshold.

"Unclassified entities don't last," she said. "The System treats them as errors. Not threats. Errors." She gripped the doorframe, the staining on her hands dark against the pale wood. "Sixty years ago. A woman in the northern territories. Opposed fragments, accidental consumption. Same as you."

*Go on,* Aldric said, very quietly.

"The System reclassified the ground around her. Threat zone. Contamination spiked, creatures spawned from the nearest dungeon mouths, heroes converged." She wasn't looking at Maren. "The heroes weren't there for her. They were responding to the zone."

"She died," Maren said.

"Nothing remained of the host." Thessa let go of the doorframe. "The System didn't kill her directly. It changed the ground until it couldn't hold people and let the rest happen on its own."

The room was very still. Derry had pulled himself back to standing but he was pressed against the wall by the door, his mouth working around words that wouldn't form.

"How long?" Maren asked. "From when the thing formed to the correction."

"The text didn't say. Weeks. Maybe months." Thessa stepped back from the doorway. "East gets you Harrow. North from Harrow gets you the Crook."

"The Crook?" Derry said. His voice caught on the word. "What's the Crook?"

"A contamination camp in a valley between dungeon mouths. Enough active sin-eaters to look at you and decide whether you're salvageable or whether you need to be cut open before the ground starts changing."

"Then tell them we're coming," Maren said.

"No."

"You just said..."

"I named a direction. I didn't send a letter." Her hand stayed on the doorframe. "I'm not putting my name on you. If heroes turn your pack over and find a written lead, I deliver your bounty to the last working camp in this region. If I send word ahead, they lower their guard for a man carrying a correction risk I don't understand. I'm not doing either."

"If you die before Harrow, that's the road deciding for me," she said. "If you reach the Crook, they can look at you with their own eyes and choose."

Derry swallowed. "Harrow's a hero town."

"Yes," Thessa said. "Which means caravans move, roads are marked, and northbound supply wagons leave at dawn. If you go, that's your route."

Maren stood because standing was something he could still do. His legs held.

"Find the road with contamination carts and ash barrels," Thessa said. "Stay on it. Don't ask after sin-eaters in the square. Don't say my name. If you reach the Crook, they will smell you before you finish your first sentence."

He moved toward the door.

"Maret's grandmother grew up on the same soil," Thessa said. "No tremors."

Maren stopped with one hand on the frame. The wood was rough under his palm. There was a nail head there worked nearly smooth by years of fingers.

"Thessa," he said, and had no second half.

"Go," she said.

Maren walked out into the street. Derry followed, shoulders hunched, the mended tear at his shoulder pulling where his posture strained the stitching. Behind them, Thessa's door closed. Not slammed, because Thessa didn't slam anything. Just shut.

They crossed the emptying market square while the apple seller packed bruised fruit into a crate and the linen woman folded her table. The light had gone gold and low. The hum under everything was the same as always. Maren had just never known what it was holding.

He kept seeing the field before he saw the table. The grey shine on the roots. The strip of barley that looked mostly fine until Thessa broke it open. The farmer at the far end of the row waiting for a real answer, not a performance. Then the three bowls returned, darkest to lightest. Active. Residual. Structural. Three plain trade words, and he'd spent half his life not knowing one of them while families watched him kneel and believed the ground would stop hurting them if he looked solemn enough.

*She is right about the correction timeline,* Aldric said. His voice had lost its usual edge. *The Order's records described similar patterns. Territories destabilized, hosts removed. The corrections were always territorial. I have no reason to doubt her account.*

*How long do we have?* Vess asked.

*I do not know. The records described outcomes, not intervals.*

*Then we assume weeks,* Vess said. *And we move.*

At the edge of Silt, the track met the main road east. One set of wagon ruts was streaked with old grey ash, and the ditch beside it carried the stale sweet smell of contamination work. Thessa's road. Maren stopped long enough to look at his hands. Still clean. The only marks on them were calluses from carrying a bowl that had never done anything.

Derry dragged both hands over his face. "Harrow first, then north."

"Yeah."

He hitched his pack too high on his shoulders and started down the ash-marked road without waiting for Maren. The distance opened quick. Five steps, then more.

Behind them, Silt's chimney smoke thinned into the late-summer sky. Ahead, the road ran east under the last of the light. Maren followed Derry toward Harrow, with the ash-streaked ruts underfoot and the thing under his ribs waiting.


# Chapter 8

The road past Silt smelled different. Fresh contamination had a sharp ozone-sweetness that bit the back of Maren's nose. This was older, settled into the dirt like grease in a cookpot. Decay with a sweetness underneath, and under that a mineral flatness that stuck to the roof of his mouth. He breathed through it because there was no alternative. The road went forward and the smell went with it.

Scorch marks darkened the road stones every few hundred paces now. Some were old enough that grass had crept into the blackened edges. Others were fresh, the surface still glossy, still faintly warm underfoot in the afternoon heat. Heroes had been through here. Were through here regularly, if the layering was anything to go by. Maren stepped around the marks without thinking about it, the way you step around puddles. The hum was louder out here, a low steady drone that sat behind his teeth and would not leave.

Derry walked ten paces ahead, his pack hitched too high on his shoulders. He had been adjusting the straps since they left the outskirts of Silt, pulling at the leather, letting it go, pulling again.

He was probably right not to start talking yet.

*Your pace has dropped,* Aldric said. *We are losing daylight.*

Maren did not answer. He was watching the quest-light in the ditch, a thin sheet of pale luminescence pooled in the low ground the way rainwater pooled after a storm, except rainwater did not glow and was not warm. It lay there with a soft golden shimmer, like light caught in shallow water. Beautiful if you did not know what it meant.

He had been thinking about Thessa. The insults had landed where they belonged, heavy and specific and earned. Maret with both hands around a cup she still could not hold steady. A boy's spoon rattling against the rim because his wrists would not stop shaking. That weight sat where it sat. He was not going to put it down.

The other part, though. The warning. Unclassified entities did not last. The System corrected them. Sixty years since the last one, and nothing left of the host. Thessa had said it and left it sitting between them.

Which meant whatever he was carrying was on the same clock he was.

"Vess."

No response. That was standard. She treated the first use of her name as a notification, not a summons. She would respond when she was ready, or when the silence had lasted long enough to count as acknowledgment.

"The contamination in that hollow. The quest that brought you in."

*What about it.*

"What was it? The quest itself. Before it pooled. Before I touched it."

The pause lasted six steps. Maren counted them. Vess's pauses were measured things, the silence of someone deciding how much information to declassify.

*A bounty quest,* she said. *Anchored to the holdings of a minor lord. Torren Blackhall. He held land east of the Silt road, maybe two days' walk from where your hollow was. He had purchased quest access for the territory around his estate.*

"Purchased."

*Landholders can buy quest generation for their territory. The System creates quests, heroes come through, spend money, generate trade. Blackhall's holdings were close enough to the dungeon approaches that the purchase was cheap. Standard practice for minor nobility.*

Maren did not know that. He had spent so long inside a System costume that half the basic mechanics were still news to him. The fraud had been thorough enough that the gaps were showing up now like cracks in plaster, everywhere and all at once.

"So the quest was his. Generated on his land. And it went bad?"

*It didn't go bad. He used it.*

"Used it how?"

*There were tenant villages on the eastern edge of his holdings. Forty or fifty families, mostly agricultural labor. Timber workers, some of them. They had disputed his cutting rights for two growing seasons. There was a boundary disagreement. Whose forest, whose trees, whose profit. The kind of thing that goes to a magistrate in most holdings. Blackhall did not take it to a magistrate.*

Derry had stopped ahead, waiting where the road bent around a stand of scrub oak. His silhouette was a stocky question mark against the yellowing sky.

"You coming?" he called.

"Yeah. Just give me a minute."

"You're talking to them again."

"Yeah."

Derry nodded. He sat down on a flat stone by the road's edge and pulled his water skin from his pack. He drank, wiped his mouth, drank again. He did not ask what the voices were saying. He had stopped asking after Silt.

Maren kept walking, slower now, closing the gap between them but not all the way. Close enough that Derry could see him. Far enough that talking to himself looked like mumbling instead of conversation.

"Blackhall killed them," he said to Vess. Not a question.

*He hired mercenaries. A company of eight. They went through the eastern villages over three nights. The quest activity on his land generated enough System noise to cover the contamination signatures of what happened. Bounty notifications, threat assessments, the normal chatter of an active zone. Anyone checking the area afterward would read the deaths as quest casualties. Dungeon-adjacent attrition. Common enough that no one investigates.*

Vess said it in the same flat voice she used for distances and approach routes. No emphasis. No tremor. She could have been describing weather.

"Fifty families."

*Approximately. The contract did not specify an exact count. I was not hired for the aftermath. I was hired for Blackhall.*

"The Nightguild."

*Yes.*

"You were going to kill him."

*I was going to complete the contract. A client in one of the surviving settlements had reached the guild through the standard channels. Payment was adequate. The target was accessible. I had his schedule within a week of deployment. I had the estate mapped in ten days. The purchased quest gave me operational cover. I could move through the contamination zone without triggering secondary System alerts because the area was already noisy with quest activity. The same cover Blackhall used to hide the killing would have hidden me on the way in and on the way out.*

There was a hitch in the cadence Maren could not place. Vess's voice stayed flat, but the sentence bent in the middle as if one support held and the next had rotted through.

"What happened?"

*I was inside the quest's operational radius. Moving through the contamination perimeter at the edge of the estate. Two days from completion. The approach was clean. I had checked my route twice. Then I was inside you. The interval between those two states isn't present. I don't remember the consumption.*

"A gap."

*The memory isn't degraded. It's absent. The distinction matters. Degraded memory is fog. I had a head injury at fifteen, lost three weeks of training, and those weeks feel like looking through dirty glass. The details are present but distorted. This isn't that. This is a room that has been emptied.*

Maren reached the bend in the road. Derry was still sitting on his stone, water skin balanced on his knee, watching a beetle navigate the road dust between his boots. He glanced up when Maren stopped.

"Sausage?" Derry said. He held up a leathery coil. "Got it in Silt. Vendor charged too much but it's not bad if you pull the casing off."

"Maybe later."

"You look like you're about to be sick."

"I'm fine."

"That's what you said before the ditch outside Fenhold."

"Different ditch."

Derry did not push. He put the sausage back in his pack and went back to watching the beetle. It had reached a scorch mark and was navigating the glossy black edge with the careful deliberation of an insect that did not understand the obstacle but was going to get past it anyway.

Maren sat down on the road's edge, close enough to Derry that the silence between them was companionable instead of strained. The quest-light in the ditch beside him glowed faintly. The hum droned. The sun was low enough that the scrub oak cast long shadows across the road, and in the shadows the scorch marks were invisible, which was a small stupid mercy.

"Vess."

*Yes.*

"You had a sister."

The silence that followed snagged hard. It had weight in it, real resistance. Vess had hit a locked place and found no way around it.

He had not planned to say it. It came from a conversation days earlier, one of the fragments' arguments, Vess and Aldric sniping at each other while Maren tried to eat, and she had said one sharp line about the Order of the Veil and then mentioned a sister in the same flat register. One word. Then she moved on. Maren had filed it automatically, the old reflex, detail as currency, and now the word had come out of him like a seed he had been carrying in his cheek.

*I had a sister.* A beat. *Have. Had. The tense is unclear.*

"What was her name?"

The pause stretched past anything Vess usually allowed.

*It starts with an open sound,* she said at last. *Two syllables. I can hear my mouth beginning it. I can't get farther than that.*

Maren's hands were flat on the warm road stone. He pressed them harder against the surface. The grit bit his palms.

*I can give you the distance from Blackhall's bedroom to the servants' staircase,* Vess said. *Fourteen feet. I can give you his steward's name and his breakfast preferences and the rotation schedule for his perimeter guard. I can tell you how long to keep a wire tight after a man stops kicking, because stopping and dead are not the same thing. All of that is here. Complete. My sister's name is not.*

"What do you remember of her?"

Another pause, shorter this time. Vess answered the way she answered route questions, one piece after another.

*A chipped blue cup with one handle. She tapped it against her teeth when she was thinking. Wet hair down the middle of her back after washing. Garlic on her fingers. A cut on the heel of her palm from cleaning river fish with a bad knife. She hated mutton fat and scraped it off with the side of her thumb. I know those things. I know the sound of her walking when she was trying not to wake anyone. The name is gone.*

"Older or younger?"

*Smaller,* Vess said. Then, after a beat that landed harder than the first answer, *I don't know if that means younger.*

The beetle had reached the other side of the scorch mark. Derry was still watching it, seemingly absorbed, but his jaw was set in the way it set when he was pretending not to listen to a thing that frightened him.

"Vess. The massacre. When you talk about the families, the fifty families, you don't sound angry."

*Angry. I know. The contract flagged personal motivation as a risk factor. I accepted it anyway. Below-rate fee. This one was personal. The villages were —*

She stopped hard. The sentence hit a wall and died there.

*I can feel it start,* she said. *The beginning of the feeling is there. Then it cuts off. I took the contract for a reason I can document and can't feel.*

Maren breathed. The contamination smell was in his mouth and in his throat and the hum was steady and the quest-light in the ditch glowed its soft indifferent gold.

He had spent days listening for what Vess could do in a fight. It took this to hear what had been done to her.

*I have them too,* Aldric said. Quiet. Formal. The voice of a man filing a report he did not want to file. *The gaps.*

---

They camped in a hollow off the road where the scrub oak thinned enough to see the sky. Derry built the fire, small and careful, the fire of a man who had been sleeping outdoors for a week and had gotten efficient about it. He cut sausage with his belt knife, ate slowly, stared at the flames with the particular distance of someone whose thoughts were two hundred miles away and centered on a girl who was learning to count.

He offered Maren a piece. Maren took it. The casing was tough and the meat was too salty and it was fine.

"Tomorrow's worse," Derry said. "More patrols. Some of the roads have checkpoints, or they used to. My cousin went through here four years ago and said there were heroes just sitting at the crossroads, scanning everyone who passed."

"Scanning."

"Checking profiles. Class, level, party affiliation. The whole thing. And you've got"

"Nothing."

"I was going to say a situation." Derry cut another piece of sausage and inspected it in the firelight. "You've got a situation."

"I've got a situation."

"Right. So tomorrow, you're the cousin. Quiet. Head injury. I can talk for both of us if I have to. Lenna always said I was a bad liar but honestly I think I'm all right when it's, you know. When it matters."

"You're a terrible liar."

"Yeah." He almost smiled. "Yeah, I know."

He kept turning the knife in his hand after that, wiping grease off one side and then the other even though there was no reason for it.

"Pell's going to turn three next week," he said. "Lenna wrote it on the wall by the stove before harvest started because Pell keeps asking when she's big. She holds up two fingers and says three. Wrong every time. Same wrong every time." He glanced at Maren and then back at the fire. "I keep seeing her hand. Not even her, half the time. Just the hand."

Maren chewed the sausage and swallowed. It felt dry all the way down.

"You'll get back," he said.

"Maybe." Derry shrugged one shoulder. "I know. Maybe's what we've got. Still. I keep thinking, if I miss it because I decided to help you pretend you hit your head at a checkpoint, that's going to be a real stupid story to bring home."

"It is."

Derry let out a breath through his nose. "Good. Just checking you were still able to say obvious things."

Brush shifted in the scrub oak. Probably an animal. Probably wind. Vess's tactical assessment ran through Maren in a brief cold line, direction, distance, threat probability, no pursuit pattern, and then it was gone. Automatic. A reflex from a woman who could not reach her own anger but could still map an ambush in the dark.

They sat without talking. Derry finished his sausage, wiped his knife on his trousers, folded it, and put it in his pocket. He leaned back against his pack and closed his eyes. Not sleeping yet. Maren could tell from his breathing. He was only making the shape of sleep and waiting for his body to believe it.

"Aldric."

*Yes.*

"The gaps."

The pause was long enough that Maren almost spoke again. But Aldric's pauses were construction. He built the answer to his own specifications before he released it.

*My service record is complete,* he said. *Fourteen years with the Order of the Veil. I can provide the name of every member of my cohort, every deployment, every site investigation. My training in System theory is intact. The research Commander Dellan authorized, the investigation into structural anomalies in the System's deeper architecture, I remember the commission, the scope, the findings.*

"But."

*I remember the dungeon. The one where I died. I remember the briefing, such as it was. The approach march. The entry. The first chamber. Standard layout. Moderate contamination. Residual quest activity from a previous clearing. I remember the hum there. It was different. Lower, more sustained. A harmonic I had not heard in any previous site.*

He paused again. When he continued, his syntax was tighter, more clipped, the formal architecture drawing in on itself.

*I remember reaching the second chamber. The wall. Then the stone ceased to be stone and the System's architecture showed through. Bare structure beneath the interface. The frame under the overlaid data. I remember seeing it. I remember that I understood the pattern in front of me. I remember the understanding arriving, the shape of it, the magnitude. It was —*

He stopped.

Aldric completed every sentence. It was a defining feature of his voice, as fixed as Vess's flat delivery or Derry's trailing qualifications. When Aldric started a sentence, it ended. Properly, grammatically, with the finality of a door closing. That was who he was. A man of complete sentences.

*I do not remember what I understood,* he said. *I have tried. There were structures on the wall. Lines, joints, repeating cuts. I can see the start of the pattern and then it is gone. I can recite the first chamber. Thirty-two feet by twenty-one. Contamination, fragment signatures, all intact. The understanding I reached in the second chamber is gone. The memory has a clean edge. Nothing past it.*

"Can you remember what you were about to say?"

*No. Only the shape of the loss. It is like leaning on a door that opens onto open air.*

Derry's breathing had evened out. He had fallen asleep with his head on his pack and his mouth slightly open. In the firelight his face was slack, younger, scrubbed of the worry that had been tightening it since they left Fenhold. He looked like what he was, a farmer of thirty, who should have been home.

*My death,* Aldric said. The word came out with the same careful precision he used when he was trying not to let fear show. *The understanding I reached, and then my death. Both absent. Both removed with the same precision. If the dungeon collapsed on me, which is what the Order's records state, then the memory of that collapse has been excised. If the collapse is false, then the falsehood reached the record as well.*

*Then the removal is evidence,* Vess said. *Not damage. Not drift. Evidence.*

*Yes.*

"The System," Maren said. Derry did not stir.

The fire popped. Somewhere beyond the hollow, quest-light hummed in the ditch. Maren looked at Derry asleep against his pack, mouth half open, then down at his own hands in the firelight.

"If it starts happening again, you tell me when. Right then."

*That assumes we will recognize it,* Aldric said.

"Fine. When you reach for a memory and there is a hole, you say that."

Vess answered first. *All right.*

Aldric took longer. *Very well.*

The fire was low. Maren put another stick on it, a piece of scrub oak dry enough to catch quickly. The new flames threw shadows across Derry's sleeping face and across Maren's hands, which were rough and dirt-lined and holding the stick a little too tight.

He was carrying two people. Damage had gone through them, maybe deeper than any of them could see, and they were still themselves inside it. Vess with a sister reduced to a blue cup, garlic on the fingers, a cut in the palm, no name. Aldric with a wall full of meaning and a death he could not get back.

The part of his life where they had only been useful had ended without anyone announcing it. Vess was not just the knife in his ribs when a fight started. Aldric was not just the voice reading a world Maren could not see. They were damaged in ways that would keep damaging them if no one bothered to notice. That sat badly in him. It was still true. He was the only witness they had left.

Maren sat with them. The hum droned in the dark, steady and low, and the quest-light in the ditch beyond the hollow reflected the stars, a second sky pooled in the low places of the world. The contamination smell had settled into an almost ignorable layer, the way any constant discomfort became background if you sat with it long enough.

Derry snored once, softly. The sausage he had been saving had slipped from his pack and lay in the dirt near his boot. Maren picked it up, brushed it off, and put it back. A small thing. The kind of thing you did for someone without thinking about it. He had no idea whether small things like that survived whatever happened to the dead in contaminated ground, or whether the System stripped those away first and left only the useful pieces.

He thought about Thessa's warning and the clock he was on. He thought about the correction coming. He thought about the two people inside him, already altered in ways he could see and probably more he could not, and about how little that told him about what a full correction would do. Whether it would peel them down farther. Whether it would take the blue cup and the garlic and the wall and the hum and leave nothing but a few usable instincts rattling around in his skull. Whether Derry, if it came to that, would ever know what had been lost.

*You should sleep,* Vess said.

"Yeah."

*Your body will function at approximately sixty percent capacity tomorrow if you don't.*

"I know."

*Then sleep.*

He did not. He sat with the fire going to coals and the stars turning overhead and the hum in his teeth, and he thought about what it meant to carry people who had been cut and not know the rule behind the cuts. He thought about the System out there in the dark, vast and invisible to him, and about how little it took to disappear inside it. He thought about his hands, which had faked a hundred rituals and killed a man and failed Thessa's test and were now, apparently, the only hands left to do anything for these two people.

He did not sleep. But he stayed.


# Chapter 9

They smelled Harrow before they saw it. Ozone and char, layered thick enough to close Maren's throat while the town was still just a smudge between hills. Underneath was something sweeter, hot stone baking in late summer, and the particular warmth that quest-light left on surfaces after it had pooled long enough. The road had been getting worse for two hours. Scorch marks overlapped on the packed dirt until the whole path looked like the floor of a forge, burn patterns crossing at angles that made no sense until you understood that heroes didn't clean up after themselves. Some of the marks were old, pale gray, scuffed by cart wheels. Others were darker. One was still warm when Maren's boot caught its edge, and he moved his foot off it the way you'd step off a hot coal.

*Thirty-seven,* Aldric said. *Thirty-eight. Thirty-nine. That one is fresh, less than a day old. Someone deployed a class-three fire projection in the middle of a public road.*

"How do you know it's class three?" Maren muttered, barely moving his lips. He'd gotten better at that, the ventriloquism of talking to his own skull. A week of practice, and he could almost pass for a man muttering to himself, which was at least socially acceptable if not dignified.

*The scorch radius. The char depth. The residual heat signature is still readable on the interface. Do you want the full damage assessment or can I trust you to walk around it?*

"I can see a burn mark on the ground. I've been seeing burn marks."

*You have been seeing scorch marks the way a child sees a hearth fire. I am reading you System-verified damage signatures with class attribution, decay rates, and more detail than your feet deserve.*

*Four,* Vess said.

Aldric stopped. Maren waited.

*She does that on purpose,* Aldric said.

*Four heroes ahead. Two at the gate, one on the wall, one buying something from a stall just inside. The gate pair are low-tier, two maybe three. Wall is tier four. The one at the stall is interesting.*

"Interesting how? Interesting meaning they won't bother us, or interesting meaning we turn around right now?"

*Interesting meaning I can't get a clean read at this distance and I don't like not knowing.*

Derry was already sweating. He'd been sweating since they'd spotted the first road marker for Harrow, a charred wooden post with HARROW burned into it in common and a wash of quest-light crawling over the top like wet blue moss. Maren could read the wood. Derry read whatever the light said and went pale, the color draining out of his face and leaving only a thin damp sheen behind.

"Big place," Derry said, which was unnecessary. He wiped his forehead with his sleeve and the sleeve came away dark. "Lot of people, though. That's good, right? More people means it's harder to spot one, harder to pick one person out of a crowd."

"Derry."

"Yeah."

"You're fine."

"I know I'm fine. I'm saying it's good. The crowd thing. That's, yeah."

Derry stopped talking for about fifteen steps, which under these conditions was something like a personal best.

Harrow sat in a shallow bowl between two hills, and the hum hit Maren before he could see the town proper. He'd always heard it, the low vibration that System-heavy areas made, a wasps' nest buried in a wall. In Fenhold it had been background, the kind of sound you stopped noticing the way you stopped noticing your own breathing. Here it was a pressure. It sat behind his sternum and stayed. The air felt denser, like walking into a room where too many people were talking. His ears popped twice on the slope down.

*The interface density is remarkable.* Aldric's tone shifted, less irritation now, more the careful attention of a man cataloguing something he'd only read about in reports. *Quest notifications are layered three deep in some areas. I can barely isolate individual prompts. There, that board ahead, the tall frame by the market square. Do you see it?*

A wooden frame taller than he was stood nailed to a post at the edge of the market square. Things were pinned to it. Papers, maybe. Leather strips. Some kind of thin fabric that moved in the breeze. To him it was a bulletin board outside a village hall, just bigger and busier.

*That is a quest board. There are forty-one active quest postings. Sixteen standard dungeon clearances. Eight contamination reports, and the residue levels listed on some of these are frankly appalling. Nine bounties. Four escort requests. And four that I would classify as recreational.*

"Recreational."

*Someone is offering three gold pieces to recover a cat from a low-tier dungeon mouth. The cat is named Brindlefur. There is an illustration. It appears to be hand-drawn. The cat appears to be wearing a small hat, and I cannot determine whether that is artistic license or an actual hat.*

Vess said nothing, which was its own kind of commentary.

"Three gold for a cat," Maren said.

*The bounty on your head is four thousand gold crowns,* Aldric said. *At three gold per cat, that is one thousand three hundred and thirty-three cats, with one crown left over. In case you needed perspective.*

They passed through the gate without challenge. The two heroes there barely looked at them. One was eating something that dripped grease onto her gauntlet, and the other was arguing with a cart driver about road-damage fees. The argument was loud and specific, involving scorched wheel rims and a dispute over which hero's fire ability had done the actual damage. Maren kept his eyes forward, his pace unremarkable, his mouth shut.

Aldric did not keep his mouth shut.

*Gear shop. Gear shop. Another gear shop. That one specializes in elemental resistances, judging by the signage. Tavern. The sign says "The Cinder" and there are scorch marks on the sign itself, which I suspect is intentional. Tavern. That building has no sign and I have no idea what it is, but there is a significant amount of quest-light pooled against its foundation and someone has drawn a rude picture on the wall in soot.*

The quest-light was the only part Maren could verify independently. It gathered in the gutters like pale, lukewarm water, faintly luminous even in full daylight. In Fenhold it collected in ditches and hollow stumps, barely enough to notice. Here it ran in actual rivulets between cobblestones, and where it pooled against walls the stone had taken on a glow like bone held against a candle flame. Maren stepped over a stream of it that ran across the road from a cracked drain and felt the warmth through his boot.

A man walked past them and raised one hand, casually, the way someone might wave to a friend. Something happened, a sharp localized heat on the left side of Maren's face, a crack like a branch splitting, and then a scorch mark appeared on the wall beside him, black and smoking. The man hadn't broken stride. He was adjusting the strap on his shoulder bag with the hand that had just put a burn mark on a building.

"What," Maren said, very quietly.

*Class-two fire projection. Casual discharge. He appears to be, ah. He moved up one rank on the damage board. He was testing a new calibration.* Aldric's tone carried the specific disbelief of a man who had once served an order that regarded System abilities as solemn responsibilities. *He scorched a wall. For a leaderboard position.*

*Damage rankings,* Vess said. *Overhead. Every building has one. It is a local hero ranking system based on verified damage output. Updated in real time.*

Maren looked up and saw nothing but rafters and awning poles and the pale sky between them. The rankings were invisible to him, like everything else. He was standing in a town built entirely around information he couldn't access, navigating by scorch marks and smell and the commentary of dead people.

*The man who just scorched the wall is ranked sixty-three,* Aldric said. *He is now sixty-two. He appears to be checking. He has stopped walking to verify his new position. He looks pleased.* A pause. *A woman across the street appears displeased. I believe she was sixty-two. She is gesturing at him. He is gesturing back. This may escalate.*

The woman fired something at a wall further down the street. Another scorch mark. The heat reached him from twenty feet away. A child walking between them didn't flinch. Nobody flinched. This was weather to them.

Maren walked under the invisible leaderboards and the visible scorch marks and tried not to think about the bounty floating over his own head.

---

The market square was worse. Heroes were everywhere, not fighting, not questing, just existing in the specific way that people with System-enhanced abilities existed in their natural habitat. Two women were comparing weapon stats loudly enough that Maren could hear numbers he couldn't verify. A man in scaled armor was doing something to the air around his hands that made it shimmer and pop, drawing a small crowd of onlookers who apparently found this entertaining. A knot of younger heroes, teenagers maybe, with the shiny-faced confidence of people who'd just gotten their first class assignments, clustered around the quest board and argued about which dungeon had the best loot-to-risk ratio.

*Knife woman by the wall,* Vess said. *If she decides you're a problem, you bleed before you get your hands up.*

Maren flinched before he realized she wasn't talking about anyone nearby. She was looking through his eyes at a woman leaning against a wall across the square, cleaning under her fingernails with a short knife.

*Short blade. Clean grip. No wasted motion. The man behind her is slower, but he keeps checking the crowd reflections in that shop window, which means he's not here to shop. The boy at the fruit stall in green has training. If he commits, he reaches you before Derry finishes saying your name. The woman with the ale is useless unless she falls on you. The tall one at the corner watches exits first and people second. I don't like him.*

"Thanks."

*You're welcome.*

"Can you stop sorting the whole town into ways I die?"

*No.*

"Is there anyone in this town who couldn't kill me?"

*The cat on the quest board, presumably. Though I lack information about the hat.*

Derry had fallen behind again. Maren turned and found him three stalls back, nodding vigorously at a vendor who was showing him something wrapped in leather. Derry's smile was so wide it had to hurt. His shirt was soaked through at the collar and the dark stain was working its way down between his shoulder blades.

"What are you doing?"

"Just looking!" Derry's voice cracked upward, too loud, too cheerful. "This is, what did you say this was? A grip thing?"

The vendor, a broad woman with forearms like rolled rope, looked at Derry with the flat patience of someone who'd dealt with confused farmers before. "Grip wrap. For polearm users. Absorbs impact on the shaft."

"Right, right, grip wrap! My cousin uses a, he's got a thing, a pole, a spear thing, and he always says the grip is the most important part."

"You don't have a cousin with a spear," Maren said.

Derry's smile didn't falter so much as solidify, like plaster setting wrong. "I might. You don't know all my cousins."

"I know all your cousins. There are two. One raises goats and the other one can't lift his right arm above his shoulder."

The vendor looked between them with the expression of someone deciding whether to be amused or annoyed. Maren put a hand on Derry's arm and steered him away from the stall. Derry resisted for a moment, turned back, waved at the vendor, said "Sorry, we might come back, it's really nice stuff, the grip thing," and then let himself be moved.

"You have to stop doing that," Maren said, low.

"Doing what?"

"Buying things. Talking to people. Smiling like that."

"Like what?"

"Like a man who's about to be arrested and wants everyone to think he's having a nice day."

Derry wiped his face with both hands. The sweat came right back, beading along his hairline within seconds. "I'm trying. I am. I just, every time someone looks at me, my mouth opens, and it starts. I told that woman back there about Pell's counting. With the beads. The little counting frame with the beads. She didn't ask. She was selling rope. I told a rope vendor about my daughter's arithmetic."

*His cortisol levels must be extraordinary,* Aldric observed. *The man is producing enough sweat to alter local humidity.*

"Not helpful," Maren said, to Aldric and to no one visible, which earned him a glance from a passing hero that he pretended not to notice.

They needed food that would keep, and they needed to know which road past Harrow went toward the Crook without cutting through active dungeon territory. They needed to accomplish both without anyone looking at Maren long enough to see what wasn't there.

*The dried-goods vendor three stalls east,* Aldric said. *But the man sitting beside him, armored, large, on a crate, has a passive scan ability. I can see the active aura. He is reading the classification of everyone who passes within ten yards.*

"So we go around."

*The next nearest food vendor is across the square. Past the quest board. Past the two taverns. Past the table.*

Maren looked. The table was, in fact, on fire. A woman in scalemail sat next to it, laughing, while a man in a leather coat poured ale over the flames. The ale did nothing useful. A third person, a boy maybe sixteen, was trying to smother the fire with his jacket, and the jacket was also now on fire.

"Do they know," Maren said, "that the table is on fire?"

*They appear to consider it recreational.*

"And the jacket?"

*Also recreational, I must assume, given that no one is alarmed. The boy is laughing now. The jacket is ruined. I want you to know that in fourteen years of service I never once set furniture on fire without tactical justification.*

Derry had stopped beside a stall selling polishing compound and was asking the vendor about the proper way to maintain leather boots, which was a topic he did actually know something about, and the vendor was answering with the weary tolerance of a man who'd had this exact conversation six hundred times. Derry's hands were in his pockets, but Maren could see the tremor in his forearms.

Maren reached the food vendor on the far side of the square, a thin man with sun-bleached hair and a stall that smelled like salt and old smoke. His provisions looked road-worthy: dried meat, hard bread, something in waxed paper that might have been cheese or might have been soap. Maren was reaching for his money when the vendor's eyes shifted, not to Maren's face but to a point slightly above and to the left of his head, the place where, Aldric had explained weeks ago, System-sighted people saw the classification data that accompanied every person like a name pinned to a shirt.

The vendor looked at the spot, then looked again. His expression did something subtle and wrong, not suspicion, but the face a person makes when they reach for a step that isn't there.

"Class?" the vendor asked in the same conversational tone someone in any other town might use for *where are you from.*

"Sorry?"

"Your class. Just, you passing through? Most of my regulars are ranged-damage types, they like the jerky. Melee goes for the bread. What's your build?"

The hum in his chest intensified, though that might have been his heart. "Support class," he said, with the easy tired confidence of a man stating something too boring to lie about. "We're just passing through. Need road food."

"Support should still display, though." The vendor squinted at the empty space. "Everyone displays. Even noncombat roles. Healers, buffers, scouts. They all show a classification."

"Gear interference. It's been, look, it's a whole thing with my interface. It's a known issue."

The lie came smooth, but the vendor's hand was slow reaching for the jerky, and his eyes kept drifting back to the void where Maren's classification should have been, the way a person's eyes will keep returning to a gap in a fence where a board ought to be. Not alarmed yet. Bothered.

Maren put coins on the counter, too many, enough to buy the vendor's entire stock of jerky twice over. "Just the bread and the jerky. Keep the change. We're in a rush."

The vendor looked at the coins, then at the void, then at the coins again. Greed and curiosity fought a brief visible war across his face, and greed won, but not by enough to stop his mouth.

"I've never seen a blank read," he said, handing over the food. "Not once. Not even broken gear gives you an error I can recognize."

Maren took the food and walked away. Behind him the vendor was still talking, not to Maren anymore but to the woman at the next stall, and Maren heard the word *blank* and then *weird* and then something he couldn't catch because Derry was beside him now, breathing hard, clutching a small jar of boot polish he'd apparently purchased.

"You bought boot polish," Maren said.

"She was very convincing."

"You're wearing sandals."

Derry looked down at his feet. He was, in fact, back in sandals. His boots were tied to the outside of his pack, which made the boot polish worse. He looked from the jar to the boots and then back again, as if the objects had conspired against him.

They still needed the road. Thirty feet ahead, bolted to a post beside a public water trough, a weathered route board pointed north in three directions. Notices had been pinned under it so many times the wood looked furred with old nail holes.

*Left branch is the ridge road,* Aldric said. *It skirts the nearest dungeon mouths, then takes the Crook service turn six miles north. There is a maintenance marker on the lower strip and, irritatingly, a levy notice nailed over half of it.*

Maren caught Derry by the elbow and steered him toward the board. "Read the wood," he muttered. "Not the light."

Derry squinted at the painted arrows like a man trying to remember his own name. "North ridge road. Gate levy. Hazard markers. And there, smaller. Crook service turn six miles north. Mule traffic, drainage wagons, that sort of thing. That's probably us. We look very mule traffic."

"Good. Left fork."

Behind them the vendor said, louder now, "I've never seen a blank read." The words carried. In Harrow, they probably carried on purpose.

The woman at the next stall stood up. Blue cloak, work gloves, ordinary face, the sort you stopped seeing the second you looked away. She crossed toward the route board without hurrying. Her eyes went first to the empty place above Maren's shoulder. Then to the notices pinned beneath the arrows.

*Bounty slips,* Aldric said. His voice had thinned to something precise and dangerous. *Four current. One is yours. The sketch is useless. The text is not.*

Maren kept his pace even. Derry did not. Derry slowed by half a step, which was enough.

"Hey, Korr," the woman said to the armored man on the crate. "Read me the fourth notice."

Korr turned with the bored reluctance of a man being asked to do his job in public. His expression changed when he looked at Maren and found nothing. He got off the crate.

"Unclassified entity," Korr read. "Male, late twenties. Lean build. Dark hair. System profile absent or corrupted. Four thousand crowns, live delivery preferred."

Derry made a small sound in the back of his throat.

Korr looked up from the notice. This time he looked at Derry as well. "And the other one looks like he might throw up. That helps."

"We're leaving," Maren said. "Now."

*The woman at the board,* Vess said. *She's already moving.*

---

Maren didn't turn around. The road north forked past a tavern with a green-painted sign; he could see that much. The left fork should take them toward dungeon territory and eventually the Crook. He angled toward it.

*She has stood up,* Vess said. *The woman. Blue cloak. She was seated at the stall where the man with the passive scan sits. She looked at you when you passed and now she is walking.*

"Could be nothing."

*She is walking at pursuit pace. Not running. Matching your speed at fifteen yards. She signaled with her off hand, two fingers and a lateral sweep. That is a flanking directive.*

The market noise continued around them, the burning table, the invisible leaderboard overhead, the boy with the smoldering jacket, who had given up on his jacket and was now watching it burn on the cobblestones with his arms crossed. None of it had changed, but the air around Maren had turned thin, and the hum in his chest had become a specific directional pressure, like a finger pressing on his sternum.

*Two more are moving from the tavern entrance on your right,* Vess said. *They were seated. They are not seated now. The one in front is tier four, minimum. He moves like someone who has done this before.*

Derry hadn't heard any of it, the fragments were inside Maren's skull, but he'd seen something change in Maren's face, or his pace, or the way his shoulders had come up, because Derry's own face drained to the color of old cheese.

"What?" Derry whispered. "What is it? What's—"

"Don't look behind us."

Derry immediately looked behind them.

"I said—"

"Sorry, sorry, I know, I just, there's a woman, she's sort of, she's walking fast—"

*She is not the primary concern,* Vess said. *The man from the tavern is. He has a scan active. A real one, not the passive ambient type. He is actively querying you. And he is getting—*

*Nothing,* Aldric said. His composure had cracked and the words came fast. *He is getting nothing. The physical description matches half the men in this region, that was never enough. But a blank read is unique. She spotted the void, not your face.*

A voice behind them, not shouting but pitched to carry across a market without sounding like a command: "Hey. Support class. With the blank. Stop walking, please."

*Please,* Vess noted. *That is someone trained to de-escalate before escalating.*

Maren did not stop. He walked faster. The distinction between walking fast and running was closing rapidly, and Derry was beside him, breathing in those awful wet hitches, the boot polish still in his hand.

"Oh god," Derry said. "Oh god, oh shit, Maren—"

"Walk."

"I'm walking. I'm walking, but they're, she's—"

*Run,* Vess said.

Vess only spent single-word instructions when the decision was already made. If she said run, walking had expired a second ago.

They ran. Maren's pack bounced against his spine. Derry's sandals slapped the cobblestones, too loud, too flat, the wrong footwear for flight and the wrong man for it too. Behind them the voice called out again, louder now, and other voices picked it up. Not a mob, not yet, but the specific rising attention of a crowd that had noticed something interesting happening and was turning to watch.

*Left at the fork,* Aldric said, his formality abandoned entirely, directions barked like a man reading a map while the house burned. *Left, then straight, the road opens past the—*

*Right,* Vess said.

*I said left.*

*You said left because the road goes north. I said right because there are three heroes between us and your left fork and two of them have already turned.*

Maren went right. The turn took him between a shuttered gear shop and a squat building that stank of animal fat and tallow. Quest-light pooled in the alley's gutter, warm against his ankles when he splashed through it, and the scorch marks on the alley walls were so dense they'd merged into a continuous dark stain, years of hero traffic burned into the stone like a record of carelessness.

Derry was behind him, still running and still making that sound, not panting but something closer to the noise a man makes when he's trying very hard not to scream and the effort is coming out through his breathing instead.

*Alley opens onto a side street in twelve yards,* Vess said. *Wall breach beyond it. Half-blocked by collapse, but still passable.*

"How do you know that from in here?"

*Interface damage report. No repair ticket. Nobody bothered.*

They burst out of the alley onto the side street and Maren understood at once why the breach had stayed open. One side of the wall had been melted smooth as candle wax. The other had slumped into a heap of blackened timbers and broken stone. Someone had shoved a pair of planks across the rubble to turn it into a rough shortcut, and right now a woman with a handcart was trying to drag cabbages through while a mule balked beside her.

Behind them a whistle cut the air. Another answered farther up the wall.

A boy in a soot-streaked apron tore past the mouth of the alley, sprinting uphill toward the proper gate. "Blank read!" he shouted. "North side breach! Blank read moving north!"

The woman with the cart froze. The mule rolled an eye white.

"Move," Maren said.

She did not move fast enough. Maren hit the handcart with his shoulder, hard enough to jolt his teeth. The cart tipped. Cabbages burst across the stones. The woman screamed at him. The mule jerked sideways and slammed its flank against the broken wall, blocking the gap for one stupid terrible second.

*Three seconds,* Vess said. *Maybe four.*

Maren grabbed the mule's harness and yanked. Derry squeezed past him, hit the planks wrong, and went to one knee in the rubble with both hands out, skinning one palm open on the stone.

"Up," Maren said.

Derry made a frightened choking sound. Maren caught the back of his shirt and hauled him bodily through the gap. His own pack snagged on a splintered beam hard enough to wrench him backward. Cloth tore. One loaf of bread and half the jerky spilled into the dust and the warm luminous slime crusted along the breach stones. He let them go.

A horn sounded from somewhere deeper in Harrow. Then another, farther off, nearer the north road.

They stumbled down the outer slope of rubble and hit dirt. Maren looked back once, just long enough to see the proper north gate fifty yards over and a rider already breaking from it at a gallop, leaning low over the horse with a satchel bouncing against his hip.

*Courier,* Aldric said. His voice had gone cold again. *The relay is already moving.*

They ran until the wall fell behind them and the road bent between scrub hills. Only then did Maren force himself to slow. His lungs burned. Derry was cursing under his breath now, clutching his scraped palm against his shirt.

"Walk," Maren said. "Normal. Like we belong here."

Derry was doubled over beside him, hands on his knees, making bellows sounds. The boot polish was gone. So was half their food.

"I can't. I don't remember what normal looks like."

"Walk like you're going to check on the cow. The cow got out again. You're annoyed but not surprised."

Derry made a noise that was either a laugh or the first stage of something worse, but he straightened up and walked. The walk was terrible, stiff-armed, too rigid, a man wearing the idea of casual movement like an ill-fitting coat, but it carried him forward along the road beyond Harrow's walls.

The hum lessened. Quest-light thinned to occasional dull patches in the roadside ditches. The scorch marks became older, paler, spaced apart enough that the packed dirt between them looked like actual road again.

*Confirmed,* Aldric said after a silence just long enough to hurt. *Multiple witnesses. Every town between here and the Crook will have the read before we get there.*

Maren didn't answer. He walked. The bread and jerky in his pack were lighter now. His money was lighter too.

Derry was walking beside him with the rigid mechanical gait of a man whose legs hadn't caught up with the fact that they were no longer sprinting. His hands shook, not with the subtle tremor from the market but with a proper shake, the kind that made his fingers useless, and he pressed them flat against his thighs to make them stop and they didn't stop.

"Maren," Derry said.

"Yeah."

"I talked to a woman about cloaks."

"I know."

"I don't wear cloaks. I've never, I told her about Pell. About the counting beads. She was selling cloaks and I told her my daughter was learning to count. And then I bought boot polish, and my boots were on the pack the whole time." He looked down at his hand. "I lost the polish anyway."

"Yeah."

"My hands won't stop."

Maren looked at Derry's hands. The shake was bad, fingers spread, tendons standing out, the kind of tremor you got when the body's terror outran the mind's ability to manage it. Maren's own hands were steady, and he didn't know what that meant. Maybe the killing two weeks ago had burned something out, some connection between fear and its physical expression. Or maybe the fear had just moved deeper, past the hands and the sweat and the shaking, down into the flat dry recognition that things were worse now and would keep getting worse, and the road ahead led into dungeon territory because the road behind had a description of him running along it faster than his feet could.

*The Crook is four days north,* Vess said. *Five if the road has deteriorated past dungeon-approach standards.*

*If we reach the Crook,* Aldric said.

Vess said nothing.

Behind them, faint but carrying, a horn answered another horn. Not market noise anymore. Not rumor either.

Maren walked north. The scorch marks on the road grew older and fewer, until there were none at all and the road was just dirt, and the dirt was just the world, and the world didn't care what the System thought he was or wasn't.

But the people in it did. That was the problem. That had always been the problem.


# Chapter 10

The road past Harrow turned to packed earth and stayed that way. No scorch marks out here. The heroes went the other direction, toward the dungeon mouths, and left their damage there. Maren's feet had been swollen since yesterday, and the cooler morning air was the first mercy in a week, settling into the backs of his hands like water. Late summer letting go. He could feel the season turning in the longer shadows, in the grass along the road going brittle and pale at the tips.

Derry hadn't said much since they'd run from Harrow. Not nothing. He'd offered Maren some dried meat at dawn, asked if the road felt right, started in about the clouds and let the sentence die in his teeth. After that he walked with his mouth shut and his eyes on the ruts, as if one harmless remark might open a door he couldn't close again.

*Two figures on the ridge. North side. Half a mile back.*

Vess, calibrating threats. She'd been doing it since Harrow, quiet updates delivered with the flat precision of someone checking instruments.

*Hero-class?* Maren thought.

*Farmers. Hay rakes. Not a concern.*

Aldric hadn't spoken all morning. When the paladin went quiet, it meant he was either seeing a pattern in the System's architecture that he didn't know how to name or composing a lecture so thorough it required structural planning. Both demanded silence, and both usually ended with Maren feeling worse about himself.

They came around a bend where the road widened into a clearing. Someone had cut back the brush on both sides, maybe for a seasonal market nobody used anymore. The stumps were sawed low and gone grey. The man was sitting at the far edge. Sitting, not standing. His hands on his knees, empty.

Maren stopped.

Derry almost walked into him, then looked where Maren was looking, and his hand went to Maren's elbow. Not to guide him. To hold on.

The man wore ranger gear that had seen real weather, leather darkened at the stress points, stitching redone along one shoulder over an old tear, repaired without vanity. His pack sat against a stump, buckled shut. A short sword rested on the ground beside his right boot, positioned where he could reach it but clearly not where he was reaching for it. His hair was cropped close, and his face had the color of someone who slept outside more often than in. He favored his right knee. Maren could see it in how he sat, the leg extended slightly, not quite willing to fold all the way.

What Maren noticed first wasn't the gear or the weapon or the deliberate stillness. It was the old smile lines at the corners of the man's mouth, still there even though nothing in his face was using them.

*Hero-ranger. Active class. High level. The bounty is open on his interface.* Vess, each word placed like a stone. *He can see what you are. He's been here at least an hour.*

*And he has not drawn his weapon,* Aldric said. The paladin's voice was careful, which was different from his usual formality. Careful like a man stepping around broken glass on the floor. *That is not a good sign.*

Maren understood why immediately. A ranger who attacked was a problem you could solve, by running, by fighting, by being lucky. A ranger who sat down and waited for you to walk to him wanted terms, and running or fighting wouldn't touch those.

"There's two ways this goes," the man said. His voice carried the clearing without effort, trained to project over distance, the habit so deep it worked even here. "One of them involves me trailing you for another three days, and honestly my knee isn't up for it this week. So I'd rather the other way."

Derry's hand tightened on Maren's elbow.

"What's the other way?" Maren said. His own voice came out steadier than the inside of him warranted, the performance booting up on its own, charm and deflection and the right tone, mechanical as breathing.

"We talk." The man shifted on the stump, resettling his weight off the bad knee. "I'm Caul. I've been behind you since Harrow. Ahead of you now, which I realize looks staged, but the road only goes one way from here and I walk faster than you do."

*He is telling the truth about his name. Caul Henning. Registered with the Wayward Reach ranger company. Twelve years active service.* Aldric stopped. A silence that felt different from his usual pauses, not deliberate, not tactical. Arrested.

*What?*

*His party roster shows two members deceased. Recent. Three months ago.*

"You can sit," Caul said. "I've got water. Your friend looks like he's about to fall over."

Derry did look bad. The color had left his face, and his shirt was damp at the collar despite the cool morning. His grip on Maren's elbow kept tightening and loosening, his body already in the fear before the rest of him caught up. But he was looking at Maren, not at Caul, and Maren could read that look with all his professional practice: *Do we run?*

*Running would be inadvisable,* Vess said. *He has sight lines on both exits from this clearing. Brush cover to the south, but the terrain drops into a gully thirty metres in. He chose this spot.*

"Yeah," Maren said to Derry, though it wasn't really an answer to the question Derry was asking. He let go of the tension in his shoulders, a conscious decision to rearrange his body into a loose easy shape, and walked to the middle of the clearing and sat on the ground. Not because he wanted to sit. Because standing in front of a seated man with a sword on the ground made the power too obvious, and Maren's whole professional life was about rearranging the power in a room until he could find a way to work it.

Derry sat too, but farther away. Not beside Maren, the way he would have a week ago. About four feet to Maren's left, knees drawn up, arms wrapped around them. The distance was small, and Maren measured it automatically, with the part of himself that catalogued what people were telling him with their bodies before they'd decided to tell him anything with their mouths.

"So," Maren said.

"So." Caul looked at him. His eyes were the light grey of old iron, and he didn't push, didn't fill the quiet, just kept Maren in that steady look until the clearing felt arranged around the answer he wanted. "You're the unclassified. The bounty."

"I've been told."

"Your profile had a sin-eater registration before the classification scrambled. Did you know that? It's still there underneath. Eleven years of service logged."

Maren's chest went very still. Not a tightening. A cessation, the way a room goes quiet when everyone hears the same sound at once.

"I know who I am," Maren said. "I don't know what you want."

"I want to tell you what happened." Caul recapped a water skin he'd been holding and set it beside his boot. The gesture was unhurried, almost domestic, a man settling in for a conversation over a table, not confronting a fugitive in a clearing. "After that, you can go. For a while." He looked at Derry, then back at Maren. "Your friend should hear it too."

Derry said nothing. His arms were locked around his knees, and he stared at the dirt between his feet like there might be a seam in it wide enough to crawl through.

"Three months ago," Caul said, "my party cleared a dungeon near the Ashfold border. Standard contract. Minor threat creatures, containment perimeter, System-flagged quest. We'd done a hundred of these. Probably two hundred. You stop counting after the first year."

He spoke the way someone reads aloud from a document they've memorized, each sentence placed and cadenced with the even rhythm of a field report. But the rhythm was too even. Too practiced. Maren recognized it because he recognized performance, and this man had rehearsed what he was about to say. Not to be convincing. To survive saying it.

"The site was supposed to be clean. That's what the log said. Proper sin-eater on file, all the seals in place. System records showed the contamination cleared eighteen months before we got there. Haley checked it herself. She always checked. She trusted the record."

*Haley Keth,* Aldric said, very quietly, reading from whatever the System kept on file for the dead. *Healer-class. Twenty-six at time of death. Renn Ostara. Shield-bearer. Twenty-nine.*

Caul's hands stayed on his knees. "The dungeon was routine. Threat level minimal. But the contamination wasn't gone. It had settled into the substrate, below what a standard scan reads, in the deep residue a real sin-eater is supposed to pull. Old contamination. Layers of it. Years built up because someone kept marking the area clean and walking away."

The clearing was quiet. Somewhere behind them a bird kept repeating a three-note call, and Maren wanted it to stop because the sound was too ordinary for what was happening in his chest.

"Haley went down first. The contamination spiked when the quest activated. The old residue caught the fresh System energy and everything came up at once, like pulling a tarp off a floor that's been rotting underneath." Caul paused. The practiced rhythm slipped, and what was under it showed for a second, a man trying very hard to hold a shape that grief kept pulling out of true. "Renn tried to get her out. The spike caught him too."

Maren's hands were in his lap. He was looking at them. They were still, which surprised him, because the rest of him felt like a hook had come loose inside his ribs and was swinging free, knocking against the walls of him with each breath.

*He is describing contamination persistence from incomplete sin-eating,* Aldric said. The paladin didn't sound like the paladin, not lecturing, not moral, not correcting. A strain Maren hadn't heard from him before. *A genuine sin-eater clears the contamination from the substrate. A fraudulent one only removes the surface. What he is describing is exact.*

*I know,* Maren thought, hard enough that the thought had edges. *I know what he's describing.*

Caul was watching him. The ranger's face hadn't changed, still the settled field-report composure, but his eyes had slipped past professionalism. They were looking at Maren the way you look at a word you're trying to read in bad light.

"The sin-eater on that job looked real on paper," Caul said. "Class mark. Ritual sequence. Every line stamped and filed. Everything that was supposed to mean safe. The contamination was still there because the work under it was empty." He paused and let the silence sit. "Do you understand what I'm telling you?"

"Yeah," Maren said. "I understand."

"There are more of you than people think. Fake sin-eaters. Some of you are System-blind. Some just figured out that doing it wrong pays the same as doing it right." Caul's fingers pressed into his knees, the knuckles whitening briefly. "The System doesn't audit the work. It checks the paperwork. It checks the registration. It doesn't check the ground. It assumes the job got done."

Derry made a small sound. Not a word, a tight exhaled noise, the kind of sound he made when he was trying to process a fact too large for speech. Maren didn't look at him. He couldn't look at Derry right then, because if he looked he would see Derry's face doing what it was doing, understanding exactly what Caul was describing and knowing exactly who it applied to, and that understanding would be written in every line of Derry's open, honest, terrible face.

"Haley liked birds," Caul said. The shift was sudden, a crack in the practiced frame he didn't try to cover. "She kept a journal. Drew them. She wasn't any good at it. Heads too big. Wings too short. Renn used to tell her they looked like angry potatoes with beaks." His face shifted in a way that wasn't a smile. "He could draw better than she could, but he'd never have told her that. He just let her show him, and he said they were good."

*Don't say anything,* Vess said. The assassin's voice carried its usual operational flatness, but there was a precision in it that Maren had learned to recognize as more than routine. *He isn't asking for a response. He's showing you what he lost. Let him.*

Caul reached into the front of his ranger's jacket and took out a folded piece of paper, creased and re-creased so many times the folds were nearly worn through, the edges gone soft. He didn't unfold it. He held it in his hand for a moment, then put it back inside the jacket.

"I've been tracking since they died. Not your bounty specifically. I picked that up in Harrow. Your profile matched reports I'd been collecting. I've been tracking the problem. Certification gaps. Places where the contamination records don't match the service history." He smoothed the front of his jacket where the paper sat. "There are a lot of them."

Maren started to say he wasn't what Caul thought, that Caul had the wrong man, that he didn't know what he was talking about. The lie came up whole and familiar. Years of practice had made it as natural as swallowing.

He didn't say it. Not because honesty won. Honesty had never won a fight inside Maren. But the lie would have required him to look at Caul's face while he said it, and Caul's face belonged to a man carrying a dead woman's bad bird drawings in his jacket. Some performances couldn't survive the audience.

"You're not what?" Caul said. Patient. Waiting the way a stone waits.

"I don't know what to tell you," Maren said. "That's true. I don't know what to tell you."

It was the truest thing he'd said in thirteen days. The sentence sat in his chest like a river stone.

Aldric said nothing.

"I'm not asking you to tell me anything," Caul said. "I'm telling you so you know what's out there. What's been left behind in the villages where sin-eaters took the fee and didn't do the work." He looked at the sky, checking the light or maybe checking himself. "You're headed for the Crook."

Maren didn't confirm or deny. His mouth was dry. His hands were very still in his lap.

"I know you are, because there's nowhere else from here unless you're planning to walk into dungeon territory and die. Which I don't think you're doing." Caul stood up from the stump. The right knee gave him trouble. Maren could see the hitch, the way he braced a hand against the stump and pushed through the stiffness, the joint complaining in a way he'd clearly learned to manage but not hide. "I'm going to give you a day. Not because you've earned it. Because the Crook is where sin-eaters answer for this kind of thing, and I'd rather they handle it than me taking it into my own hands on a road that I'd have to write up afterward." He picked up his pack strap, then stopped. "I want this done right. Haley would've wanted it done right."

"A day," Maren said.

"Starting now. I'll be a day behind you. If you're at the Crook when I arrive, we do this properly. If you're not there, we do it differently."

He picked up his short sword and sheathed it in a motion so practiced it looked involuntary, the hand finding the scabbard the way a tongue finds a missing tooth. Then he shouldered his pack. The buckles clinked against each other, a small ordinary sound. He looked at Derry for the first time with no tactical assessment in it. Just one ordinary man looking at another and seeing the specific misery of being here because of someone else's decisions.

Then he walked past them, back the direction they'd come from. His footsteps on the packed earth were even and unhurried. The bad knee didn't slow him. It only changed the rhythm, a slight hitch every other step, an asymmetry in the sound that Maren listened to until it faded into the distance and the clearing was empty except for the two of them and the three voices in his skull, all of them quiet.

---
