Maren sat where he was. The ground was cool through his trousers, and a root was pressing into his left thigh, a specific insistent discomfort that gave him a clean point to organize his attention around.

*Haley Keth,* Aldric said eventually. *She completed her healer certification at twenty-two. Three years of study at the Ashfold academy. She specialized in contamination triage. The medical side. How to stabilize someone exposed to quest residue.* A pause. *She should not have needed to use that training on herself. The area was supposed to be clean.*

*I know.*

*You do know. You have known for years what your work leaves behind.*

*Yeah. I know that too.*

Aldric went silent. Not the pointed quiet of disapproval this time. A heavier kind of silence.

Maren closed his eyes. The red behind his eyelids was warm from the morning sun, and he kept them shut because opening them meant the clearing was still there and Derry was still in it, four feet to his left, quiet.

"Maren." Derry had kept his mouth shut so long the name came out dry and misplaced, like he had to test it before trusting the rest of the sentence. "He was talking about us."

"Yeah."

Derry swallowed. His hands moved against the fabric of his trousers with a dry scraping sound.

"His friends went into that place and it wasn't clean, because — because somebody did what we did," he said.

Maren opened his eyes. The clearing looked the same, grey stumps, brushed-back scrub, the road curving away in both directions, the bird still going at its three-note song as though the morning hadn't changed. The root was still pressing into his thigh.

"Not us specifically," he said. "He said the sin-eater was certified. Properly registered. We never had that. I had a bowl and a routine." He heard himself doing it, laying out the thin careful distinction that left room for his own shape inside the guilt. He hated it and kept going anyway.

"But someone like us," Derry said.

"Someone like me."

"Like us, Maren. I was — I fed you the cues, I told you what the System was showing so you could fake it, and I was right there. Every ritual, every village, I was right there."

*He is correct,* Vess said. No judgment. Only the fact.

Maren looked at Derry. The four feet between them felt built now, not empty. Derry's arms were locked around his knees. Dirty red-brown hair lay flat on one side from sleep. The skin at the corners of his mouth had gone white. He looked at Maren for a second too long, then dropped his eyes back to the dirt.

"But how many, though," Derry said. "How many villages — in all that time, how many places where it's still sitting in the ground because we said it was gone."

"I don't know." True. Maren had never counted. Counting would have required him to think about the villages together, and the whole fraud only worked if each one stayed small enough to leave behind. Twelve coppers. A bowl. A nod from the family. Move on. "I never counted."

"A lot," Derry said.

"Yeah."

Derry rubbed a thumb across his knuckles. "Lenna'd had honey set aside before we left, and she'd been talking about this cake for weeks, and I just kept — I kept thinking I'd get back in time for it. That's the thing I was holding on to. Out of everything." He swallowed again. "People were living in it, Maren. In Fenhold. In my house."

The sentence landed and stayed there.

*He is right,* Aldric said. Not to punish. Just to confirm.

"Did you know, though?" Derry said. "All that time — did you know the contamination was staying?"

Maren opened his mouth. The truth came up whole and hot enough to choke him. By the time he got breath around it, he'd already started cutting it smaller.

"I didn't think about it," Maren said. "Not the way you're asking. I thought about the ritual and the fee and the next village. I didn't think past that."

Derry's face tightened in a small specific way around the eyes.

"If somebody at the Crook asks me what we were doing out there," he said, "I'm not — I can't make it sound clean. I can't do that. I can't. And if they ask about Fenhold, about my place, I'm telling them that too."

He stood up. He turned toward the road without offering a hand, without the half-second pause where he'd usually check Maren's direction and match his pace. He just started walking.

Maren got up. His left leg was stiff from the root, and he shook it out. The ceramic bowl in his pack shifted when he shouldered it, the smooth familiar weight of it against his spine. The same bowl. The same one he'd carried into houses where people were grieving and used to perform a ceremony that did nothing while they stood in the doorway with the particular relief of people who believed the contamination was being lifted from the room where their husband or mother or child had died.

He followed Derry.

They walked. The road was empty, and the packed earth was dry. The shadows had that longer, thinner quality late summer took on when autumn started winning. The hum was louder out here, farther from Harrow's System-dense interference, the background frequency of ambient System activity settled into Maren's hearing like tinnitus.

Derry walked ahead. Not far. Ten feet maybe. But he didn't fall back. For all the years they'd walked between villages, splitting the fee and the fraud and the ordinary shape of a friendship, Derry had always matched Maren's pace. Now he didn't.

The distance held through the rest of the day, steady and precise. Derry never let Maren draw even; if Maren gained a step, Derry found one too, keeping that strip of road between them as if he needed the space to think inside. They climbed into the cooling hills where the road narrowed and the trees grew closer together. The hum got louder. The shadows stretched.

At dusk Derry cut off the road without asking and took the first campsite that would do, a ring of old stones beside a dry wash where the bank dropped away under scrub roots. He put his pack down on the far side of the fire circle before Maren had shrugged his off. Ten feet on the road became fifteen with the blackened stones between them.

No one said they should stop. No one said they were tired. Derry crouched and gathered deadfall in quick efficient motions, all business now, no farmer's chatter about the quality of the wood or whether the weather would hold. Maren went for water because it was the task left. By the time he came back from a thin trickle caught in the wash, Derry had the fire lit and two hunks of bread set out on separate stones.

He didn't hand Maren his portion. He set it down and looked away. That was worse.

Maren unslung his pack. The bowl knocked once against the side of it, a small ceramic click. Derry's head came up at the sound. His eyes went to the pack, then away so fast the movement had shame in it, like he'd walked in on a private thing by accident.

They ate without appetite. The bread was stale enough to scrape the roof of Maren's mouth. Smoke drifted low because the air had gone cooler after sunset, carrying that dry-leaf smell autumn sent ahead of itself. Under it sat another smell, faint but ugly now that Thessa had given it a name: sweet-metallic, damp stone, the edge of old rot under clean water.

*There is contamination in the wash,* Aldric said after a while. *Old. Thin. The water is passing over it rather than holding it.*

*Not enough to hurt you from drinking once,* Vess said. *Enough to taste, if you know what you're tasting.*

Maren looked at the cup in his hand and wished he hadn't.

Derry was turning his bread over and over without eating it. Finally he said, "How many houses do you actually remember?"

Maren had to think about it. That was answer enough before he opened his mouth.

"Bits," he said.

"What bits?"

"A doorway with smoke worked into the lintel. A woman with flour on her wrists. A man who kept coughing into a rag and apologizing for it. Candle grease down the side of the bowl. Mud floors. Wet wool. Kids being told to wait outside." He looked at the fire because looking at Derry made the list worse. "Not names. Mostly not names."

Derry shut his eyes for a second. "Fuck."

Nobody spoke after that. The fire settled lower. Once, somewhere down the wash, a night animal crashed through brush and went still.

When Maren spread his blanket, Derry shifted his own farther back without comment, just an extra drag through the dirt, another two feet bought and set in place. He lay down with his back to the fire and his back to Maren both, curled around himself with one hand under his head and the other closed hard around the strap of his pack.

Maren stayed sitting.

The bowl was out before he registered taking it out. He held it in both hands, thumbs along the chipped rim, and the glaze had that greasy-smooth feel it always had after years of being washed in cold water and wiped on whatever cloth came closest. He could have traced every part of the fraud on it with his fingers. The nick near the base from when it clipped a doorstep and survived. The hairline scratch inside from when Derry packed it with the cookpot and swore for ten minutes because he thought he'd broken the whole act. Eleven years of handling it like a tool, a prop, a good reliable lie.

*You never once asked what was left behind,* Aldric said.

"No," Maren said under his breath. Not an argument. Just the shape of the thing.

Below them the wash kept giving off that thin wrong smell. He'd walked past it for years without knowing why certain wells tasted off, why some rooms had a sweetness under the mold, why the back of his throat sometimes prickled after a ritual and he blamed candle smoke. The knowledge did not arrive like revelation. It arrived like a man coming home and finding his own furniture in someone else's house.

Across the fire Derry did not move, but Maren could tell he wasn't asleep. His breathing stayed wrong. Too deliberate. Too even.

Maren set the bowl in his lap and listened to the hum in the dark until it separated into layers: the ordinary ambient thrum of the System in the road, the thinner dirty thread down in the wash, the fire settling inward on itself, Derry awake and not speaking. He kept his hands on the bowl until they ached.


# Chapter 11

The road past Harrow got worse in a way that felt deliberate, as if the land itself was shedding the pretense of being traveled. Ruts deepened and multiplied. Scorch marks on the remaining flat stones grew older and more layered, the kind that came from years of hero-class abilities fired in the same direction without anyone repairing the surface underneath. The ditches were choked with quest-light residue, not the faint shimmer Maren was used to from Fenhold but thicker deposits, waist-deep in places, the pale glow moving sluggishly like creek water with flour stirred through it.

Derry walked ahead of him. Twenty feet now. Since Caul, they hadn't gone shoulder to shoulder once; yesterday Derry had kept a steady ten feet between them all day, the silence still shell-shocked, still trying to decide what to do with Caul's accusation. Now the distance had doubled. The silence had gone tight as fence wire. Derry had pulled it that way one careful stretch at a time, with the kind of attention to the road that suggested he was picking through blame, not ruts.

Pell hadn't come up since the night before Caul found them. The omission sat between them like a third traveler, taking up more room than either of them acknowledged.

*The road drops about forty feet in the next half-mile,* Aldric said. *There is a creek bed at the bottom. The contamination density increases near the water.*

"Worse how?"

*Pooling. Quest residue collected in a natural basin. Roughly six feet by four, moderate depth. Active contamination, not residual. An abandoned quest was left here recently enough that the residue has not fully settled.*

Vess, who had been quiet most of the morning: *Small enough to practice on.*

Maren's foot caught on a root and he stumbled. Derry glanced back, then looked away.

"I wasn't thinking about—"

*Yes, you were,* Vess said.

She was right. Since Caul's quiet, measured explanation of how Haley and Renn had walked into a dungeon that should have been clean, Maren had been running the numbers. Not the kind Aldric could read off the interface. The other kind, the human arithmetic. Eleven years. Roughly monthly rituals. Call it a hundred and thirty villages, farmsteads, crossroads where someone had died or a quest had expired and the contamination needed clearing. A hundred and thirty times he'd knelt with his ceramic bowl and his murmured nonsense and his careful, practiced sincerity, and the contamination had stayed exactly where it was.

How many people that had killed, he didn't know. Thessa might. Thessa probably kept count.

The creek bed came into view through a stand of scrubby sycamores, the water barely a trickle this late in the season, running over dark stones that were darker near the banks where the quest-light had stained them. The pool was exactly where Aldric had said it would be: a shallow depression in the creek bed's widest point, the residue sitting in it like pale broth poured into a hollow and left to thicken. All it looked like was light. It looked warm, almost inviting, if you didn't know what it was.

The smell hit him next. Ozone and a sweeter rot underneath, the specific contamination odor that most people filtered through their interface as data: threat levels, clearance requirements, class-appropriate warnings. To Maren it was just a smell. It had always been just a smell.

"I'm going to try a pass," he said.

Derry had stopped at the tree line and stayed there.

"Maren—"

"I know."

"You don't know what I was going to say."

"You were going to say it's dangerous, or I don't know what I'm doing, or both, and you'd be right about all of it." Maren set his pack down on a flat stone near the bank and took out the ceramic bowl, chipped at the rim. "I'm going to do it anyway."

Derry's mouth worked. Whatever he wanted to say got chewed up by the distance between them, and Maren was already kneeling, already arranging the bowl on the flat stone the way he'd arranged it a hundred and thirty times before, the choreography so worn into him it felt older than thought.

*That is not how you begin,* Aldric said.

"I know that too."

*The containment posture. Thessa would have started with it. Hands open, fingers spread, body low. The bowl serves as a focal point for directing the consumption, not as a receptacle.*

Maren remembered Thessa's test. She'd asked him to demonstrate a containment pass, then a residual clear, and he'd failed both so completely that she'd actually laughed, not cruelly, just the exhausted surprise of a professional watching someone demonstrate total ignorance of her field.

But he'd watched her hands while she laughed. The way she held them, loose, not rigid, the fingers not spread in the ceremonial way Aldric was describing but separated naturally, the way you'd hold your hands before lifting a cracked bowl and hoping it stayed whole.

*Lower,* Vess said. *Whatever you're doing with your back, stop. You look like you're praying. You're not praying.*

"Thanks."

*You're trying to eat it. Get lower. Your center of gravity needs to be beneath the contamination, not above it.*

This was the strange coalition. Aldric brought theory. The Order of the Veil had studied sin-eating mechanics from the bank, with measurements and nomenclature. Vess brought the body knowledge of someone who'd spent years learning how to move through spaces that fought back. And Thessa's remembered impatience filled the gaps with practical instruction stripped of both theory and grace.

Maren got lower. Mud and small stones pressed through his trousers at the pool's edge, cold and gritty. The bowl went in front of him on the flat stone where the quest-light was thickest. The light moved around the bowl's chipped rim like water parting, then closing.

The hum was louder here. A low-frequency pressure behind his ears that he'd heard his whole life without anyone else acknowledging it. In Fenhold it had been background noise. Here, this close to the pooled residue, it had a quality he'd never noticed, not steady but pulsing, a rhythm older than a heartbeat.

His hands opened. His fingers spread. Not the ceremonial spread but the fragile-object spread, the Thessa version.

And then he had to do the part he'd never done.

---

The posture was the easy part. He'd had eleven years to learn how to kneel, where to set the bowl, how long to hold still. Thessa's laughter in Silt had made that plain. The real thing required willingness underneath the shapes, an unguarded opening he had never practiced.

Thessa hadn't named it because she didn't think it needed naming. It was obvious to her, the way her own breathing was obvious. You opened yourself to the contamination. You let it in. Not metaphorically. The contamination was a physical presence with weight and texture and temperature, and the act of consumption was the act of taking that presence into your body and dissolving it there.

That meant letting the contamination in.

*The Order's theory,* Aldric said, and Maren could hear him choosing words carefully, the old stiffness in his speech pulled tight over strain, *describes the initial phase as a voluntary permeability. The sin-eater's consciousness must become receptive to the contamination's signature without resisting it. Resistance prevents dissolution.*

"So stop resisting."

*Yes.*

"Helpful."

*I have never claimed to be a practitioner. I am providing the theory.*

Vess: *Ignore the theory. Think about when you get hit. Your body tenses. It absorbs impact. This is the opposite. You need to not tense.*

That was closer. Not perfect, because nothing about this was going to be perfect, but closer. Maren looked at the pool. The quest-light sat in it, faintly warm, that ozone-sweet smell thickening in his throat. After eleven years of kneeling like this, his body knew the choreography by reflex: lean forward, murmur the ritual words he'd pieced together from scraps, place his hands on either side of the bowl, breathe in, breathe out, wait. In the old version, nothing happened during the wait. Maren held the pose until it felt like long enough, then stood up and told the family the contamination was cleared.

Now he leaned forward, and instead of murmuring the usual incantation he said nothing. Both hands went into the quest-light.

It was warmer than he'd expected. Not hot, warm the way skin was warm, body temperature, alive-feeling. The texture was wrong for light. It resisted his fingers with the gentle friction of wet ash, a substance that hadn't quite decided on a state. The hum shifted pitch when he touched it, dropping lower, and the smell surged, ozone cracking in the back of his throat like biting tin.

*Contamination levels are responding,* Aldric said. His voice had shifted, the professional reserve thinning into something more attentive. *The interface registers your interaction as a process I cannot translate cleanly. The System sees you touching the residue but cannot classify the interaction. The classification attempt is cycling. It is trying to assign you a role in the process and failing.*

"That's because I don't have one."

*Precisely. That absence matters. I am observing a structural response. Continue.*

Maren tried not to tense. Vess's instruction came back to him: the opposite of absorbing impact. Getting hit, your muscles locked up before the blow landed, your body smarter than you were, preparing for damage before the conscious mind even registered the threat. The opposite was letting the blow arrive without preparation. Taking the damage open, without the reflex that protected you from it.

So he let the quest-light in.

The first thing that happened was his stomach turned over. It wasn't nausea yet, just the precursor, the body's early warning that foreign residue was entering that didn't belong. The warmth from the pool crawled up his forearms and across his wrists, not on his skin but under it, a sensation like a fine wire threading through the muscle fibers of his arms. The ozone taste intensified until he could feel it in his sinuses, behind his eyes, a chemical sharpness that made his vision blur at the edges.

*The contamination signature is wrong. Hold. The lattice is—*

Aldric stopped.

The silence was immediate and total. Not the trailing-off of someone interrupted or distracted. One half-finished sentence, then nothing.

Maren knew that kind of nothing from the creek where the fragments had collided. Aldric had gone wordless there too, so abruptly the quiet had felt like a blow. This one came with a pressure behind Maren's eyes and a deeper drag in the hum, enough to make his teeth ache.

*Keep going,* Vess said, and her voice had gone careful. She'd noticed the silence too.

Maren kept going.

The contamination was inside him now. Only a thread of it, maybe less, the leading edge of the pool, but enough to register. It moved through his chest like a cold wire, settling against his ribs, and the wrongness of it was profound, a foreign presence in territory that his body recognized as occupied. His lungs pushed back. His actual breathing hitched, his diaphragm spasming as if he'd swallowed water wrong, and for three horrible seconds he couldn't get air in.

Then the thread dissolved. Not all of it, only a fraction, a wisp. The cold wire in his chest lost coherence, fraying into strands, and a different warmth spread from the center of him outward. Part of the contamination had been consumed.

His body rejected the rest.

It wasn't nausea. It was worse than nausea. Every muscle from his throat to his pelvis seized simultaneously, a full-body clench that drove him forward onto his hands, the bowl rocking on the flat stone beside him, creek water splashing up between his fingers. His vision whited out for a count of two, not darkness, whiteness, as if the nerve channels for sight had been flooded with too much light, and then it came back in pieces, the creek bed reassembling itself in fragments: mud, stone, the quest-light still pooling, his own hands flat in the shallow water with the fingers locked open, the tendons standing out like ropes.

Vomit tore out of him. Creek water and the hard bread from that morning and a bitter metallic slick that burned his throat coming up and left a film on his teeth. The retching came in waves, three of them, each pulling from deeper in his gut, and by the third there was nothing left to bring up and his body was just convulsing around emptiness, his back arching and his jaw locked open over the water.

When the retching stopped, the hum was quieter. Not by much, maybe a quarter of its previous volume, but the difference was obvious, the way you'd notice if someone turned down music you'd been listening to for hours. The pool had shrunk. The quest-light at its edges had receded, the pale shimmer pulling back from the banks by a handspan on each side, and the contamination smell had thinned from chemical sharpness to a stale after-smell, the ghost of what had been there.

A real clearing had happened. Not enough, not well, but real, and for the second time in his life nobody had died.

*Maren,* Vess said. Then, after a pause that was long for her: *You need to roll onto your side.*

---

Rolling onto his side wasn't happening. His arms wouldn't unlock, and his knees had found some arrangement with the creek-bed mud that was keeping him upright, and changing the arrangement seemed like a decision that could go wrong in ways he was too wrecked to calculate.

The tremors started in his hands and moved inward. Not shaking. Tremors, a different thing, a deep-muscle vibration that seemed to start in the bone, as if whatever anchored his muscles had been knocked loose and the muscles were trying to find their hold again. His fingers were still locked open, spread in Thessa's fragile-object position, and the tendons across the backs of his hands jumped and rippled in patterns that didn't correspond to any signal he was sending.

"That went great," he said. His voice was wrecked, thin and ragged, the sound of someone who'd been screaming, though he didn't remember screaming.

*You screamed,* Vess confirmed. *About midway through. Derry is standing approximately thirty feet away and hasn't moved since it started.*

Maren turned his head slowly because the creek bed was doing unreliable things with its distances, the stones shifting their relative positions in ways that made his inner ear rebel. Derry was at the tree line, exactly where he'd been when this started, his arms crossed over his chest. Maybe it was concern. Maybe it was him holding himself together. The distance between those two readings was the distance Derry had traveled from Maren in the past two days.

"I'm fine," Maren said. It was the most obviously false thing he'd said since the last time he'd told a family their contamination was cleared.

*Roll onto your side.* Vess again, and the patience in her voice still didn't sound like Vess. *If you pass out facedown over the creek, you'll inhale water. It's about four inches deep. That's enough.*

Eventually Maren rolled onto his side. The mud was cold and smelled like iron and crushed leaves and the fading contamination. There, with his cheek against the wet ground, he let the tremors work through him, the deep-muscle vibration loosening its hold on his ribs and his forearms and the long muscles along his spine in slow, stuttering increments.

*You're shivering,* Vess said. *That's your body trying to regulate temperature after the consumption. It'll pass in a few minutes. Keep your knees drawn up. It conserves heat and keeps your airway open if you vomit again.*

"How do you know this?"

A pause. Then: *I was trained to understand what bodies do when they've been pushed past capacity. The specifics are different. Recovery protocol's similar.*

Some observation about the gap between assassination training and sin-eating recovery tried to form, but his throat was too raw and the thought came apart before it reached his mouth.

Aldric was still silent.

The quiet sat in the back of his skull like a thumb pressed there. No commentary. No correction. Just that dense pressure and the low hum under it.

"Aldric."

Nothing.

"What did you see?"

Nothing.

*He won't answer,* Vess said. There was no irritation in it. She was reporting a fact. *Maybe he's still with whatever made him stop. Maybe he just doesn't want to say it yet.*

"Is that a problem?"

*Depends what it was.*

Maren pressed his cheek harder into the mud. The cold was organizing, giving his senses a specific and local thing to hold instead of the diffuse wrongness the consumption had left behind, a residual unsteadiness in the center of his chest where the contamination had partially dissolved and partially been expelled. The place where the cold wire had frayed felt tender, raw, like the inside of a healing burn.

"How much did I clear?"

*Maybe a fifth of the pool. Hard to estimate without System data.* A beat. *Not bad for someone who's literally never done this before.*

"A fifth."

*Of a very small pool. A training pool. Not the kind of contamination that collects outside active dungeon mouths.*

"Right."

*But not nothing.*

In the mud, he let that sit. That wasn't nothing. A fifth of a small pool, cleared through a combination of remembered instruction and borrowed instinct and whatever counted as sincerity in a man who'd spent his adult life perfecting the art of looking sincere, and the physical cost came clear as he inventoried it: hands still trembling, chest tender where the contamination had threaded through and been rejected, throat raw from screaming and vomiting, muscles in his back and sides aching with the deep soreness of recent seizure, a persistent wobble in his vision when he moved his eyes too fast. The rest of the day was going to be a bad walk. Maybe longer.

But a fifth of the pool was gone. The part of the creek bed where the quest-light had receded looked different, the stones paler, closer to their natural color, the water running over them without the sluggish quality the contamination gave everything it touched.

*Drink water,* Vess said. *Slowly. If it comes back up, wait and try again.*

His water skin was in his pack, on the flat stone where he'd left it, six feet away. Getting up to reach it seemed like the kind of ambitious project that required planning and maybe a committee.

Derry brought it to him.

He appeared at the edge of Maren's vision, crossing the distance from the tree line. At Maren's head he crouched and set the water skin on the ground without quite touching him, his hand steady, his face, from this angle and through mud-smeared vision, closed in a way Maren recognized from years of reading faces professionally.

"Drink it slow, not — Pell had a fever last spring and she kept trying to gulp water and it came right back up, and I kept saying just sip it, just a little, but—" He stopped. His mouth closed over whatever came next, and he stood and went back to the tree line.

Maren drank. Slowly, the way Vess had said. The water tasted like leather and the copper residue that still coated the inside of his mouth, and his throat closed against it once before accepting. It stayed down.

"I can do this," he said. Not to Derry. Not entirely to Vess, though she was the one who answered.

*You can do this badly. Which is better than not doing it at all, and worse than doing it well. The gap between where you are and where a real sin-eater is could kill you.*

"Yeah."

*I'm not saying don't. I'm saying know what you're risking.*

Time passed in the creek bed. The tremors eased. The mud dried on his cheek in a thin crust that cracked when he finally sat up, pulling himself upright against the flat stone where his pack sat. His ceramic bowl was still there, the quest-light residue drying on its surface in a faint, flaking film. The bowl came up in his shaking hands. It was still an ordinary bowl, chipped at the rim, but now the pale crust on the glaze marked work his body had actually done.

The ritual had changed the ground in front of him. His body was wrecked and a fifth of a small pool was cleared and Aldric had cut off mid-sentence and stayed that way and Derry was still at the tree line with his face turned down the road. The contamination smell that had been thick and chemical when Maren knelt down was thinner now. The air above the pool smelled more like mud and water and the first edge of autumn in the leaves overhead.

The rest of the pool still sat in the hollow, patient as old debt. A fifth gone meant four fifths left. Thessa would have called that the sort of result that still ruined wells and made children cough through the night. The creek was safer by a handspan and still not safe.

The bowl went back into its cloth, his fingers fumbling the edges, the fine tremor still visible in his hands. Too much time went into tucking it back into the pack because the alternative was looking at Derry, who had turned his face down the road toward the Crook.

"If you're going to do that again," Derry said without turning, "tell me before if I'm meant to drag you out or leave you there. I stood here the whole time trying to decide, and I kept thinking if I touched you wrong I'd make it worse."

Maren tightened the knot on the cloth. "If I stop breathing, drag me."

Derry rubbed a hand over his mouth. "And if you're breathing and still doing that?"

"Then let me finish."

Derry nodded once. "Say it first next time."

Maren looked at his back and nearly said he was sorry. The word never made it out. Sorry was too small for a creek bed full of proof.

*When he talks again,* Vess said, meaning Aldric. Her voice had gone careful, each word set down one at a time. *Listen.*

"Why?"

*Because he stopped talking when you touched the pool,* she said. *And the hum hasn't sounded right since.*

Maren stood up. His legs held, barely, the muscles in his thighs protesting through a sustained wobble that he managed by leaning on the flat stone and then on his pack and then on nothing, standing free for a moment, swaying, before his balance decided to cooperate.

He looked once at the thinned edge of the pool. Water ran over those stones with an ordinary creek sound. The center still carried that low drag against the back of his teeth, but the strip he'd managed to clear had gone quiet enough that he could hear the trickle underneath.

"Let's go," he said.

Derry was already walking. Twenty feet ahead, the distance maintained, the silence still pulled tight between them.

Aldric said nothing.

Maren shouldered the pack and followed. Walking hurt. The first few steps told him exactly how much. His calves twitched without warning. A stab caught under his ribs every time he breathed too deep. When he tightened his right hand on the pack strap, two fingers lagged behind like they belonged to a slower body. None of it felt dramatic. It felt workmanlike, the kind of damage you could keep moving on now and pay for at dark.

Derry kept his twenty feet. The bowl knocked warm against Maren's spine, and under the hum he'd heard all his life another note kept dragging lower, steady enough by the next rise that he couldn't pretend he wasn't hearing it any longer.


# Chapter 12

The hum had changed.

It wasn't louder. Maren had been hearing the System's hum since he was old enough to lie awake wondering what it was, and it had always sat at the same low pitch, ambient and ignorable, like wind or someone else's heartbeat through a shared wall. But since the consumption at the creek bed, the real one, the one that left him on his hands and knees with his mouth tasting like pennies and ozone while Vess talked him through the shaking, the pitch had dropped. There was something underneath the sound he'd always known. A vibration that sat in his back teeth and the flat bones of his skull.

He'd tried to ignore it for two days. Two days of walking through scrub oak and exposed shale, deeper into dungeon territory than he'd ever been, with the road no longer pretending to be a road. Two days of Aldric's silence. These were not his usual silences, the pointed ones, the annoyed ones, the ones that came loaded with moral judgment. This was the silence from the consumption. The one that started when Maren's eyes were rolling back and his hands were cramping into claws around the ceramic bowl. Aldric had seen something during the ritual and he'd stopped talking and he hadn't started again, not really.

Derry was asleep across the dead fire, curled on his side with his pack wedged under his head. He'd turned in an hour ago, said he was tired, which was accurate in the way most of Derry's stated reasons were accurate without being the reason. Derry slept facing the road now. He'd been doing that since Caul. His back to Maren, his breath even, his body aimed in the direction of home.

The first cool night of the season. It wasn't cold yet, but the air carried an edge the summer air hadn't, a sharpness that cut through the contamination smell, which was itself different this deep. It smelled less sweet and more mineral. The ozone undercurrent that usually signaled quest residue had thickened into something almost geological, like the air over limestone when it rained.

Maren sat against a birch trunk, the bark a pattern of rough and smooth against his shoulder blades, and watched the embers die. His body had mostly recovered from the consumption. His hands were steady. His stomach had settled around midday. But the hum sat in his molars and something about the night, the thin quality of the starlight, the way quest-light bled above the southern horizon like a stain on wet paper, made the ordinary act of sitting still feel precarious. As if the ground under him was not quite solid.

*You should rest.* Aldric. The words had the flat, procedural quality of a man checking a box, concern filed and duty noted.

"You've been quiet for two days," Maren said. Low, because of Derry. "Not your normal quiet. Not pissed-off quiet or disgusted-with-my-choices quiet. This is the quiet from the consumption, and I'm done waiting for you to come out of it on your own."

The hum sat in his teeth while the embers went grey.

"Aldric."

*I know.*

"Then talk to me. What did you see?"

*What I have to say will change the shape of things. I am telling you that before I begin because once I say it, it does not unsay. I have been trying to put it in order for two days. It resists order.*

"That's your problem. Start anyway."

Another silence. Then Aldric's voice, and something in it Maren had never heard. Not the formality, the sarcasm, the controlled grief, or the careful way he laid every sentence out like a report. There was something raw under all that. The rough catch in a man's breath when he bites down on pain and talks anyway.

*When I first entered your consciousness, I thought your System-blindness was absence. Missing faculty. A dark room with no lamp. That was wrong. The System did not behave around you the way it behaves around absence. It kept... sliding.*

"Sliding how?"

*Off you. Around you. Failing to find purchase. I noted it. I filed it as an irregularity. I should not have filed it.*

Maren's hands went flat on his thighs. His fingers pressed the seam of his trousers, each thread under them clear and separate.

"You're doing that thing where you make it sound precise and useless at the same time."

*Very well. You keep using the wrong word for yourself. Blind suggests the thing is there and you cannot perceive it. That is not what is happening. The System reaches for you and cannot get hold. It cannot write on you.*

Maren looked over at Derry's sleeping back, then back at the fire that wasn't fire anymore, just a soft bed of ash.

"So what, I'm immune now?"

Aldric took a beat before answering.

*If you require the blunt word, yes.*

Immunity was the word.

The word did not reach back and fix anything. It did not fix the years Derry had stood half a step behind him at rituals, coughing once for green prompt and twice for red because the village sin-eater could not see the damn residue over a corpse. It did not fix the market habit of tapping prices into Maren's palm because numbers in the air were for other people. It did not fix his mother's face the first time he told her there was nothing there, no glow, no prompt, no class-mark, and watched her decide that careful gentleness was going to be the rest of her life with him.

"That is a clean word for a filthy life," he said.

*I did not say it spared you anything. I said the diagnosis was wrong.*

"Fine. Then use the right one."

The hum pulsed again. It came up through the shale and the tree and into his jaw.

*The interface every other person sees from birth is not the thing itself. It is a surface. An inner surface. A wall.*

"A wall to what?"

*Containment.*

It was one word, plain enough that Maren almost missed it.

"No. Don't do that. You don't get to drop one priest-word into the fire and leave me to sort out the ashes."

For once Aldric did not bristle.

*I have been watching from the only vantage I think this world allows. Inside a consciousness the System cannot touch, I can see seams. Borders. Places where the visible interface meets structure. During the consumption at the creek bed, when you actually opened yourself to it, I saw the structure flex. I saw where contamination comes from. I saw pressure on the far side of the wall.*

*The hum changed then,* Vess said. *He's not imagining that.*

Aldric went on as if each word had to be worked past a mouthful of grit.

*Everyone else sees classifications, levels, prompts. They see wallpaper. I am seeing what is under the paper. The thing those surfaces were built to hide. The System is not arranging the world, Maren. It is holding something in place.*

"You're sure that's what you're seeing," Maren said. "Not just the kind of story a dead paladin tells himself when the rules stop making sense."

The answer did not come quickly.

*I am sure about the seams. I am sure about the pressure. I am sure the System fails at your edges in ways it should not. What lies beyond the wall, I cannot name with certainty. Containment is observation. Motive is hypothesis.*

"What's on the other side?"

His answer came slower than anything Maren had heard from him. It wasn't reluctance. It was more like a man groping elbow-deep through muddy water for something small and important.

*I do not know. Not in any way I can classify, which is part of the problem. It does not fit the categories the System uses because it is older than the categories. Older, wilder, and pressing. I can see where the containment has thinned. I can see where something behind it pushes back.*

The vibration hit his teeth hard enough that he had to unclench his jaw.

"And me? Where do I fit in that?"

*You do not fit. That is the point. The System writes itself onto human consciousness at birth. Interface, classifications, all of it. Like dye taking cloth. It has been trying to do that to you since the day you were born and it keeps failing.*

"So I'm what. Bad cloth."

*No. A place the dye does not take. A place the wall does not close cleanly near active mouths and heavy contamination. That is where System-blindness appears. Not at random. At strain points.*

Maren's laugh came out thin and mean.

"Careful. You're about half a sentence from calling me a crack in the world."

*If the phrase is offensive, take that up with the architecture. I am describing where you stand in it, not complimenting you.*

He should have had something ready for that. Some easy answer. Some deflection that would let the whole thing slide past without sticking. Instead he sat there with his hands on his knees and the hum in his bones and thought of every person who had ever spoken to the air above his shoulder because Derry was the one who could tell them whether a prompt had changed color.

Vess spoke into the quiet.

*I can feel it too. Since the creek bed. Not all the pretty wall language. Just pressure. Like something leaning when it ought to stay put.*

Aldric answered her, but he was really answering Maren.

*What formed when Vess entered you, what the System named unclassified, is not just a classification error. From where I am looking, it reads like widening stress. The collision between us inside a host the System cannot write on has made the breach worse.*

"The bounty."

The thought arrived whole. The same sick speed old fraud-logic used to arrive with, a pattern seen all at once from outside.

"It's not — " He stopped. "The bounty isn't punishment. It's maintenance. I'm the leak and it's trying to close me up."

*Most likely.* A pause. *The corrections. The heroes. Repair attempts. I cannot prove intent, but the behavior fits repair better than punishment.*

"With me in it."

*Yes.*

Maren swallowed. His throat worked dry.

"At the creek bed I felt something push back. I thought that was the contamination or me about to throw up."

*You did throw up,* Vess said. *Those are not mutually exclusive.*

For one breath, absurdly, he nearly smiled.

Then Aldric said, *You touched the structure itself.*

The smile died.

*Contamination is erosion made material. Sin-eating is maintenance work. That much I would stake my name on.* The report-cadence stumbled. *The rest is theory. But when you opened yourself properly, you touched the seam from the wrong side. The bowl narrowed it. Gave the opening a —* He stopped. *A boundary. I watched it happen and I do not have the framework to explain what I saw.*

Maren looked toward his pack without meaning to. The ceramic bowl was wrapped in cloth inside it. Same chipped rim. Same market glaze. Nothing about it looked equal to the sound in his teeth.

"Thessa's been doing cleanup blind."

*From inside it,* Aldric said. *She knows the work. She does not know the architecture. I do not think anyone alive does.*

The night bird had not called again. The scrub oak stood black against the stained horizon, each branch a fracture in the faint quest-light. Derry's breathing was even and unknowing, the breath of a man who still got to think the world broke in ordinary ways. Bad weather. Bad roads. A sick cow. A hero with a grudge.

Maren sat with it.

---

He sat with it long enough for the ash in the fire pit to lose every last vein of orange.

Vess did not speak. Her silence had a different quality from Aldric's. It was less pressurized, more watchful. Maren was grateful for it. The revelation sat in his chest like something swallowed too fast, all corners.

He picked at the birch bark behind him. Pulled a strip free and turned it between his fingers until it tore.

"The Order of the Veil," he said.

Aldric's silence shifted. Became the kind of quiet that falls over a card table when someone finally names the cheat.

"You reached for me on purpose."

*Yes.*

"Why."

*Because I was already looking for people like you.*

"People like me," Maren repeated. "Meaning what."

*The Order investigated anomalies. Misclassifications. Bounty errors. Places where the interface showed seams it should not show. I was assigned to that work. Later I asked for more of it.*

"And somewhere in all that righteous paperwork you found the blind ones."

*The System-blind,* Aldric said automatically, then, a moment later, *yes.*

He kept going before Maren could bite at him again.

*I found a pattern first. Not names. Birth clusters near active mouths. High contamination regions. Interface distortion around specific individuals. Then I found older records. Very old. Copies of copies. Bad copies. They treated System-blindness not as failed integration, but as structural irregularity. Stress marks in the containment.*

"Fenhold."

*Yes.*

"You came to inspect the stress mark."

This time Aldric did bristle.

*I came to investigate a pattern that might have explained what the System was hiding.*

"Near enough."

The birch bark gave under his thumb. Split into white curls that clung to his skin.

*I requested survey assignment to the dungeon mouth nearest Fenhold. I told the Order it was a routine structural assessment. It was not routine. I wanted proximity to a confirmed System-blind host and an active site.*

"And then the site killed you."

The answer took time.

*The site was stable when we entered. Three independent assessments confirmed it. Four hours into the survey, the dungeon's classifications changed in real time. Walls marked load-bearing became pass-through. Supports redesignated. Stone that should have held dissolved under new rules. The dungeon did not fail geologically. It was redefined into collapse.*

"By the System."

*That is what it looked like.*

"Aldric."

The name stopped him.

"Did the System kill you for looking too hard."

When Aldric answered, the old report-cadence was still there, but wearing through.

*I cannot prove intent.* A silence. *I can say the changes were targeted. I can say no other site in the region shifted that day. I can say I was —*

He stopped. Started again, quieter.

*I was buried alive. In the deepest part of the architecture I had ever seen. If you want the plain version. Yes. It may have killed me for looking too hard.*

May have was not certainty. Somehow that was worse.

"And after that you climbed into my skull because it was the only place your investigation could keep breathing."

*Yes.*

There was no padding around it this time, no tidy framing, just yes.

"Without asking."

*I could not ask.*

"You could've left me alone."

*I could have ceased. I did not.*

He waited for Aldric to dress it up. He did not.

Then, after a silence long enough to feel chosen, Aldric said, *I told myself embedding in you would let me observe without changing the fault itself. That was convenient. It was also wrong.*

That was the one that hit.

The back of Maren's throat flooded sour. He got up too fast, turned away from the fire pit, and bent over with his palms braced on his knees. Spit hit the shale. It was not enough to vomit. That was worse than vomiting. He stayed there, breathing through his mouth, until the taste of acid thinned. Then he shoved both hands into his hair and held on. If he could have taken the top of his skull off like a lid and shaken both of them into the dirt, he would have.

When he straightened, he went to his pack, dug through cloth and stale bread and spare shirt, and dragged out the ceramic bowl.

Derry shifted once in his sleep, then settled.

Maren came back to the dead fire with the bowl in both hands.

"Before we talk about getting anybody out," he said, "prove one damned thing."

No one answered for a beat.

Then Aldric said, *Proof may be too strong a word.*

"Then we're done here."

*There is one part we can test,* Aldric said, fast enough that Maren heard the strain under the formality. *Not the wall. Not what is beyond it. The bowl. Set it in the ash.*

*Small test,* Vess said. *If your fingers start locking, stop. If your teeth feel like they're humming from the roots, stop sooner.*

Maren stared down at the bowl. It was cheap ceramic. Two old chips roughened the rim, both long since glued back with pine resin. His thumb found one of the repaired seams automatically.

"You chose me," he said. "You got in. Since then it's been — bounty hunters, and Caul, and people dead because I left villages dirty, and — " He couldn't get past Derry. "That thing at the creek bed nearly killed me and you're still hiding behind the investigation."

*The investigation matters,* Aldric said, and there was the old armor again, thin and familiar and infuriating.

*No,* Vess said. Quiet. Flat. *Not that part first. He knows it matters to you. Say the other part. You picked him anyway.*

Aldric did not answer.

Vess added, *He's allowed to hate that.*

Maren gave one short laugh that was not laughter.

"Good. Somebody in here can still count."

He set the bowl in the center of the dead fire pit instead. Ash shifted under the base with a dry whisper. The stone ring was still warm in two places if he held his palm low enough over it, heat hiding under the grey the way embers hid under a kicked-over fire.

The bowl looked stupid there. Market ceramic in a fire pit three days from anywhere sensible. Thin glaze, cheap curve, one hairline scratch running from the chip toward the inside. The kind of thing you'd use for oats or onions or washing blood off your hands if you had no better basin. Not a tool for anything built into the bones of the world.

"What do I do."

*What you did at the creek bed,* Aldric said. *Less of it. Do not take anything in. Just open enough for the pressure to notice the shape.*

"That sentence means nothing."

*It means the thing you did by instinct and nearly died from,* Vess said. *Do the first inch of it. Not the rest.*

His mouth went wet at once. Not with hunger. With memory. Copper under the tongue, the awful full-body warning that came before the real heave. His hands knew before the rest of him did. The old fake motions had always been theater: thumb on rim, two fingers under base, head bowed at an angle meant to read devout from a grieving family's doorway. The real opening had nothing to do with that. It was lower. Meaner. A slackening somewhere behind the breastbone, like unlatching a gate you had spent years leaning your whole weight against.

He hated that his body remembered.

Derry breathed in, breathed out. Soft. Unaware. A sleeping man ten feet away while Maren crouched over a dead fire trying to let just enough of the wrong thing touch him to prove a dead paladin wasn't just wrong in an expensive way.

"If this wakes him," Maren muttered, "I'm blaming both of you."

Neither answered.

He crouched. One hand on the rim. One hand braced on his own knee because the shale had started its slow tilt again, the not-quite-level feeling that came before his balance went treacherous. He let his shoulders loosen by half an inch.

The hum changed immediately.

Not louder. Narrower.

The sound that had been spread through the ground and his jaw and the back of his eyes drew inward as if someone had taken a wide, blunt knife and turned it edgewise. His molars stopped ringing all at once. The silence there was so abrupt it hurt. The ache moved into his palm instead. Ceramic against skin, and under the glaze a vibration so clean and steady it made the tendons in his wrist tighten.

Nothing happened in the pit for one breath.

Then the ash nearest the bowl twitched.

Not from wind. The night was still enough that the birch leaves held their faces flat. Not from Maren's shaking either. The tremor in him was fine and fast and private. This was different. A grey line of ash no wider than a bootlace lifted, slid across the blackened stone, and laid itself against the outer curve of the bowl.

He froze.

Another line followed. Then three more, each one dragging a faint trail through the soot, until the dead fire looked combed. Ash from opposite sides of the pit moved inward in slow dry threads and began collecting against the ceramic. When the first little heap hit the chip in the rim, it climbed. It should not have climbed. Dry ash did not crawl uphill against glazed pottery. This did. Grain by grain, patient as frost, until a dark crescent built itself along the inside of the bowl.

Maren's throat closed.

*There,* Aldric said, and for once the word held no lecture, only naked relief. *It narrows when given a boundary.*

*No breeze,* Vess said. *No hand in the ash. That's real.*

The bowl grew heavier.

Not truly heavy. Still cheap ceramic, still something he could crack by dropping on stone. But his hand received it as if weight were accumulating from someplace that did not respect distance. The vibration in the glaze deepened until it matched the pulse that had lived in his teeth for days. A smell came off it too, sudden and intimate: wet limestone, pennies, the sweet-rotten edge of contamination stripped of all its village disguises. Not grave flowers. Not blessed oil. Not the things he'd taught himself to expect while lying to widows. This smell was cave water over old metal. Ground opened with something dead in it.

The southward stain of quest-light shivered.

It did not vanish. It narrowed, same as the hum had, pulling from a broad smear into one brighter thread for the space of a heartbeat before spreading again. Small change. Brief. But Maren had been staring at that stain half the night, and he knew what it had looked like a blink earlier.

His fingers started to cramp.

Not the full seizure from the creek bed. Nothing that violent. Just the first treacherous drawing-in, tendons shortening, thumb wanting to lock against the rim. He snatched his hand back.

The ash fell at once.

The little crescent inside the bowl collapsed with a whisper. The threads on the stone loosened into ordinary mess. The hum rushed back into his teeth hard enough to make him gasp through them. His stomach lurched. He swallowed it down and tasted acid anyway.

*Enough,* Vess said.

Maren sat back on his heels. The shale bit through his boots. He kept both hands spread wide on his thighs so nobody could mistake the shaking for agreement.

The bowl sat in the ash with a charcoal smear inside it. Nothing miraculous now. Just cheap ceramic dirtied by a campfire. But the smear was wrong. A crescent line, higher on one side than the other, like something had leaned there from within.

"What," he said carefully, because if he didn't say it carefully he'd start shouting and Derry would wake, "does that prove."

Aldric answered in the same stripped voice.

*It proves the vessel changes the behavior of the pressure when you open. It proves the bowl was not incidental at the creek bed. It proves there is a difference between leakage diffused through a place and leakage given shape.*

"And the rest."

*The wall is still the best model I have,* Aldric said. *What presses against it, what the System intends, whether this bowl can separate anything or repair anything, those remain inferences.*

Maren looked from the bowl to Derry's sleeping back and then south again where the quest-light had already gone broad and ugly as before.

That was the part that settled it. Not the lecture. Not the old Order records. Not even the ash climbing the bowl, though that would be living in his head a while. It was the way both of them, finally, had run out of certainty in the same place. They had one true thing and a pile of guesses around it, and the one true thing was dangerous enough to drag ash uphill and start locking his hand after a few breaths.

He reached in and picked the bowl up by the base. It had gone cool again. Almost innocent.

"All right," he said.

He sat down again, not against the birch this time. On bare shale, the stone biting cold through his trousers. The bowl stayed in his lap. Ash marked the inside in a crooked grey crescent. His hands had started shaking, fine and fast, a tremor running from thumb to wrist.

"Here's what happens now," he said. "You see something in there, you tell me when it happens. Not two days later when you've turned it into a report. Not after you've decided what I can handle. When it happens."

Aldric was quiet long enough that Maren thought he might refuse.

Then: *Agreed.*

"And I'm not putting this to my mouth again blind." Maren tightened his grip on the bowl until the glaze bit his palm. "Not for your Order. Not for your wall. Not for whatever — " He stopped. Drew a breath through his teeth. "It just pulled ash uphill because I breathed wrong for three seconds, Aldric. I'm not improvising with that. We get to the Crook, we find Thessa, somebody who's actually been doing the work for thirty years looks at this and tells me what I'm holding. Then maybe the Woundmouth."

*It is the thinnest site,* Aldric said, automatically, because of course that was the first place his mind went.

"Good. Then it'll still be there after the Crook."

*It nearly turned your hands inside out last time,* Vess said. *He's right.*

That shut Aldric up.

Somewhere in the scrub oak, a branch cracked under its own weight and fell. Derry shifted again, one hand coming free of the blanket, palm-up on the shale, fingers loosely curled. The hand of a man who planted things and pulled weeds and held his daughter while she counted to ten and missed seven every time. A hand that had spent eleven years feeding lies to Maren at exactly the right moment so the village could keep believing. A hand that did not belong anywhere near a camp where ash could climb a bowl because Maren breathed wrong for three seconds.

Maren looked south. The Woundmouth was out there somewhere beyond the dark ridges, quest-light bleeding into the sky instead of pooling. The Crook was between here and there. Thessa was between here and there. If the bowl could answer him at a dead campsite, if one sloppy half-opening could make the ground answer back, then the road had narrowed hard. Thessa first. The Woundmouth after. There was no room left for pretending and even less for guessing.

The ceramic was cool at first. Then, slowly, under his palms, it picked up the same low vibration that lived in his teeth.

The ash mark inside it held.

He sat with the bowl in his lap and the terms he'd just set hanging in the dark between all three of them. No one argued. The hum kept on, deep and patient under the ground, while the night thinned one grain at a time around Derry's sleeping back and the southward stain of quest-light, and the wrong little crescent of ash stayed where the ceramic had taught it to stay.


# Chapter 13

The first cool morning came with mist. Not the stinging wet of a storm front but a thinner drift, like breath caught between the hills and held there. Maren woke with his back against a stone wall, the remains of a waymarker shelter, three walls and no roof, and found Derry already up with his pack on his knees, facing the road.

He wasn't facing the fire. He was facing the road.

Maren catalogued it the way he'd catalogued a hundred tells across eleven years of fraud: the set of Derry's shoulders, the packed bag, the direction of his attention. The grammar of a man preparing to move. He filed it and said nothing.

"Morning," Derry said, without turning. "There's water in the skin. I boiled some earlier. It should still be warm. Warmish."

"Thanks."

Maren drank. The water tasted like the iron pot Derry carried, which tasted like every meal they'd shared since Fenhold. He sat down across the dead fire and pulled his boots on, working the left one over the blister on his heel that had gone from raw to calloused over nineteen days.

*He packed before he built the fire,* Aldric observed. *The fire was an afterthought. The pack was first.*

Maren didn't answer. The paladin had been quieter since his own revelation, the System as containment, the wall, the crack, architecture visible only from inside a blind man's skull. When he did speak, each sentence sounded expensive. The constant sarcasm had thinned. What replaced it was worse: sincerity.

*I merely note the sequence,* Aldric said. *You have already drawn your own conclusion.*

"So," Maren said aloud, rubbing his hands against the chill. The air smelled different this morning, less of baked-road dust and more of green at the edge of rot. "How far do you figure we are from the Crook?"

Derry's shoulders shifted. "Three days, maybe. Four, if the road keeps going to shit. I asked at that farm stand yesterday, the woman with the bad teeth. She said the valley's about two days past the split, but I don't know which split she meant."

"The one by the creek crossing, probably."

"Right. Yeah. Probably."

Silence. Usually Derry would've stuffed it with weather or bread or a story that didn't need telling. This time he kept facing the road.

*Vess,* Maren thought. *You're quiet too.*

*Listening,* she said.

They packed and walked. The road between Harrow and the Crook was a generous word for it, a track beaten into the ground by sin-eater boots and survey teams, pocked with old scorch marks from hero patrols. The land rose and fell in long swells. Trees thickened. Quest-light pooled in the ditches like rainwater lit from underneath. Maren could see the raw shimmer. He just couldn't see the data everyone else took for granted.

The hum was louder here, a low drone behind his teeth and in the hinge of his jaw. System activity, Aldric had explained. The infrastructure maintaining itself. The sound of the wall holding.

Derry walked a half-step ahead.

Maren knew what that meant. He'd built a life reading the geometry of bodies, the angle of a shoulder, the extra pace of distance. It was how he'd faked rituals for eleven years: not through knowledge of the System but through knowledge of people. Derry's body was saying what his mouth had not said yet.

At midday they stopped at a stream crossing where water ran clear over flat stones. Derry pulled out bread and the end of a hard cheese he'd traded for at the farm stand. He handed Maren his portion without meeting his eyes, then sat on a rock facing downstream.

"Cheese isn't bad," Maren said.

"Yeah, she said it was sheep, maybe goat. One of those." Derry bit bread, chewed, kept watching the water. "Pell would've liked this. We found her elbow-deep in the flour bin the week before we left. White from the wrists up. She looked proud as hell about it."

Maren could picture it too easily: the white hands, the startled face, a swallowed laugh while somebody hauled her out. The story should have warmed the space between them. Instead it warned him.

"Little ghost," he said.

"Yeah." Derry swallowed. "Yeah."

The word died there.

*He still hasn't looked at you once,* Vess said.

*I know.*

*You're not going to say anything.*

*No.*

*That's either kind or cowardly. I can't tell which.*

They walked through the afternoon. The road got worse: deeper ruts, older scorch marks, ground stained by years of quest-light exposure. The trees had started to turn. One branch had gone yellow in a whole green canopy, like a warning somebody forgot to take down.

Derry tried to make a joke about the smell, the ozone-sweet contamination stink hanging heavier in the low ground, and laughed before he finished it. Maren gave it what he could. That died fast too.

*The road forks in roughly a mile,* Aldric said. *The left branch descends toward the valley she described. I can see increased System density in that direction. The interface thickens.*

"There's a fork coming up," Maren relayed. "Left takes us down toward the Crook."

"Right," Derry said. "Good."

He said it like a man hearing results he had already braced himself for.

They made camp before the fork in a clearing with a fire ring built from flat stones. The ground was tramped down from other travelers, maybe sin-eaters, maybe nobody worth knowing. Derry gathered wood with more force than the work needed. Maren watched him from the fire and tried to remember the last real question Derry had asked him.

It had been three days, maybe four. That was before Aldric's revelation, before Maren had sat in the dark with containment structure, wall, crack, you are the gap ringing through him. Derry had asked whether Maren thought they'd ever go back to Fenhold. Maren, still shaken and honest in the wrong direction, had said he didn't know if the man who left could go back to it.

Derry hadn't asked another question after that.

The fire caught and they ate dried meat, the last of the cheese, water from the stream. Derry faced the road. Maren faced the fire and let the arrangement speak for itself.

"I was thinking," Derry said.

Maren's hands went still around the water skin.

"About logistics. How far the Crook is. What happens when you get there with the sin-eaters, and the examination, or whatever Thessa's going to do. I was thinking about what makes sense. Going forward."

His voice kept restarting. He pushed the fire with a stick, adding clauses and backing away from them.

"Derry."

"Because the thing is, I know this isn't, I don't want you to think I was, I wasn't trying to."

"Derry."

He stopped. Sweat stood on his neck despite the cold. His mouth kept trying to settle and couldn't.

"Just say it," Maren said. He kept his voice warm. The con man's gift: giving people permission to do the thing they were going to do anyway and making it feel almost clean.

"I can't come to the Crook."

There it was. Maren's chest went heavy, not the false anguish he'd faked over strangers' dead, just a slow settling, like a door closing in a room he'd known was being emptied.

"Yeah," Maren said. "I know."

Derry folded in on himself with relief. The stick dropped from his hand. "Shit, Maren, I've been trying for days. I've been trying."

"I know."

"Pell's turning three while we're out here. I won't be there. Lenna's alone with her, and every extra day I stay north I keep thinking about that instead of the road in front of me."

"She needs her father," Maren said.

Derry looked at him. His eyes were wet, but the tears held. "I talked to Caul."

The heaviness in Maren's chest went cold. The farm stand, the packed bag, Derry facing the road since morning. None of it was loose anymore.

"When."

"Two days ago. At the farm stand. The woman was showing you that map and I went around back. He was there. Or he found me, more like. He must've been tracking us."

*Obviously,* Vess said. *Caul is a ranger.*

*Be quiet,* Maren thought.

*I'm stating operational fact,* she said.

*Be quiet.*

She went quiet.

"What did you tell him," Maren said. It wasn't a question. His voice had gone flat, the performance dropping out before he could stop it.

"I didn't sell you out. I didn't tell him where we camped or how fast we were moving. I told him the direction. That we were heading for the Crook. That's all." Derry gripped his knees. "He already knew where you were going. He wanted me to say it out loud."

"And he gave you safe passage."

"He said I could head south. The bounty's on you, not me. If I walked that way, he'd make sure no hero patrol gave me trouble." Derry swallowed. "He said Pell deserved a father who was alive."

Caul had used her name. Derry must have given it to him. Maren tasted that like metal.

"I'm sorry," Derry said. "Maren, you're my best friend. We've been doing this since we were kids. I wouldn't if I had another way."

"Don't."

"I just don't know what else to do."

"Did you tell him about the fork?"

Derry blinked. "What?"

"The split by the creek. Left branch. Did you give him that too?"

"Yeah." Derry's voice shrank. "I said left. I said three days if the road held, four if it got worse. I told him that's what you'd heard. That was all. I swear it."

The stone in Maren's palm turned once. Left fork. Three or four days. Enough to stop Caul ranging wide and start him walking like a man who knew where the road narrowed.

*He no longer has to search every approach,* Aldric said. *He can commit to the valley.*

*Or wait where the road pinches and let you come to him,* Vess said.

Derry scrubbed both hands over his face. "I wasn't trying to make it easier for him."

"No." Maren looked into the fire. "You were trying to go back."

Derry nodded once.

"Leave before light," Maren said. "Go south from the fork and keep going. If you see a patrol before the main road, use Caul's name before they decide to make a story out of it."

Derry stared at him. "You're giving me instructions."

"I'm telling you how not to die between here and Fenhold."

Mist started creeping back into the trees. Quest-light in the ditches showed through it, pale and blurred. The hum pressed harder at the roots of Maren's teeth. The cut cheese sat between them on a flat stone and sweated in the firelight.

"When are you going," Maren said.

"In the morning. Before light, probably. If I head south from the fork I can make the main road in a day and a half. After that it's just walking." Derry pulled his knees up. He looked worn down to the bone, like relief had reached him and found no space to sit because guilt was taking up the room.

"Take the cheese knife," Maren said. "And the extra water skin. And that fire-starting kit you made in Harrow."

"I'm not taking your things."

"I'll be at the Crook in three days. There'll be other sin-eaters. I'll manage."

"I came because you needed me."

"I know." The stone kept turning. "I wish you'd told me before you told him."

Derry opened his mouth. Closed it. Tried again. "Remember that well-cleaning when we first started charging? The widow with the red scarf. You dropped the ash packet straight into the water and told her the blessing had to go all the way down."

Maren huffed once. "You were the one hanging off the bucket rope with the rake."

"Because you said I had longer arms."

"You did."

"You let go."

"I slipped."

"You slipped on purpose."

"I remember, Derry."

The grain in Maren's voice stopped him. Not the words, the break under them. Derry got up, crossed the fire ring, and sat beside him for the first time in days. They stayed that way while the mist thickened and the quest-light in the ditch pulsed once, slow as a heart.

"Lenna's going to ask what happened," Derry said at last. "What do I tell her?"

Maren looked down at the char on his thumb. "Tell her I'm still north. Don't tell her more than that."

"And Pell?"

"Tell her I hope they got the flour out of her hair."

Derry turned toward him. "You remembered that."

"Yeah," Maren said. "You kept talking about them. I heard you."

Derry's hand landed on Maren's shoulder, heavy and rough, the hand of a man who knew soil and livestock and the exact weight of a sleeping child. It stayed there a count of three. Then Derry got up, went back to his blanket, turned his back, and said nothing more.

Maren sat with the stone until the fire burned down to coals. The mist settled. The hum kept on.

*You should sleep,* Aldric said.

*In a minute.*

*There is nothing productive about this.*

*In a minute, Aldric.*

The paladin went quiet. Vess was already silent. Her silence was not absence. It was the trained withdrawal of a woman who knew how to leave a space without ceasing to occupy it.


# Chapter 14

The valley between the dungeon mouths tasted like scorched copper.

Maren had been walking through worsening contamination for two days, the smell thickening from background haze to something that lodged behind his sinuses and wouldn't clear, but the last quarter-mile dropped into a different register entirely. The ground under his boots was grey-dark and slick with decades of accumulated quest-light residue. The hum from three dungeon mouths, northeast, southeast, and the wide one dead ahead, layered into something that wasn't quite a sound anymore. It pressed against his chest, a low vibration that worked up through the soles of his boots and settled into the gap where the unclassified thing lived, and the thing hummed back.

*Five sin-eaters,* Aldric said. His voice had the edge it got when he stopped arguing and started sorting the world into neat pieces. *All registered. Properly classified as maintenance tier, if the System still bothers to differentiate. The profiles are thin. I do not think anyone has updated them in years.*

"Five," Maren said. He'd been expecting more.

*The historical registry shows fourteen designations for this site. Most are marked inactive.*

"Dead?"

*Or quit. The classification does not distinguish.*

The valley opened below him in the last of the evening light, and the Crook was smaller than anything he'd prepared himself for.

He'd had a picture in his head, not grand, not that naive, but substantial. A camp with structure. Tents in patterns, a central fire, the organized bustle of people who did the same essential work gathering to share the load. Instead: five canvas shelters strung between boulders on the valley floor, smoke from two cook fires, a handful of figures moving with the careful efficiency of people who had stopped wasting motion years ago. One of the shelters had collapsed on its windward side and no one had fixed it. A line of washing hung between two stunted trees, the clothes stiff and faded from contamination exposure. The whole thing looked less like a professional gathering and more like a disaster camp that had forgotten to leave.

*Three approach angles from here,* Vess said flatly. *Two with cover. One visible from every point in the camp. You're on the visible one.*

"Good."

*That wasn't a recommendation.*

"I know." He kept walking down the exposed slope, hands open at his sides, no pack on his back because he'd lost it two days ago and hadn't replaced it. Nothing on him but his clothes, his bowl wrapped in cloth inside his shirt, and three coppers he'd found in the mud outside a collapsed waystation. Coming to the sin-eater gathering looking like exactly what he was: a man with nothing left to try.

The absence to his left was louder in the silence. No nervous chatter about the road or the weather or whether the bread had gone off. No second pair of feet on packed dirt, no Derry filling every quiet moment with stories about his daughter's counting or his wife's tomatoes or the cow that wouldn't stay fenced. Three days of walking alone, which meant three days of walking with only the voices in his head, and the voices in his head had the decency to stay quiet about the person who should have been walking beside him.

The woman by the nearer cook fire saw him first. Short, square-shouldered, a grey bandage wrapped around her left hand that had gone stiff and dark at the edges. She stood fast enough to knock the ladle against the pot. A tall man by the collapsed shelter straightened from the rope he had been retying, one shoulder lifting a little higher than the other before he rolled it back into place. Beside the second fire, an older man kept working a piece of dried root between his teeth and watched Maren over the flames. At the far shelter a woman with three stoppered clay jars at her feet shifted into the doorway. Near the water cask, a young man barely past twenty caught his own wrist in his other hand and went still.

Then Thessa came around the far shelter, a ceramic bowl in her stained hands, and stopped.

The distance between them was maybe forty feet. There was a fresh split across the base of her thumb, blackened at the edges from old residue. She looked at Maren the way she would look at a contamination pool that had appeared where one should not be.

"You came," she said.

"Wasn't anywhere else."

"No." Her eyes tracked across him, or past him, reading the interface, the profile, whatever it showed about the thing he was carrying. He'd never seen what they saw when they looked at him, but he'd gotten better at reading the reactions. The bandaged woman's reaction was fear. The older man's was professional assessment, the kind that measured before it responded. Thessa's was harder to parse. Something between fury and a recognition she didn't want.

"Your friend's not with you," she said.

"No."

She waited. He didn't elaborate. The silence held the shape of everything he wasn't going to say about Derry, about a man who'd chosen his daughter over a fight that wasn't his, about waking up on the road alone for the first time in weeks with the understanding that he had earned exactly this.

Thessa turned to the bandaged woman. "Petra. Get him water."

"I saw his profile," Petra said. "Thessa, that bounty—"

"I saw the bounty. I know what happened in Silt. I'm not reading him against three live mouths while the valley's still turning over." She pointed with her bowl toward a flat stone beyond the outer fire, where the ground ran darker. "Denna, mark him outside the shelter ring. Ruald, first watch. Kael relieves him at moonset. Dawn's when the hum banks low enough for a clean read."

The woman with the jars bent immediately and chose one without comment. The older man by the second fire, Ruald then, grunted around his root and stood. The tall man at the collapsed shelter gave one short nod.

Petra went. Denna crossed to the flat stone, shook a ring of grey powder around it, and pressed three fired-clay markers into the ground at the widest points. The powder gave off a faint sour hiss where it touched the darkest residue patches.

Maren reached the camp's edge and stopped because he'd learned not to walk into spaces where people were afraid of him. He stood at the border, contamination-darkened stone on both sides, and waited to be invited or turned away.

"You sleep inside that," Thessa said. "If your residue jumps in the night, the line tells us before you reach a shelter."

The tall man by the collapsed shelter came over, wiping his fingers once on his trouser seam. Taller than Maren had expected from above, with the kind of angular thinness that came from decades of physical work on insufficient food. The left side of his coat hung lower where that shoulder never sat quite right.

"Kael," he said. "I work the western reaches."

"Maren."

"I know who you are. Your bounty's been visible for weeks. Every hero in three provinces can see it." Kael's voice stayed dry and even, as if he were naming weather that had already arrived. "Thessa told us about Silt."

*He does not seem hostile,* Aldric said carefully. *His System profile shows contamination tolerance readings well below baseline. He has been absorbing more than he should be, for longer than is sustainable. All of them are.*

"Overworked," Maren murmured.

*Severely. Yes.*
