Thessa was watching him from across the camp. Ruald dragged an upturned bucket to the far side of Denna's marker line and sat on it without taking the root from his mouth. Denna went back to the shelter opening with the jars. The young man still had his hand locked around his wrist.

*The young one is not on the registry,* Aldric said. *No maintenance designation at all.*

"Sit," Thessa told him. She pointed inside Denna's ring. "Eat if they'll feed you. At dawn we look at what happens when you open."

"And then?"

"That depends on what we find."

He sat. Petra brought him water in a clay cup with a crack running down one side, mended with brown resin. She set it on the stone beside him rather than placing it in his hand. The distance of a woman who had seen his readings and didn't want to be within arm's reach. Then she sat across the fire and unwound the bandage from her hand, working carefully, and the skin underneath was raw and mottled with a contamination pattern Maren recognized from his own failed attempts, the discoloration of flesh that had opened to something toxic and hadn't fully recovered.

"Bad consumption?" he asked.

She looked at him sharply. "You'd know what that looks like?"

"Had a few."

"Yeah. Source pool in the northeast mouth. Standard approach barely dented it and my hand was in the bowl when the backwash hit." She shook her head and started rewrapping. "Doesn't matter."

"She nearly lost the use of it," Kael said, easing himself down onto his stone and bracing a hand against that bad shoulder until it settled. "The contamination is denser this season. The standard approaches work less than they did."

The fire was warm. The cool of the turning season was settling into the valley with the fading light, the first real chill Maren had felt since leaving Fenhold, and his body registered it gratefully after weeks of late-summer heat on dusty roads. His legs ached from three days of solo walking. The gap in his chest, the space he'd started thinking of as the crack because Aldric had called it that and the word had stuck, pulsed heavier in the presence of concentrated contamination, as if it were responding to the density around it. A slow, dull throb in time with the triple hum.

*The young one is still watching you,* Vess said. *He's not deciding whether to trust you. He's deciding whether you're going to kill them in their sleep.*

Maren looked at the young sin-eater, who stood ten feet away with his hand still on his wrist, his face tight with an anxiety that didn't seem to belong exclusively to Maren's arrival, more like the residue of something that had been building for a while.

"I'm Maren," he said.

The young man said nothing. After a moment, he went back inside the shelter.

"Hale," Petra said. "He doesn't talk much lately. The work's been hard."

The valley settled into its evening sounds: the low compound hum of three dungeon mouths, the crack and pop from the fires, the distant drip-echo of quest-light pooling somewhere below the surface. Maren ate what Kael put in front of him: grain porridge with a heel of dense bread that tasted faintly of the contamination in the water they'd used to cook it. Everything here was contaminated. The food, the water, the air, the ground. These people lived in it, worked in it, ate it, breathed it, and went back to work the next morning.

After he ate, Denna freshened the grey line around the stone and set the clay markers deeper. Ruald took first watch on the bucket just beyond it, root in his teeth and an iron poker across his knees. Kael relieved him sometime past midnight. Maren slept outside the shelter ring on the thin bedroll Kael lent him, wrapped in a blanket that smelled of ritual residue and woodsmoke, while the line around him stayed dull and unbroken until dawn. The hum from the dungeon mouths didn't stop. Neither did the thing in his chest, which pulsed faintly in time with the contamination outside, as if it were breathing too.

---

The examination started at dawn, when the light was grey, the dungeon hum had banked low into the earth, and the contamination separated into layers instead of one long night-churned blur.

They didn't call it a trial. They didn't call it anything. Thessa told him to sit on the flat stone near the collapsed shelter where the contamination was densest, and the five working sin-eaters arranged themselves around him at varying distances. Thessa closest. Kael three paces back. Petra and Denna at the midpoint. Ruald beyond them with the root gone still in one cheek. Hale stayed outside the circle altogether, his hand on his wrist, his face white.

Thessa knelt in front of him. She held her bowl, darkened from long use, edges worn smooth by her thumbs, and closed her eyes. Around her, the others did the same.

*They are scanning you,* Aldric said. *Through the contamination channels, using their sin-eating sensitivity to read your residue patterns. I do not have a clean term for it. The closest I can get is that they are listening to your contamination the way a physician listens to a heartbeat, but through the System's maintenance layer.*

Maren sat still. Something shifted in the contamination around him, not their attention exactly but a change in the ambient density. The contamination pulled toward the sin-eaters' open channels the way water flows toward a drain. Inside him, the fragments stirred.

*They can feel us,* Vess said. Her voice was flat and controlled, which meant she was working to keep it that way. *Both of us. The scan is reaching the fragment layer.*

*I am aware.*

*They should not be able to reach that deep. That is not standard sin-eating.*

*No. But this close to three active dungeon mouths, with contamination this dense, their sensitivity range is significantly extended. They can see things that would not be legible in a normal setting.*

Twenty minutes. Maren sat through it the way he'd sat through every uncomfortable examination of his life, a performance of stillness that had once been a fraud's best tool and was now the closest thing to honesty he could manage. He couldn't see what they were reading. He couldn't contribute to his own assessment. He sat on the stone and waited for the verdict while people with decades of real skill read the contamination in his chest and decided whether it was something they could work with or something they needed to destroy.

Thessa opened her eyes first. Her jaw had gone tight enough to show the muscle jumping near the hinge. She took one breath through her nose before she spoke.

"How long have you been carrying the second fragment?" she asked.

"Since the road past Fenhold. Sixteen, seventeen days."

"The collision between them has gotten worse. The opposed classifications are generating friction at the interface boundary, two currents pushing against each other in a narrow channel. You felt it?" She looked at Kael.

Kael nodded, his bad shoulder ticking once before he stilled it. "The fragments aren't seated properly. Neither of them. It's as if they're floating in the host rather than anchored. And the space between them—"

"The unclassified thing," Thessa said.

"Yes."

"It's grown since Silt."

"Considerably."

Maren's hands were flat on his knees. The gap pulsed once, dully, as if it knew it was being discussed.

*She is not wrong,* Aldric said quietly. *Whatever we are, whatever I am inside you, it is less stable than it was two weeks ago. Whether that is the entity or the proximity to this level of contamination, I cannot say. But something has shifted.*

"There's more," Thessa said. She stood and faced the circle. "His contamination pattern is inverted. Every time he's tried to consume, he's opened too—"

"Wide," Kael said. He was already nodding. "Too fast. The contamination never seats long enough to break down."

"You felt it," Thessa said. Not a question.

"During the scan." He shifted his weight forward. "Like pouring through a cracked bowl. It hits the gap and goes back out."

Denna spoke for the first time. Her voice came out rough, as if it had spent years breathing dungeon air and had finally given up on ever smoothing back down. "Goes out where?"

Thessa's thumb pressed hard against the rim of her bowl. "Outward. Wider dispersal, lower density. The source point looks cleaner."

"Because he's shaved the top off it," Kael said.

"And smeared the rest thin across the surrounding field." Thessa stopped. The next words cost her something. "The local reading drops. The total doesn't."

The silence this time landed differently. Maren could read it in the way Petra's bandaged hand clenched around nothing, in the way Ruald forgot to chew, in the way Hale took a step backward. The horror of contamination workers discovering that a contamination source had walked into their camp.

"The sites behind you didn't clear," Thessa said, turning back to him. "You blew the tops off the pools and shoved the rest out past standard catch lines. The center reads cleaner, so people think the work is done. It isn't. The spread just stops showing where anyone knows to look."

"I didn't know," Maren said.

"No. You didn't. Because you were faking."

He had nothing for that.

*She is right,* Aldric said. *At the creek the contamination crossed you like water through a broken sluice. It never seated. It touched the gap and went back out thinner. The width made it look like clearance.*

"Consume him," Thessa said.

It took Maren a moment to understand she wasn't talking to him. She was talking to the circle.

"Break down the fragments. Dissolve the unclassified structure. We eat it. One of us takes him, and we end this."

"Thessa—" Kael began.

"He's carrying two hostile fragments in an unstable configuration." She turned to the circle as if Kael had not spoken. "An entity the System can't classify. A contamination pattern that poisons every area he passes through."

"We can read that much," Petra said. Her bandaged hand was clenched at her side.

"Then you can read what happens if we leave it." Thessa's voice went flat. "He's not a sin-eater. He's a contamination event with legs. If a pool showed these readings, we'd consume it without discussion."

"A pool doesn't speak," Kael said mildly.

"Neither does the contamination in the villages he's been spreading for eleven years. The children growing up in it don't get to speak either."

Maren took it. He sat on his stone and looked at the split at the base of Thessa's thumb and understood, with a clarity that bypassed every defense he'd ever built, that she was right. From every angle that mattered to these people, the only people who did the work that kept the world from drowning in its own waste, he was the problem, not the solution.

"Who takes him?" Petra asked. The question was practical, not moral, and the practicality was worse. "I can't. My hand's barely functional. Kael?"

Kael shook his head slowly. "His readings are too high. The unclassified structure alone is a risk. If any of us tried a full consumption and failed, the backwash would redistribute him instead of dissolving him."

"So what do we do?" Petra looked at Thessa. "Call someone?"

"There's no one to call." The first words came flat. Then the rest came faster. "There are five of us. There should be fourteen. The contamination's denser every season and there are fewer of us every year and the standard approaches don't work the way they did. I can't keep pretending they do."

She bit the line off. Her fingers cinched around the rim of her bowl until the knuckles went pale.

"I don't have a clean answer," she said. "But leaving him as he is means leaving the problem to grow."

"Test me," Maren said.

Thessa's eyes snapped to him.

"You can't consume me safely. You said it yourself. The readings are too high, the risk is too great. So test me instead. Make me do a consumption under your supervision. If I can't manage it, if I'm nothing but a contamination event with legs, then you work out something else. But you've got nothing to lose from watching me try."

"Except more redistributed contamination across our workspace," Denna said from her position in the circle.

"If it goes wrong, the contamination's already here. This valley's baseline is worse than anything I could add."

*That is not entirely true,* Aldric said. *But it is close enough to be tactically useful.*

Kael scrubbed a hand over the bad shoulder and said, "Let him try."

Thessa looked at Kael. Looked at Maren. Looked at the valley around them, the three dungeon mouths breathing their contamination into the low ground, the grey-dark stone, the faint shimmer of quest-light pooling in the morning air, the raw glow without data, without labels, without the overlay the others navigated by.

"Fine," she said. "Supervised. Every step. And if what I see looks like redistribution instead of clearance, I stop you. Hard."

"Fine."

"Get up. We do it now."

---

They moved to the nearest dungeon mouth within the hour, the morning mist still thinning around them. Vapour rose from the contaminated ground in thin tendrils, catching the pale light, and the hum from the opening was a physical pressure that Maren could feel in his teeth. The stone around the mouth was nearly black from years of quest-light saturation, and the contamination smell was dense enough to coat the inside of his throat.

Maren knelt ten paces from the mouth with his ceramic bowl unwrapped and in his hands. His bowl sat on the ground in front of him, and in this place, surrounded by real sin-eaters, it looked exactly as fraudulent as it was. No staining. No ritual wear. Clean, cheap, useless.

Thessa stood behind him and to his right. Kael, Petra, Denna, and Ruald were arrayed in a loose half-circle at twenty paces, close enough to observe, far enough to absorb the backwash if the consumption went wrong. Hale stood another ten paces back with both hands at his sides, fingers curled, watching with the rigid attention of a young man who didn't want to be the first to flinch.

"Kneeling posture," Thessa said. "Bowl in front of you, open. Elbows off your knees. You need your chest open for the intake."

He adjusted. The stone was cold and damp through his trousers.

"Breathe first. Not the performance breathing you've been doing. The actual pattern. In through the nose until you feel the contamination enter your sinus cavity. Hold it. Let it settle behind your sternum before you open. If you tear the channel wide on the first pull, you'll spray it back out the way you've been doing."

*She is describing the primary intake sequence,* Aldric said. *It matches what the Order's texts describe, but more embodied. Less theoretical. She is teaching from practice, not doctrine.*

Maren breathed in. The contamination entered through his nose. Not a metaphor, a physical sensation, the dense copper-sweet mixture that was the valley's baseline air. He held it. The taste crept along the roof of his mouth, down the back of his throat. The gap in his chest stirred.

"Open," Thessa said.

He opened. Not his mouth. This wasn't about the mouth, despite the name. Opening was the thing he'd learned on his own near the creek bed, the thing that had nearly broken him: a deliberate surrender of the boundary between himself and the contamination around him. Like unclenching a fist he hadn't known was clenched somewhere behind his sternum. The inside of his chest peeled back, and the first rush of contamination hit hard enough to make his vision spark.

This time it didn't flash straight through. It caught under his sternum instead, heavy, grinding, hot, as if the contamination had finally found purchase instead of a hole.

"There," Thessa said sharply. "Hold it there. Don't reach."

*It is seating,* Aldric said, startled. *For the first time it is actually seating.*

Then the pull ran wider anyway. Contamination answered from beyond the nearest pool, dragging through him in a pressure wave that locked his shoulders and set his teeth buzzing.

"Wider," Thessa said, and her voice had changed. The instructional flatness was gone. "He's pulling wider. Kael, do you see the radius?"

"I see it."

*Outflow is lower than intake,* Aldric said. *You are not venting all of it. Some of it is breaking down in the channel.*

The contamination poured through the gap and the gap was not a hole but a channel, a crack, and the crack widened as the contamination flowed, and the flow was not something Maren was controlling. His hands were locked on the cold stone beside his knees. His bowl sat empty because the bowl was for collection and there was nothing to collect. The load under his sternum stayed heavy while the rest moved through him in a thinner stream, some of its weight burning off in the gap. His vision blurred. The hum from the dungeon mouth rose in pitch and the mist around the opening churned and his body wanted to double over and vomit but Thessa's hand was on his shoulder, holding him upright, her grip hard and professional.

"Don't collapse. Keep the channel open. If you close it with contamination still in transit—"

He kept it open. His muscles were shaking. Aldric had gone quiet, the quiet that meant he was seeing something in the System's architecture that outpaced language, and Vess was counting.

*Fifteen seconds. Your body starts losing structural coherence around thirty. Close soon.*

"I can't. I don't know how to close it from here."

"Close the same way you opened," Thessa said. Her voice was tight but held. "Clench. The fist behind your sternum. Find it and close it."

He found it. He closed it. The contamination flow cut off like a valve shutting, and the shock of losing something that had been moving through the core of him knocked him sideways off his knees. He caught himself on his palms. His bowl tipped and rang against the stone, a clear ceramic note that sounded absurd in the contaminated dawn. His arms shook. His stomach heaved once, dry, and he spat on the ground and the spit was dark with residue.

"Stay down," Thessa said. She was standing over him, reading the interface. Around her, the other sin-eaters were reading too.

He was on his hands and knees on contaminated stone, shaking, spitting dark, and the gap in his chest felt wider than it had before the consumption, as if something had been stretched that wouldn't go back. His bowl lay on its side next to his hand, empty.

*Maren.* Aldric's voice came back rough, stripped of its usual register. *The mouth dropped. The northeast seep dropped with it. So did the residue shelf above camp. I am not seeing the outward bloom we got at the creek. Nothing on the perimeter is rising.*

The silence in the circle was different now.

Maren got himself back to kneeling. His hands were smeared with the dark residue of contaminated stone, his forearms trembling. He picked up his bowl and set it flat in front of him and looked at the clean white interior, then at Thessa.

She was staring at the interface readouts without blinking. Her thumb had gone still against the bowl's rim.

"No scatter bloom," Kael said. He had one hand hooked under the high shoulder as if holding it in place. "The mouth is down. The side pools are down."

"Outer markers held," Denna said from her position. She sounded startled.

"I can see that," Thessa said.

Petra was flexing her bandaged hand, staring at whatever the interface was showing her. "How? That's not how it works. We consume and dissolve. We don't reduce ambient levels across an entire field."

"The gap," Thessa said. She was still reading the interface, her thumb pressed white against the bowl rim. "Same thing that made him dangerous. Before, the width threw the contamination back out thin. This time the intake seated first, and once it started breaking down, the width pulled on the connected field instead of pushing."

Kael nodded slowly. "Carried the drop instead of the spill."

Thessa nodded once. The other sin-eaters were looking between her and Maren.

"Sixty years ago," Kael said. "The one you told us about."

"They were consumed by the System. Corrected." Thessa's voice went flat. "Nothing left."

"But before that—"

"Before that, the records say contamination levels in their region were the lowest in a century."

Petra had stopped flexing her bad hand. Ruald had forgotten the root in his cheek. Hale was no longer at the far edge of the observation line. Denna was looking at Maren instead of the ground.

Thessa looked at Maren for a long time. He knelt on the cold stone with his dirty hands and his clean bowl and his trembling arms and the gap in his chest that was wider than it had been an hour ago, and he waited.

"You're sloppy," she said. "Your intake form is wrong. Your closing technique is worse."

"Yeah."

"And you did more in twenty seconds than I've done in a day."

Maren said nothing.

*Don't say anything,* Vess said. *She's working through something. Let her get there.*

Thessa looked at the valley, then at Maren kneeling on the contaminated stone with his clean bowl.

"Not entirely useless," she said.

Maren breathed out.

"We start with the intake form. Your posture's wrong, your breathing's wrong, your closing technique will kill you." She turned to the circle. "He doesn't eat, sleep, or breathe without my say-so until I'm satisfied he won't blow us all apart." Back to Maren. "And the first time you fake anything, one gesture, one posture, one breath that's performance instead of practice, I stop teaching and we go back to the consumption discussion. Clear?"

"Clear."

"Get off the ground. You look like you're praying."

He got off the ground. His legs barely held. Aldric said nothing. Vess didn't either. The morning light reached the bottom of the valley and the quest-light pooling near the dungeon mouths caught it, a faint shimmer, like water reflecting something Maren couldn't quite see, beautiful in the way things were beautiful when you didn't have the data to know what you were looking at.

Thessa walked toward the cook fire. Petra followed, flexing her bandaged hand. Ruald spat his root into the dirt and went after them. Kael hooked a spare flat stone a little closer to the fire with his heel before he turned away. Denna stooped to gather her clay jars from beside the shelter entrance and carried them in under one arm. Hale stood where he was a moment longer, then nodded once at Maren before turning away.

Maren picked up his bowl. The ceramic was cool in his hands, the same cheap clay it had always been, no heavier and no lighter for what had just passed through it. He went to the fire and sat on the stone Kael had nudged into place while the others moved around him and nobody told him to move.


# Chapter 15

The valley smelled different in the early morning. Cooler air pushed the contamination stink down close to the ground and thinned it enough that Maren could almost pretend the ozone-sweet rot was not there. Almost. The hum from the three dungeon mouths had banked low with the night, not gone quiet so much as dropped into the earth. He had slept badly on the thin bedroll Kael had lent him, the entity pressing against the inside of his skull in slow rhythmic surges like another body trying to breathe through his ribs, and when he had crawled out before sunrise the Crook had looked peaceful in the ugly way contaminated places could look peaceful. Quest-light lay in the low spots with a faint shimmer, pale as moonlight on standing water.

He was east of the camp now, on his knees in cold dirt, trying to remember the second containment pass Thessa had drilled into him the afternoon before. The ceramic bowl rested against his thigh in its loose cloth wrapping because he had carried it out with him automatically. He was not using it. He was only trying to get the order of the motions right before Thessa came looking and informed him, with complete justice, that he had forgotten half of what she had taught him overnight. His left wrist kept collapsing. His fingers kept stiffening at the wrong moment. Every time it happened he had to start again.

Behind him, the camp was waking up in pieces. Petra had the fire going, crouched low over a blackened pot with her bandaged hand wrapped around the spoon because the cold still made the knuckles seize. Her bad left hip pulled her sideways whenever she shifted her weight, but she moved around it with the old mean patience of someone who had stopped expecting the body to cooperate years ago. Errin Denna had four clay jars open on the table by the shelter entrance and her nose nearly in one of them, making the same small dissatisfied sound every time the overnight seep had turned a sample. Kael sat on an upturned bucket near the eastern lean-to, chewing root and watching Maren's hands with the expression of a man trying not to laugh at somebody else's bad morning. Ruald worked at the water barrel, rewrapping the grey-brown cloth strips he used for his own half-improvised containment work. The strips smelled faintly of burnt sugar and old residue. He had mentioned once, while complaining about a boot seam, that his wife folded them for him at home, matching the corners exactly because she hated crooked cloth.

Denna took the little bone awl from behind her ear, scratched a fresh notch into the leather tally tied to her belt, and put the awl back without looking. She ate with her left hand and wrote with her right. That was one of the only things Maren knew about her, along with the fact that Thessa called her Denna and Kael called her Errin when he forgot himself. Hale was farther south on first-light watch where the valley rose toward the old residue beds. From here he was mostly a dark shape against the grey, standing still in the way only watchful men stood still, like movement was something to be spent carefully.

"No shoulder," Thessa said behind him.

Maren flinched hard enough that the bowl slipped against his thigh. He turned and found her already up, hair flattened on one side from sleep, coat only half buttoned. "You people move too quietly for honest work."

"You open from the shoulder again, you'll tear the pull crooked and contaminate your own lungs." She crouched without ceremony, caught his left wrist between two dry fingers, and turned it a few degrees inward. "There. Now do it the way I showed you."

"You say good morning like a threat."

"If you wanted pleasant company, you should not have come to a work camp."

Petra snorted over the pot. Kael spat root into the dirt and nudged his bucket a little with his heel so he could get a better view. Maren tried the motion again. His wrist held this time. His fingers did not.

"Too stiff," Thessa said.

"I know."

"Then stop doing it wrong."

Denna lifted one of the jars, frowned at it, and said, "This one's gone bitter."

"Bury it deeper," Thessa said without turning. "And stop breathing into the mouth."

"I'm not breathing into the mouth."

"Then your face is too close to it for some other stupid reason."

Ruald laughed once through his nose. Petra said, "You can tell she's rested."

It was such a normal camp line that for one stupid second Maren let himself believe in a normal morning. The pot hissed. Kael worked the root from one cheek to the other. Denna set the bad jar aside and reached for another. Hale stayed motionless on the ridge. Thessa released Maren's wrist.

Ruald came over from the barrel with one strip half wrapped around his hand. "You're closing too soon," he said to Maren. "Looks like you're trying to strangle it."

"Very helpful."

"You want helpful, stop doing that."

Denna held out a stopper without looking. "If you've got spare opinions, cork this."

Ruald thumbed the stopper into the mouth of the jar and handed it back. Denna twisted it tight, scratched another notch into the tally, and muttered, "Fourth bad turn this week."

Petra tasted the spoon, frowned, and shook another pinch of dried herb into the pot. "Everything's turning early."

"Not everything," Kael said around the root. He nudged a flat spare stone a little closer to the fire with his heel. "Maren's still useless right on schedule."

"Comforting."

"You'll know if I'm trying to comfort you."

Petra set out six tin cups by feel, one of them mashed into an oval from some older accident. On the ridge Hale lifted one hand without looking back, the signal that he was still there and had not seen anything worth shouting about. Denna tucked the awl behind her ear again. Ruald went back to his strips. The valley hummed under all of it like a second weather.

*Four hostiles approaching from the northwest ridge.*

Aldric's voice came fast and flat. Under that clipped delivery sat something worse than urgency. Recognition.

*They are in full combat loadout. Caul is leading them. Two melee fighters and one support caster.*

Maren came up off his knees so fast his ankle folded under him. He grabbed the bowl by its wrapping on reflex and caught himself against the trunk of a dead birch. "How far?"

*Quarter mile. Descending now. The caster is already building a targeting overlay. They have the camp.*

The bowl knocked against his wrist. He had known Caul would come. Derry had given him the direction and Caul was a tracker, patient in the way men got patient when patience was the only thing between them and the grief underneath. Maren had still thought he would have longer than this. A day, maybe two. Enough time to turn instruction into something less embarrassing than whatever he was currently doing in the dirt.

He ran.

"Heroes," he said as he crossed the camp. "Four. Northwest ridge. They've got a targeting overlay on us."

Petra's spoon stopped halfway to the pot rim. Denna's hand closed around the neck of the nearest jar. Kael's head snapped toward the ridge and the root stayed in his cheek. Ruald did not look up from the cloth at first, which was how frightened he was.

Thessa came out of the largest lean-to already awake enough to be angry. Her hair was still flattened on one side and both hands were empty. That meant she had put something down the second she heard his feet.

"How many?"

"Four. Ranger, two melee, one caster. The ranger's Caul."

The recalculation happened in her face all at once. "Leave the shelters. Carry only what matters. Denna, take the jars. Petra, leave the pot. Ruald, bring your strips. Kael, call Hale in. South to the third mouth."

"We can't outrun a hero party," Ruald said. The cloth strips hung from one hand, limp and dirty. "Thessa, we're not combat-classified. We don't have any way to"

"I'm not asking you to fight. The contamination around the third mouth is deep enough to blur target locks." She looked at Maren then, and that look had no warmth in it at all. "You're why they're here."

"Yeah."

"Then you stay and buy us the time."

It was not a question. Thessa turned and started moving the camp with the clipped, hard precision she used when work had gone bad enough that discussion was wasted breath. Denna swept the jars into a padded sling with the tally thumping against her thigh. Petra shoved the spoon aside hard enough to knock broth into the fire. Kael was already shouting for Hale down the slope. Ruald bent once for his satchel, missed the buckle, swore under his breath, and tried again.

*They will not make clean distance,* Vess said. *The targeting overlay covers the full valley.*

*Confirmed. The sin-eaters are flagged as non-combatant civilians.* Aldric paused. *Collateral authorization has been granted. The bounty's threat threshold overrides civilian protection.*

"What does that mean in actual words?"

*It means they are allowed to kill anyone between them and you.*

The first arrow hit the eastern lean-to's support pole with a flat crack and the whole side of the shelter sagged at once. Maren never heard the bow. He heard the impact. He heard Petra suck in a breath. Then a second arrow buried itself in the dirt three feet from Ruald's boots.

"Move," Maren shouted. "Ruald, move, don't stand there, move."

The two melee heroes came over the north lip of the camp at a run. One tall with a polearm. One shorter, fast, dual blades out. Maren did not have the data for them, only the shapes and the speed and the way his own body recognized trouble before his mind could sort it. Aldric's training surged into his muscles. Vess's killing instinct came up cold along his spine. By the time he decided to put himself between the fighters and the retreating sin-eaters, his feet had already done it.

The tall one reached him first. He felt the air move before he properly understood the sweep. Low strike for the legs. His body had already stepped out of it, half turned, hand clamped to the shaft to shove the arc past his hip. The weapon's weight jarred up through his wrists. Leather oil. Road sweat. Something sharp and herbal in the woman's armor padding.

*Third-tier melee. Polearm specialist. Inside her reach and the advantage disappears.*

He was already inside it. His elbow crashed into the side of her head above the ear guard. She staggered.

The second fighter came from the right with dual blades out, young enough that his face still had some softness left in it. Maren only caught that much before his body took over again. Forearm against the flat of one blade. Pivot outside the other. Knee into the boy's stance hard enough to make it buckle.

He was fighting and not fighting. The fragments operated his body while Maren occupied it, dragged through the thing by sensation and weight and smell. No damage numbers. No threat markers. Just the shock of impact in his bones, contaminated dirt giving under his boots, metal scraping leather.

Petra screamed, high and ragged and stripped of anything that sounded like fight. Maren's head turned while the rest of him was still engaged with the boy. Petra was down by the fire pit, black broth and ash running together around her knees. The arrow was lodged below her left shoulder blade. She had made it maybe three dragging body-lengths before the bad hip folded under her. Her bandaged hand clawed into the dirt hard enough to turn the knuckles white through old residue.

*Caul. Northwest ridge. Sixty yards and cycling another shot.*

The second arrow took Ruald in the throat.

He had turned back when Petra went down. That was the whole stupid human thing of it. He had turned back for a woman he had worked beside for years in a job nobody respected, and the broadhead found the gap under his jaw. He sat down hard as if a stool had been yanked out from under him. His hands went to his neck. The cloth strips spilled from the satchel, grey-brown in the dirt, still folded square from somebody else's careful hands.

*Left,* Vess said. *The polearm fighter is up again.*

His body spun. The overhead chop came down. Maren ducked under it and drove his fist into the woman's diaphragm. She folded. Something in her knee gave with a branch-snap sound and she went to the ground clutching it.

A dry pop went off east of the camp, not loud, just wrong, like a board cracking inside a closed room.

Denna jerked in the open ground between the second lean-to and the path south. One jar burst against a stone and sprayed dark slurry over her skirt. She dropped to both knees with the second jar still hugged to her ribs, stared at nothing for half a heartbeat, and folded onto her side. The leather tally knocked twice against her thigh and went still. Her right hand never let go of the bone awl. It drove point-first into the dirt beside her cheek and pinned two strands of her hair under it.

Kael slid the last few feet to her and caught her shoulder too late. "Denna's down," he said, and his voice had gone so flat it barely sounded like him. "The caster hit her with some kind of pulse. She's not moving."

Maren had enough time to count them and not enough time to reach any of them. Petra dragging blood and ash. Ruald drowning on his own throat. Denna on her side with the broken jar stinking in the dirt and the awl planted by her mouth like somebody had marked the place where she stopped. Three people down because Caul had followed him here.

*The caster is repositioning east,* Aldric said. *She is trying to establish a precision lock on you.*

Maren looked south. Thessa was fifty yards out, moving fast. Kael had Denna's shoulder once in his hand and then let it go because there was nothing there for him to do. Hale was higher on the slope, waving them toward the older contamination and then turning back long enough to make sure they kept moving. Behind Maren, the polearm woman was on the ground with her ruined knee, the boy was retreating uphill toward Caul, and the caster was somewhere he could not see because he could not see anything the System built.

*Maren.* Aldric's voice had lost its report-cadence. *The caster's next pulse will establish a range lock. If she completes the scan, they will be able to strike through the contamination.*

"How long?"

*Thirty seconds. Perhaps less.*

Maren looked past the practice patch near his knees to the residue pool between the first and second dungeon mouths, a shallow lowland full of years of accumulated contamination, cold-glowing and dense enough that even he could read its shape. The smell coming off it was layered and heavy, fresh ozone over old sweetness over the sour metal stink of residue that had been sitting in the same dirt long enough to change the dirt.

*No,* Aldric said at once. *That is a nexus-grade pool. You consumed a fraction of a minor pool under supervision and it nearly broke you.*

"If I don't do it, the caster gets the lock and everybody on that slope dies."

*You cannot control that much contamination.*

"I'm not trying to control it."

He was already running. The bowl thumped against his side. His boots slid on the bad ground. He hurdled one of Denna's broken jar shards and nearly went down anyway. Inside him the entity pressed outward as if it could feel what he was about to do and had reached for it before he had.

Vess did not answer. Her silence was its own verdict.

---

He dropped at the edge of the pool and yanked the wrapped bowl free because it was in the way. He let it fall beside him in the dirt. The contamination hummed through his trousers and into his shins, not the background hum of the valley but a local vibration with its own pitch. The glow was cold. He could feel it on the skin of his hands like the pressure of someone staring.

He used the second opening, the one Thessa had called reckless and emergency-only and too easy to do wrong. The opening did not feel like skill. It felt like stopping the body's natural flinch. Like unlatching something in himself that every sane person kept shut.

The contamination came in like floodwater, nothing like yesterday's supervised pull. It was cold and massive, more pressure than his body had any language for. His chest filled with something that was not air and was not liquid but had the weight of both. Ozone coated the back of his tongue. Copper. A sweetness on the turn toward rot. His teeth ached all the way up into the roots. The bones behind his eyes felt squeezed inward by hands that did not exist.

*The targeting overlay is degrading,* Aldric said, voice gone distant. *Their interface is flickering around the consumption point.*

More. He pulled more. The contamination rushed at the opening as if it had been waiting for one. That was the part nobody had explained to him. It wanted to go somewhere. It poured toward him with a horrible eagerness and the pool's glow dimmed as the residue went into him.

Inside his skull the unclassified thing responded at once.

It surged.

His bones started vibrating at a frequency that did not belong to any human skeleton. Every muscle in his back clenched at once, not like a cramp but like something had reached into him and tried to close a fist. His knee slammed down on the bowl where he had dropped it. Ceramic cracked sharp under him and a shard punched through his trouser leg into the skin above his kneecap. The pain barely registered.

*Maren, stop,* Vess said. For the first time since he had known her, the flatness in her voice had split. *The entity is expanding beyond your containment threshold.*

He could not stop. The consumption had momentum now. The pool was feeding into him faster than he could regulate and the entity was drinking from the same channel, unfolding inside his head, pressing against his consciousness like something trying to stand in a room that had been built too low for it.

*The System sees it.* Aldric sounded nothing like himself. *It is not registering a threat class. It is registering a void, a gap in the architecture.*

The pool went dark without going empty, as if the light had been eaten out of it and left the residue behind. The last of the contamination slammed into him, the channel snapped shut, and Maren was on his hands and knees over dead soil retching uselessly because it was not in his stomach. It was everywhere else.

The hum from the dungeon mouths jumped an octave in a single hard step, going from the low furnace-bass he had been hearing for two days to something high enough to scrape his sinuses raw. The air temperature dropped with it. One breath and the early-autumn morning was gone, replaced by the damp cold of a cellar.

*Local reclassification in progress,* Aldric said, and now the old formal cadence was back because fear had driven him into it. *Crook Valley redesignation. Threat classification: Critical. Contamination index: Extreme. Authorized response: full containment protocol.*

"What does that mean?"

*It means the System is treating this valley as a breach site. Every entity inside the perimeter is now a valid threat target. Including Caul's party.*

---

The quest-light came up first. It burst through the soil in hard bright runners, cold threads racing over the discolored ground with the speed of roots finding water. In seconds the valley floor was webbed in it, pale branching lines connecting and reconnecting until the whole low ground looked netted in visible structure.

Then the contamination spiked. The smell changed before anything else did, sweetness turning acrid, the air going thick enough that every breath had weight. The nearest dungeon mouth began giving off shapes.

They came out wrong, with too many joints and limbs bending where living bodies did not. Dog-sized things lit from inside with the same cold light running through the valley floor, threat creatures the System had made for this exact correction, an immune response given bodies.

The first one oriented toward Maren, stopped, and turned its malformed head toward the northwest ridge instead.

*They are not prioritizing you,* Aldric said quietly. *The entity makes you unreadable. They are going for anything the System can classify.*

"Caul's people."

*Yes.*

From the ridge Caul shouted, voice gone hoarse enough that Maren almost did not recognize it. "Fall back to the ridge. All units, disengage the target. We have a local correction." He broke off. Even at this distance there was still that briefing-cadence in him, trained and practiced and trying to hold. "No. Read that again. Friendly assets inside breach perimeter..." Something struck something else with a sound that was not flesh and was not stone. "Acceptable loss? We are the response team."

*He is reading the containment directive,* Aldric said. *It has marked his own party expendable until classification stability is restored.*

The polearm woman screamed.

*She's down,* Vess said. *The creatures reached her before she cleared the zone.*

Maren got to his feet. Everything about his body felt wrong now, too heavy and badly balanced, as if the contamination inside him had been packed unevenly through his bones. The cracked bowl lay in the dirt at his feet with the cloth half caught beneath it. He snatched it up by reflex.

More creatures were coming out of all three mouths. The valley floor started to crawl.

The young dual-blade fighter was crying now, wet and ragged between the clang of his blades against bodies the System itself had generated. Caul said, "No." Then again, louder and in a voice that had stopped sounding like a report. "No. Ignore the directive. Forget the target. Get behind me."

He turned his whole body away from Maren.

Whatever pursuit had brought him here was over. Caul planted himself between the creatures and the boy on the ridge, dropped any pretense that the hunt still mattered, and started cutting down what the System had sent at his own people.

Thessa appeared at Maren's side carrying one of Denna's clay jars under her arm. He had not heard her approach. She moved through contaminated ground the way other people moved through their own kitchens, feet finding the safe patches without hesitation. A smear of dark slurry had dried along the jar's shoulder where it had slopped during the run.

"Three jars left," she said. "That buys us minutes, not many."

"Who got out?"

"Kael and Hale are over the south ridge already." Her tone did not shift. "Petra didn't get back up. Ruald bled where he fell. Denna took the pulse clean and never came round."

There was no room to answer that. Thessa was moving already, and Maren followed because the valley floor had started to move in ways the human body could not survive for long.

They cut south past what had been the camp. Petra had made it almost to the path. She was on her face now, arrow still in her back, one boot twisted under the ruined line of her hip. The bandaged hand that had held the spoon all morning was buried to the knuckles in the dirt as if she had tried to drag the rest of herself forward by that one grip alone. Ruald sat where Caul's arrow had put him down, chin on his chest, the blood down the front of him already drying dark. One of the cloth strips had wound itself around his wrist in the fall, still folded square, neat as a prayer somebody else had said for him. Denna lay farther south on her side with the unbroken jar missing from the crook of her arm and the awl still planted in the dirt by her cheek. The tally at her belt had swung under her and pinned itself there. Thessa did not look directly at any of them. She bent once, snatched a strip from Ruald's spill with her free hand, and kept walking.

The thing that came at them from the nearest line of quest-light moved low and fast. Thessa uncapped Denna's jar one-handed, flung a thick ribbon of old residue across the ground in front of it, and the creature hit the slick patch with all its weight. Its forelegs shot sideways. It went over hard on too many elbows, skidding broadside into a deeper puddle where the joints began to dissolve from the inside. Thessa did not even watch it finish.

"Step where I step," she said. "Not near it. On it."

Maren tried. The consumed residue made his legs thick and unreliable, every step a negotiation between his own body and the thing packed inside it. The cracked bowl knocked his hip. The cut above his kneecap had started a hot wet trickle down his shin. Twice he planted a boot and the ground seemed to lean away under him.

Ahead, quest-light webbed the route south in bright threads no wider than a finger. Thessa moved through them without hesitation, choosing the gaps with a speed that looked like instinct and was probably three decades of not dying. Maren misjudged one line and his boot came down an inch too close.

The light snapped upward, not touching him, but close enough to throw cold across his skin.

Thessa caught the back of his coat with Ruald's cloth strip and yanked him sideways so hard his shoulder popped. "I said step where I step."

"I'm trying."

"Try with your feet."

Another creature turned toward them, head canting as the light shivered around Maren's near miss. Thessa slashed the cloth strip down across the bright thread. Old contamination soaked through the weave, turning the line muddy and thick for one blink. The creature swerved after the false signal and vanished into a knot of brighter runners ten yards east. The knot erupted. Cold light went up in a spray and the thing came apart in pieces that did not look anatomical anymore.

Thessa looped the dirty strip around her wrist and kept going. That one quick salvage from Ruald was part of the route now, part of the work. Maren hated her for the competence of it and would have hated her more if she had done anything else.

Behind them Caul shouted again, closer now because the ridge carried sound. "Stay with me. Leave the target. That's an order." There was a metallic crash. Then, lower and rougher, a line Maren almost missed under the valley hum. "The directive's wrong."

*Caul has abandoned pursuit,* Vess said. *He is holding the ridge for the surviving melee fighter. The caster is down.*

Maren stopped at the foot of the south slope. Below him the valley seethed with light. On the far side of the camp the support caster was sprawled half off the eastern bank, one arm still flung outward in the posture that had dropped Denna. Caul held the higher ground with the dual-blade boy behind him. He was not looking toward Maren at all anymore. He was looking at what the System had decided to spend.

*He has a route out,* Aldric said softly. *The perimeter is open on the northwest side. He could leave if he chose to.*

"He won't leave the boy."

*No,* Aldric said. *He will not.*

Thessa turned and looked back at him from ten yards up the slope. There was clay on her sleeve from Denna's jar and Ruald's strip hanging from her wrist. "Move. There's nothing you can do for them from here."

She meant Caul's people. She meant the ones in the camp below too. She meant all of it. Maren hated that she was right, but that did not make her less right. He climbed.

The south slope was steep and slick with old residue worked so deep into the soil it had stopped feeling accidental. The smell changed as they rose, less raw ozone, more earth gone strange over time. Twice Maren slipped and once Thessa caught him by the elbow without slowing. The jar under her arm knocked against her ribs with each step. She never set it down.

They crested the ridge. The far side dropped into scrub and thin trees already starting to turn for autumn. The air out there was cleaner by degrees, which was enough to feel like a different world.

Maren turned once before following her down. From the ridge he could see the whole valley. The camp was gone inside the web of quest-light. The fire pit was just another dark knot inside the pale grid. On the far side Caul still held the higher ground with the boy crouched behind him. He had backed almost to the opening Aldric had described, one shoulder turned to cover the retreat, every cut of the sword buying somebody else another step.

*The boy is alive,* Aldric said. *The response is already narrowing toward the center of the breach.*

Three of them, Maren thought. The sentence would not stay thought-sized. It came out of his mouth. "Three of them. Because of me."

Thessa stood beside him long enough to answer. "Yes."

She started down the far slope. Maren followed. The cracked bowl knocked against his hip through the cloth at every step, and behind him the valley kept humming.


# Chapter 16

They found a sheep pen half a mile from where the valley started to taste wrong.

Not a farmstead, just a low stone wall in a rough circle, open to the sky, with a gate that had rotted off its hinges years ago. The kind of place shepherds used for overnight holding during drives, back when anyone still drove sheep through dungeon-adjacent territory. Nobody had used it in a long time. Grass had come up through the gaps between stones and died there, thin brown stalks that crumbled when Maren brushed against them.

He sat with his back against the wall and tried to catalogue what still worked. His hands were shaking, but that was ordinary. They'd been shaking since the consumption at the Crook, since the quest had been too large and the unclassified entity had surged and the System had turned the valley into a threat zone. Two days of shaking wasn't ideal, but it wasn't new. The headache behind his left eye was new. The way his stomach kept clenching around nothing, as if his body were still trying to reject what he'd already absorbed, was new too.

The air out here smelled like autumn coming in under contamination: decaying leaves and the acrid sweetness of old quest residue, layered over each other like stains on a floor nobody mopped. The hum was louder than it should have been this far from the dungeon mouths, as if the System's reclassification had pushed the noise boundary outward. Everything within a day's walk of the Crook now sounded like standing next to an active quest.

*The instability is increasing.*

Vess. She hadn't spoken in hours, not since they'd cleared the valley and the running had stopped and there was nothing left to be tactical about. Her voice was the same as it always was: flat, economical, stating things.

Maren didn't answer. He was concentrating on not throwing up again, which required most of his attention.

*Maren. The instability is increasing and it's not going to stop.*

"I know." He said it out loud because talking to the voices inside his skull silently still felt like a thing insane people did, which was funny, because talking to them out loud in a sheep pen probably wasn't the picture of mental health either. "I can feel it."

He could. The unclassified entity, born from the collision of Aldric's paladin classification and Vess's shadow-guild classification, the thing the System couldn't name and therefore wanted to destroy, vibrated in his chest. Not pain, exactly. More like a frequency his body couldn't quite resolve, an internal hum that didn't match the external one. It had been getting worse since the overloaded consumption. Before the Crook, it had been background noise. Now it was the loudest thing in any room he walked into, including rooms without walls.

*It's me.*

"What?"

*I'm the variable. Two opposing classifications in a System-blind host produce an entity the System can't categorize. Remove one classification and the entity simplifies. Stops surging. The System can still track it, but it doesn't—* A brief pause, recalibrating. *The interaction between us is what creates the instability. Between what he was and what I was. Take me out and the math changes.*

"The math," Maren said.

*The math.*

He pressed the heel of his hand against his left eye where the headache lived. The stone wall was cold through his shirt. Early autumn had crept in while they'd been running. The nights were longer now and the warmth went out of things faster. He missed the late-summer heat of Fenhold, which was an absurd thing to miss when he could never go back there.

"Aldric," he said.

Nothing.

"Aldric, she's—"

*I heard her.* The paladin's voice was clipped. Formal. The register he retreated to when the framework was failing. *I am considering her assessment.*

*Don't consider it. It's accurate.* Vess didn't wait for silence the way she usually did. She spoke into Aldric's pause as if she'd been listening for exactly that gap. *The entity surged because I'm here. It'll surge again. Worse each time. The next time the System responds, it won't reclassify the area. It'll reclassify him.*

Maren's stomach clenched again. He swallowed and tasted contamination, that ozone-sweet undertone that clung to the back of his throat after consumption, fainter than it had been yesterday but still present, like spoiled fruit he couldn't spit out.

"So what are you saying?" He knew what she was saying. He wanted her to say it anyway, because some part of him was still the con man who needed to hear the version of events someone else committed to before he committed to his own.

*I need to leave.*

The wind came through the gap where the gate had been. It brought the contamination smell with it, stronger from the east, where the valley lay reclassified and the quest-light had flooded in so thick that even Maren could see the glow from the ridge. The dead grass crumbled against the stones.

"Leave," he said. "As in—"

*Separation. You've learned enough from the old woman to perform a consumption. The reverse should be possible. Expulsion instead of absorption. Push me out instead of pulling me in.*

*That is not—* Aldric stopped. Started again. *The ritual framework Thessa taught him was not designed for expulsion. There is no precedent for—*

*He ate a quest mid-combat two days ago. We're past the precedents.*

*You would become an unhosted fragment.* Aldric's voice had gone quieter. The formal register was holding but the control underneath it was not. *In the open. Without a consciousness to anchor to. The dissipation rate for an unhosted fragment in contaminated territory is—*

*I know the rate.*

Aldric went silent.

Maren sat with his back against the stone wall in a sheep pen that smelled like autumn and old quest residue and listened to the wind. The hum was steady from the east. His hands shook. The wrong-frequency vibration in his chest ground against his ribs, and he thought about Vess's voice the first time he'd heard it: furious, professional, livid about the paladin and the incompetent host and the fact of being dead. He thought about the way she'd talked him through the physical recovery after his first real consumption at the creek bed, the unexpected steadiness of it, how she'd narrated his breathing the way she must have narrated approach routes in the life she'd had before. In, hold, out. Professional. Nearly kind. Alive, even dead.

"How long?" he asked. "After. How long before you—"

*I don't know.* The first time she'd admitted not knowing since he'd carried her, and it wasn't really a correction. It was a gap in her information, a hole where the answer should have been, and the specific distress of it was quiet and precise. *Theory says hours. Maybe longer in contaminated air. Fragments persist where the residue is dense. But the theory also says a System-blind host can't carry fragments at all, so.*

So the theory was shit. Maren almost laughed but the headache pressed down harder and the laugh died somewhere in his sinuses.

"There's got to be another way."

*There isn't.* She said it once. No repetition. No emphasis. The words occupied their space and that was all. *Two fragments from opposed classifications are destroying your body. One fragment from a single classification is stable. The choice is straightforward.*

"For you, maybe."

*For everyone. That's the point.*

Maren closed his eyes. The darkness behind his eyelids was the same darkness he'd seen his whole life when the System's interface should have been there, a comfortable nothing, warm and unwritten. Twenty-eight years of that nothing. Twenty-three days of it being crowded with voices, and the thought of one of those voices going quiet made the nothing feel different. Larger. Colder.

"What about Blackhall?" he said.

The silence that followed was different from Aldric's silences. Aldric's silences were retreats. He went somewhere inside himself and closed the door. Vess's silence was presence without speech. She was there, fully, and the words had stopped.

*What about him.*

"Your contract. The massacre. Why you were in that quest in the first place. You were hired to kill him."

*I know what I was hired to do.*

"And you never finished it. Torren Blackhall is still alive. Still walking around."

*I know.*

Maren opened his eyes. The sheep pen. The dead grass. The glow from the east where the reclassified valley bled quest-light into the sky. His hands, shaking.

"You've been carrying that since before you died. You told me his schedule. Tuesdays the lower market. Thursdays the holding pens. Fridays he meets the reeve and drinks too much and walks home through the garden alone, and you know the garden, and you know where the hedgerow blocks the sight lines, and now you're just going to let it go?"

*Yes.*

The word sat in the air between them. Inside him, technically. All of this was inside him, the voices and the argument and the silence. But it occupied space the way a physical thing did.

"Vess."

*I thought it would matter more.* Her voice was the same, flat, professional. But the sentence was longer than anything she'd said about herself before, and that alone was a fracture in the surface. *The contract. Blackhall. Justice, if that's what it was. I thought when the time came it would be the thing I couldn't let go of. The hook that kept me here.*

*And it isn't?*

Aldric. Quiet. Unadorned. None of the formality, none of the classification language. Just a question from someone who needed the answer.

*No.* Vess. Simply. *It matters. He killed those people and used the System's own infrastructure to hide it and he's still alive and it matters. But it doesn't matter more than this.*

She didn't specify what "this" was. She didn't need to. Maren could feel the unclassified entity shuddering against his ribs, the wrong-frequency hum that was getting worse with every hour, and he understood that "this" was him staying alive long enough to do whatever came next. "This" was the work. "This" was what remained when you let the last piece of your old self go.

"When?" he asked.

*Now. Before I find a reason to stay.*

Maren put his palm to the stone wall and started to stand, because standing had the shape of refusal even when refusal didn't lead anywhere. He got halfway up before the thing under his ribs twisted hard enough to fold him over again. Not a stab. Worse. A dragging pull through chest and spine, as if the thing in him wanted east and west at the same time. His knees hit the grass. He caught himself on one hand and breathed through the contamination stink while spit gathered sour in his mouth.

*That's closer,* Vess said. No comfort in it. Just assessment. *Wait another hour and you'll be vomiting bile into the dirt while the entity tries to tear in both directions.*

"We could find Thessa."

*Maybe,* Vess said. *If you can walk. If the System leaves us the time.*

Aldric's silence lasted long enough to become its own answer.

*She is correct,* he said at last, each word set down separately. *The interval between surges is shortening. If this is to be done, it should be done now.*

---

Maren took the bowl out of his pack.

It was ceramic, glazed once and badly, the color somewhere between mud and dried blood, an indeterminate brown that had never been attractive. One side was chipped from years of careless packing and road travel. The chip exposed the pale clay underneath, a half-moon of lighter color near the rim, and a thin crack ran from it toward the base where the Crook had heated the bowl past what clay was meant to do. He'd bought it from a woman at the Fenhold market when he was seventeen and needed a prop for his first fake ritual. Four coppers. The woman had tried to sell him a nicer one for six, and he'd said no, because he was seventeen and two coppers was dinner and the dead didn't care about pottery.

He turned it in his hands. The glaze was worn smooth in the places where his fingers always sat, two spots on either side where he'd held it during every performance for more than a decade. He could feel the depressions his thumbs had made, so shallow they were invisible, worn there by nothing more than repetition and time and the hands of a man who had been lying about everything else but who had always held the bowl the same way.

It had never done anything. Not once. Every ritual where he'd placed it before a body and murmured words he'd invented and watched the family's faces for cues about when to nod, when to be solemn, when to close his eyes as if the dead were listening, the bowl had sat there being ceramic. Four coppers' worth of clay doing the work of nothing.

"I don't know if this'll work," he said.

*It'll work.* Vess.

*You cannot know that.* Aldric.

*He consumed a quest the size of a village two days ago with nothing but bad training and stubbornness. He can manage a single fragment.*

*That is not the same as certainty.*

*Nothing is. Start.*

Maren set the bowl on the ground between his knees. The grass was cold and damp through his trousers. The sheep pen's stone wall circled them, waist-high, roofless, open to the sky where the early autumn stars were coming out in patches between thin clouds. The hum from the east was steady. Quest-light reflected off the cloud cover in a pale wash that Maren could see only as ambient brightening, a sky that refused to go fully dark.

He put his hands on either side of the bowl, thumbs settling into the depressions, and tried to remember what Thessa had taught him.

The proper consumption sequence was: preparation, opening, acceptance, dissolution. He'd managed it twice now, badly at the creek bed, barely at the Crook. Both times the process had felt like pulling his own skin back, an exposure of bare interior that had no business being exposed. Like finding a door in a wall you'd thought was solid and being told to stand in the doorway while contamination came through.

For this he needed the opposite. Not a door opening inward. A door opening out.

He closed his eyes. The bowl was cold under his hands. The grass was cold under his knees. The contamination smell was sharp in the air, that ozone-sweet decay, stronger toward the valley. He could hear his own breathing, and underneath it, the voices: Aldric's formal silence, Vess's professional waiting.

"Vess."

*I'm here.*

"I know." His thumbs pressed deeper into the worn depressions on the bowl, and for a moment the clay felt thin enough to crack. "I just. Yeah. I know."

He began.

The opening was the same whether you were consuming or expelling. Thessa had been clear about that much. The sin-eater's work started with making yourself available. Permeable. Maren had spent twenty-eight years being impermeable to the System and more than a decade performing a pantomime of permeability, and the real thing was still like learning a language his body didn't want to speak. He breathed and tried to mean it. Not perform it. Mean it. The difference between those two things had been the entire distance of his life, and it was narrowing, and that should have been a comfort but mostly it just hurt.

A clenched place in his chest loosened. It was the same loosening he'd felt at the creek bed and at the Crook. The unclassified entity responded, surging toward the opening, confused and eager, the way it always did when he made himself available to the work.

*Careful.* Aldric. Not a command. Closer to worry, though he'd never use that word.

Maren pushed.

Consumption was about pulling: drawing the contamination in, dissolving it, accepting the weight. Expulsion was the opposite, and the opposite turned out to be harder, because he had to push Vess out of himself after she'd gotten into his joints. Vess was the reason he'd chosen the west side of the pen without thinking about it, stone at his back and the gate-gap in front of him. She was the reason his thumb kept checking the knife hilt whenever the wind shifted east. Those weren't thoughts anymore. She'd worn them into him.

He pushed, and she began to move, and it hurt.

Not the dramatic pain of the consumption. No convulsions, no retching. A subtler thing. Like removing a splinter that had been in his hand so long the skin had grown around it. His body had accommodated her the way a tree accommodates a fence post, growing against and around until the foreign object was structural, and now he was pulling it out and the gaps it left were shaped exactly like what was missing.

The bowl warmed under his hands.

That was new. In all his fake rituals, the bowl had never been anything but room temperature or cold. Whatever the air was, the bowl was. Now it heated from the inside, not hot, not painful, but warm the way a thing being used was warm. The glaze under his thumbs changed from dead ceramic to a surface that conducted, that accepted, that did what he'd spent eleven years pretending it could do. Four coppers. A potter who couldn't center a handle. And now, for the first time in its dull, chipped, cracked, unremarkable existence, the bowl was working.

*There's—* Vess started, and for the first time since Maren had known her, she didn't finish. A gap. A fracture in the controlled voice, as if she'd hit one of her own memory gaps mid-sentence and the sentence had fallen into it.

*I can feel it,* she said, and this sentence was complete but it cost her control. *The bowl. It's doing the real thing.*

The fake tool. The market bowl. The prop. A transfer was happening through it, through Maren, out of him, into the ceramic, and the bowl was receiving it with the patience of an object that had waited all this time to be useful, though Maren knew that was his own sentimentality talking and the bowl was just clay and heat and function and didn't care.

He kept his hands on it and pushed, and Vess moved, and the gaps she left filled with quiet.

*Maren.*

"Yeah."

*The contract is released. If you get near Blackhall—* She stopped. *No. That's not yours. It's released.*

He held the bowl and said nothing, because there was nothing to say to a woman letting go of the last thing that had kept her shape, and anything he tried would be wrong.

*The old woman's teaching will hold. Aldric's theory is sound enough, even when it's pompous.* Quieter now. Not softer. Vess didn't do soft. But the volume had dropped, as if the space between them was widening even before the separation was complete. *Your form's bad. The opening isn't. That's why I'm getting out.*

He pushed. The bowl heated. The thing in his chest, the unclassified entity, the collision, the impossible classification, shuddered and simplified. The cross-current under his ribs narrowed to one hard pull beneath the breastbone. Still wrong. Just no longer tearing in two directions.

With her came pieces of use. Not pictures. Not proper memories. Measures. The distance between a doorway and the shadow beside it. How to set a heel so gravel stayed quiet. How long a drunk man took to turn when you said his name from behind. They flashed through Maren's body and went thin at once, draining through his hands into the bowl with the heat. They'd been hers before they were ever his. His muscles still reacted as if someone were stripping a habit out of the meat.

He pushed again. More went with her. The little count he sometimes did without knowing it when strangers entered a room. The habit of giving every open space an exit and every exit a risk. The automatic correction that put his body half a step off-center from any door. Space unhooked itself from those borrowed meanings and became only space again.

*My sister,* Vess said, and the words came separate, as if she had to place them by hand. *I know there was one. I reach for what to call her and it blanks.*

And then she left.

It was exactly like a sound leaving a room. Not a door closing, not a dramatic exit, just the moment when you realized the noise that had been there a second ago wasn't there anymore. Had it stopped just now? Or a moment ago, and you were only noticing? Maren sat with his hands on the warm bowl and waited for the difference to become legible.

It came hard. The gap where the gate had been stayed only a gap; his shoulders didn't turn to keep it centered. When the wind shifted east, his hand didn't go to the knife until a beat later, after the contamination smell thickened enough for his ordinary mind to catch up. The bowl cooled under his hands. He said Aldric's name once, then again. Aldric didn't answer at once. The pause wasn't relief or one of the paladin's retreats. It was the third place in Maren's skull sitting empty, raw-edged from use.

Maren put the bowl back in his pack. The chip near the rim caught his thumb on the way down, familiar as breath. He had to move it twice before it sat clear of the spoon; the automatic correction didn't come until after the first try, and this time it was his. The thumb-depressions were still there, worn smooth by eleven years of lies and three weeks of real work. He pulled his knees up and let the stone wall take his weight while the stars came out over the contaminated valley and the hum from the east kept grinding through the dark.

When Aldric finally spoke, his voice had the clipped shape of a report and none of the steadiness.

*The entity has stabilized.* Formal. Clipped. The shape of an assessment he could keep his hands on. *The opposition is resolved. Single classification within a System-blind host. Stable, as far as I can determine.*

"Good," Maren said. The word came out flat and useless.

*Consumption capability should remain largely intact. Thessa's training addressed the foundational techniques. What you have lost is...* He stopped. *The habit of sitting where you can see an entrance. The impulse to touch the knife before you have even located the threat. Her specific observations about—*

He stopped again. Maren waited. The night bird didn't call.

*She was wrong frequently.* Aldric said it with the cadence of a man reaching for a familiar position and finding that it no longer fit his hand. *About matters of moral consequence. Her classification training prioritized expedience over duty.*

He stopped a third time.

*She was not wrong about this.*

Maren put his chin on his knees. The stone wall pressed cold against his back. His hands had stopped shaking at some point during the ritual, the first time in two days, and the stillness of his own fingers against his shins was strange, like touching the place where an ache had lived and finding it gone. The headache behind his left eye was gone too. The wrong-frequency vibration in his chest had narrowed into a simpler, steadier pull, still the unclassified entity, still the thing the System couldn't name, but no longer scraping itself raw inside him.

He missed her already.

"She didn't say goodbye," he said. "Not exactly. She said the contract was released and she said she couldn't get to her sister's name and then she was just gone."

*She said things once.* Aldric. Quiet. *You knew this about her.*

"Yeah."

*Then you know that was the goodbye.*

Maren stayed in the sheep pen until the cold worked through his shirt. Aldric didn't speak again. In the morning he would get up and keep walking east toward the Woundmouth with the bowl in his pack, one voice in his skull, and the simpler thing in his chest. He would be less than he had been, but stable enough to keep moving.


# Chapter 17

Three days past the wreckage of the Crook, the roads gave out entirely.

Not all at once. The deterioration had been gradual, ruts softening into packed earth, packed earth dissolving into root-cracked mud, until what remained was just a suggestion of direction between rockfall and scrub. The autumn air had an edge to it now. Maren's breath fogged in the mornings, though by midday the sun still warmed the stone. Quest-light didn't pool here the way it had near Silt or Harrow. It seeped. Thin runnels of pale luminescence bled through cracks in the rock and trickled downhill like water after rain, except it moved too slowly and sometimes in the wrong direction, uphill, sideways along a cliff face, following paths that had nothing to do with gravity.

The hum was constant now. Not the background drone he'd grown up filtering. This was pressure behind his teeth, a vibration in the bones of his face that made his sinuses ache. It had started as a nuisance two days ago and thickened until he couldn't ignore it, until he was walking through it the way a person walks through heavy wind.

*Left at the split rock. Then follow the bleeding.*

Aldric's guidance had simplified. In the early days of the journey, his directions came formatted like military dispatches: compass bearing, estimated distance, landmark descriptions that assumed Maren cared about northeast versus north-northeast. Now the paladin pointed and named. Left. Down. That gap. The change was partly economy, partly something else. Aldric was speaking less, and when he spoke, his voice carried a quality Maren couldn't name. Not quite fear. Closer to the sound a man makes when he's standing on a high ledge and being very careful about where he looks.

"You alright in there?" Maren said, out loud. There was no one to hear him. The last person he'd seen was three days gone, a contamination surveyor heading the other direction, walking fast, who hadn't even nodded.

*I am observing.*

"That's not what I asked."

Silence for a dozen steps. The rock path narrowed between two shoulders of dark stone, and Maren's pack scraped the wall on his left. Quest-light ran in a slow stripe at ankle height, warm against his boot when he grazed it.

*The interface here is — I can see the structure more clearly. The architecture. It's becoming more legible the closer we get, but the legibility is not—* A pause. Aldric did not pause. Or he hadn't, for the whole length of their shared existence, through running commentary and moral objection and the kind of constant narration that made Maren want to scream on good days. When the paladin stopped mid-sentence, something had changed the shape of his thinking. *The edges are physical here. I do not mean merely visible. I mean physical. The containment has thickness.*

"So when you say physical, you mean what?"

*The way a load-bearing wall is physical. You cannot see what it holds unless it fails, but the wall itself has mass, joins, stress points. And this place—* Another pause. *The wall is thin here. There are places where the containment material has been stressed beyond its design tolerance and the joining compound has degraded. Fractures is close, but not exact.*

"You're describing a building."

*I am describing what I see. The metaphor is imprecise but not inappropriate. The System is constructed. It was built. Whatever built it intended it to hold.*

Harrow had looked like infrastructure. Maren remembered that now: the quest boards and gear shops and scorch marks layered on every road, the whole town built around a System that organized everything. He'd thought of it as bureaucracy at the time, the filing system of heroism, but Aldric's description from weeks ago had changed the shape of it. It was a wall, and all the infrastructure, the classes, the levels, the quests, the bounties, was its public face, the side people could see and navigate by, while the actual structure went deep and held something on the other side.

He stepped over a narrow channel where quest-light ran like a creek, pale and faintly warm against his shin through his trouser leg. He could see it. He'd always been able to see quest-light, the raw glow without the data overlay everyone else perceived. But here the glow was different. Sick-looking. The light stuttered and pulsed with no discernible rhythm, like a heartbeat that couldn't find its pace.

The skull was quiet around Aldric's voice. Maren noticed the silence the way he noticed a missing tooth, the tongue kept going to the gap. Vess would have had something to say about this terrain. An assessment. *Defensible here. Kill zone there. If anyone's waiting, you'd want elevation.* The flat, practical narration of a woman who saw the world as a series of tactical problems, and who, underneath the tactical language, noticed the color of the light and the smell of the rock and the exact weight of the autumn air but would never have said so. He missed her. Not the way he missed Derry, which was the specific ache of losing a person who knew him from before. This was the miss of losing a perspective. The world was less precisely observed with only two of them in here.
